  • Benefits much lower (14M vs. 44M in example)

Robustness: Some fraudsters irrational, but marginal fraudsters (those near indifference) will respond. Deterrence effect still significant even if not complete.

Key Assumption 3: Fraudsters observe system deployment

If fraudsters don't know about system:

  • No ex-ante deterrence
  • Only direct prevention
  • Benefits lower initially
  • Deterrence grows as reputation spreads

Over time:

  • Word spreads through criminal networks
  • Deterrence effect increases
  • Investment becomes more valuable

Key Assumption 4: Fraudsters can't adapt

If fraudsters circumvent system:

  • Example: System flags transactions > 50,000, so split into smaller amounts
  • Cat-and-mouse game
  • Detection rate falls over time
  • Need continuous investment to maintain effectiveness

Robustness: Systems must evolve. One-time investment insufficient. Cost should include ongoing updates.

Key Assumption 5: No displacement to other banks

If fraudsters shift to other banks without system:

  • Total fraud unchanged (just moved)
  • But: Bank investing benefits (less fraud on its customers)
  • Externality: Other banks harmed (more fraud on their customers)

Implication: Industry-wide coordination may be needed for social efficiency.

Key Assumption 6: Detection errors are manageable

If high false positive rate:

  • Legitimate transactions blocked
  • Customer frustration and switching
  • Costs of system include:
    • Direct: Lost customer revenue
    • Indirect: Reputation damage
  • May make system unprofitable despite fraud reduction

Conditional Conclusion:

Investment in fraud detection system is profitable when:

1. Benefits exceed costs:

Total Benefit = Direct Prevention + Indirect Deterrence

Direct prevention: = (Additional fraud attempts stopped) × (Average fraud amount) = Δp × N × A

Indirect deterrence: = (Fraud attempts deterred) × (Average fraud amount) = N_deterred × A

Profitable if: (Δp × N × A) + (N_deterred × A) > C_system

2. Key determinants:

Higher benefits when:

  • High current fraud volume (N): More attempts to prevent/deter
  • Large detection rate improvement (Δp): 20% → 70% better than 20% → 30%
  • Large fraud amounts (A): 100,000 per attempt vs. 10,000
  • Strong deterrence effect (N_deterred): Shifts many fraudsters from profitable to unprofitable
  • Bank bears fraud losses: Full benefit captured by bank
  • Low sanctions (S): Makes Δp more important (need better detection since punishment weak)

Lower benefits when:

  • Low fraud volume: Few attempts to prevent
  • Marginal detection improvement: Δp small
  • Customers bear losses: Bank doesn't save on fraud costs
  • High sanctions already: Fraud already deterred, little room for improvement
  • Fraudsters can adapt: Circumvent system over time

3. System design matters:

Real-time blocking (System B) most effective:

  • Prevents money from leaving (reduces B to zero)
  • High detection rate (increases p)
  • Strong deterrence from both mechanisms
  • Most expensive but highest ROI if fraud volume high

Post-fraud detection (System A) cost-effective:

  • Lower cost
  • Still significant deterrence through increased p
  • Good if budget constrained

Enhanced prosecution (System C) limited:

  • Only increases S (sanctions)
  • Deterrence effect depends on S sensitivity
  • Better paired with detection improvements

4. Common analytical errors to avoid:

Error 1: Only counting direct fraud stopped

  • Correct: Include deterred attempts (often larger effect)

Error 2: Assuming fraud volume constant

  • Correct: Fraud volume endogenous to detection rate

Error 3: Comparing stopped fraud to system cost

  • Correct: Compare total benefit (stopped + deterred) to cost

Error 4: Ignoring liability allocation

  • Correct: Bank only benefits to extent it bears losses

5. Dynamic considerations:

Short-term: Direct prevention dominates (fraudsters haven't learned yet) Long-term: Deterrence dominates (reputation for strong detection spreads)

Implication: Investment may have negative NPV in Year 1 but positive over multi-year horizon as deterrence builds.

6. Game-theoretic sophistication:

Strategic fraudsters adapt:

  • System blocks large transfers → Fraudsters split into small transfers
  • System uses pattern recognition → Fraudsters vary patterns
  • Requires adaptive system (machine learning)
  • Cost includes ongoing development, not just initial deployment

Arms race: Investment worthwhile only if bank can stay ahead of fraudster adaptation.

The economic logic: Fraud detection systems create value through two channels - directly stopping fraud attempts and indirectly deterring future attempts by changing fraudster incentives. The deterrence effect, analyzed through the Legal Incentives Model (B > pS framework), often exceeds the direct effect because preventing one fraud attempt through deterrence saves 100% of the loss, while stopping an attempt in progress may still incur investigation costs. A correct cost-benefit analysis must recognize that the number of fraud attempts is not fixed but responds to detection probability - higher detection makes fraud unprofitable for marginal fraudsters who then exit. Investment is profitable when the combined direct and indirect benefits (weighted by the bank's liability share) exceed system costs, with deterrence benefits potentially far exceeding direct prevention benefits when detection rate improvements are substantial.

▶ Question 11.6: Designing an AML Compliance Program

Large financial institutions can face substantial penalties for failures in their anti–money laundering (AML) systems. Following large-scale money laundering through Danske Bank's Estonian operations, very large fines were imposed on the bank. The bank must therefore decide how to organize its internal compliance systems to prevent future violations by employees and subsidiaries.

(a) Ensuring Employee Compliance

Suppose a bank faces a large monetary sanction if employees fail to comply with required anti–money laundering procedures, such as customer due diligence and reporting obligations, even in the absence of direct involvement by senior management.

Analyze how the bank should ensure that its employees comply with required anti–money laundering procedures under this liability regime. In your answer, discuss which factors determine how the firm should design its compliance arrangements. Use economic theory.

▶ What a Good Answer Should Contain

A good answer should explain how firm-level liability for employee AML failures makes the bank internalize the expected external sanction and therefore invest in internal enforcement. The analysis should center on the employee's incentive to comply, comparing the private gain from cutting corners \(B\) to the expected internal downside \(pS\). A strong answer highlights that the bank can act on all three terms: raise \(p\) through monitoring and verifiable documentation, raise \(S\) through credible internal discipline, and also reduce \(B\) by changing compensation, sales targets, and promotion criteria so that compliance is rewarded and noncompliance is not implicitly subsidized. The key determinants include the magnitude and likelihood of the external penalty, the observability/verifiability of AML steps, monitoring and enforcement costs at scale, and the extent to which the bank's own high-powered sales incentives inflate \(B\) and thereby force costly increases in \(p\) and \(S\).

▶ Example solution

Under a regime where the bank is sanctioned when employees violate AML duties (even without senior involvement), the bank effectively bears the expected external sanction from employee noncompliance. The bank therefore has a direct incentive to reduce the probability that violations occur and to increase the probability that violations are detected early.

Deterrence at the employee level Each employee compares the private benefit of cutting corners \(B\) (saved time, higher sales commissions, avoiding "difficult" customers, meeting aggressive targets) to the expected internal downside. To make compliance privately optimal, the bank can work on both sides of the comparison: it can reduce \(B\) by changing bonuses, targets, and promotion criteria so that revenue is not earned (or is not kept) when AML steps are skipped, and it can increase the expected internal downside \(pS\). The key deterrence object on the sanction side is \(pS\), where \(p\) is the probability the bank detects a violation and \(S\) is the internal consequence (loss/clawback of bonus, demotion, termination, etc.). If the bank cannot credibly impose a large \(S\) (because employees are "judgment-proof" or firing is costly), it must rely more on raising \(p\) through monitoring and verifiable documentation; if monitoring is costly, it becomes even more valuable to compress \(B\) by weakening "high-powered" sales rewards that make shortcuts attractive.

(Illustration) If an employee gains \(B=1\) from skipping checks and the bank's internal detection probability is \(p=0.1\), deterrence via sanctions alone requires \(S>10\). If such a penalty is not credible or would be too costly, the bank can (i) invest to raise \(p\) so a smaller \(S\) suffices, and/or (ii) redesign pay so the shortcut no longer yields \(B=1\) (for example, by conditioning bonuses on verified completion of AML steps or by clawing back commissions when files later fail compliance review), which reduces how much \(pS\) is needed in the first place.

Designing the compliance arrangement: choosing \(B\), \(p\), and \(S\) Because monitoring and enforcement are costly (transaction costs), the bank should increase compliance inputs up to the point where the marginal cost of additional controls equals the marginal benefit from lowering the bank's expected external sanction (and other losses such as reputational damage). The bank has three levers:

  • Adjust \(B\) (benefits from noncompliance): reduce the payoff to shortcuts by tying bonuses and targets to compliance-verified activity (not just volume), delaying payout until checks clear, using clawbacks for later-discovered breaches, and avoiding sales metrics that implicitly reward ignoring red flags.
  • Raise \(p\) efficiently: automated flags, independent compliance review, random audits, dual control/separation of duties, and mandatory recordkeeping that makes compliance steps verifiable after the fact.
  • Choose a credible \(S\): clear, escalating discipline for documented breaches, loss/clawback of variable pay, and promotion criteria that reward compliant performance rather than only short-term volume.

Key determinants of the optimal mix

  1. Magnitude and likelihood of the external sanction: Larger or more likely external sanctions justify stronger internal arrangements—either more investment in detection \(p\), tougher credible consequences \(S\), and/or stronger reduction of \(B\) by weakening incentives for risky growth.
  2. Observability and verifiability of AML tasks: If tasks can be documented and checked cheaply, the bank can (a) raise \(p\) at low cost and (b) make pay conditional on verifiable compliance, directly shrinking \(B\). If violations are hard to verify, both \(p\) and pay-contingencies become harder, pushing the bank toward process design that creates verifiable steps.
  3. Monitoring costs and organizational scale: In large, dispersed institutions, monitoring is costly; this increases the value of standardization and central oversight, but also increases the value of reducing \(B\) so that the bank does not have to "buy" deterrence mainly through expensive increases in \(p\).
  4. Strength of sales incentives and target pressure (the size of \(B\)): High-powered bonuses and aggressive targets can inflate \(B\) and make noncompliance privately attractive. Rebalancing compensation toward compliance-verified measures can lower \(B\) and reduce the needed intensity of \(p\) and \(S\).
  5. Credibility and side effects of internal sanctions: Very harsh sanctions may be costly (litigation, morale, concealment). Where \(S\) is limited, deterrence must come more from raising \(p\) and, importantly, from lowering \(B\) by removing the internal rewards from cutting corners.

Bottom line

With firm-level liability for employee AML failures, the bank should treat compliance as an internal enforcement problem using all three levers: reduce employees' private gains from cutting corners \(B\) through compensation and targets, raise the probability of detection \(p\) through verifiable procedures and monitoring, and impose credible internal consequences \(S\) when violations are detected. The optimal design depends on the expected external sanction, the cost of increasing \(p\), the feasibility of imposing \(S\), and how the bank's own incentive scheme affects \(B\) and thus the cost of achieving deterrence.

▶ Question 11.7: Why Impose Firm Liability for Employee Misconduct?

When employees or subsidiaries of large financial institutions fail to comply with anti–money laundering (AML) obligations, policymakers often impose penalties on the firm itself. For example, following large-scale money laundering through its Estonian operations, Danske Bank faced very large fines even though neither senior management nor the firm's owners were directly involved in the misconduct.

(b) Firm liability

Why might policymakers impose large fines on the firm for failures to comply with AML obligations by individual employees or subsidiaries, even when neither firm owners nor top management were directly involved? Why not rely solely on sanctions against the individual employees who engaged in the misconduct? Discuss using economic theory.

▶ What a Good Answer Should Contain

A good answer should apply cost–benefit analysis and the legal incentives model. It should explain how firm-level liability changes the firm's incentives to invest in AML controls by making the firm internalize the expected cost of employee misconduct.

The analysis should discuss why it may be cheaper for the state to rely on firm liability rather than directly monitoring individual employees. A strong answer explains this both in terms of information advantages (the bank observes employee behavior and internal processes more cheaply than the state) and instrument choice (the bank has more enforcement levers). In particular, the bank can not only increase detection \(p\) and internal sanctions \(S\), but also reduce employees' private gain from noncompliance \(B\) through the design of compensation, targets, and promotion criteria.

Good answers should recognize the judgment-proof constraint on individual employees: if feasible individual sanctions are limited, achieving deterrence through individual liability alone would require a high—and costly—probability of detection. Because firms can pay large fines, holding the firm liable allows deterrence to be achieved with a lower public detection probability, reducing enforcement costs for the state.

Finally, a complete answer should make clear that both public monitoring costs and the firm's internal compliance costs must be included in the cost–benefit analysis, and that the optimal liability rule balances these costs against the reduction in expected harm from AML violations.

▶ Example solution

To answer this question we use cost–benefit analysis and the legal incentives model.

Key elements in the CBA: The policy problem is to choose the liability regime that minimizes total expected social costs. These include:

  • Expected harm: the social cost of residual money laundering that is not prevented.
  • Public enforcement costs: monitoring, investigations, and prosecutions by the state.
  • Private compliance costs: the bank's spending on AML precautions (systems, monitoring, audits, training).

Fines themselves are mainly transfers between the firm and the state and are therefore not real resource costs; they matter only through how they change incentives.

Individual vs. firm liability: If liability were imposed only on individual employees, the bank could save on compliance spending. However, individual employees are typically judgment-proof: there is a cap on how large a fine they can pay. As a result, deterrence would require a high probability of detection \(p\), which is costly for the state to achieve through monitoring and investigations.

By contrast, firms can pay large fines. This allows deterrence to be achieved with a lower public detection probability, reducing public enforcement costs.

Short numerical example: Suppose an employee gains \(B=10\) from ignoring AML rules. The maximum feasible individual fine is \(S=20\). To deter misconduct through individual liability alone, the state would need a detection probability of \(p=0.5\), so that the expected sanction \(pS\) is at least 10. Now suppose the firm can ensure employee compliance by investing in internal AML controls at a cost of 5 (monitoring, training, and audit trails). If a firm-level fine of \(S=200\) applies with a public detection probability of only \(p=0.05\), the expected sanction is \(pS=10\), which exceeds the cost of compliance. The firm therefore invests in compliance, employees comply, and expected laundering harm is reduced—while the state only needs a low detection probability.

Why firm liability is cost-effective: It is often cheaper for the bank to ensure employee compliance than for the state to monitor employees directly. The bank has an information advantage—it observes internal processes and behavior more cheaply—and it has more instruments. In addition to monitoring and discipline, it can reduce employees' private gain \(B\) from noncompliance by designing compensation, targets, and promotion criteria that do not reward cutting corners. These internal levers can lower expected harm at lower total cost than relying on public enforcement alone.

Role of public monitoring: Public monitoring is still necessary. For the firm to have incentives to invest in compliance, there must be a positive probability that violations are detected externally. However, because firms can pay large fines, this probability can be relatively low compared to an individual-liability regime.

Why include subsidiaries: If liability were limited to subsidiaries, firms could concentrate risky activities in thinly capitalized units that cannot pay large fines, undermining deterrence. Consolidated firm liability prevents organizational structure from diluting expected sanctions.

Bottom line: Firm liability is justified when it lowers total expected social costs by inducing cost-effective private precautions and allowing deterrence with a lower public detection probability. Individual-only liability often fails because sanctions are capped, making effective deterrence rely on expensive monitoring and still risking under-deterrence.

Tort Law

Foundations used:

  • Legal Incentives Model
  • Cost–Benefit Analysis

Learning goal: apply the Legal Incentives Model to precaution and liability decisions.

Types of Questions You Should Be Able to Answer

  • How should a firm decide how much to invest in safety and precaution?
  • When is strict liability preferable to negligence?
  • How should vague legal standards like "reasonable care" be interpreted?

Consider an auditing firm deciding how thoroughly to review a client's accounts. Each additional check is costly, but reduces the risk that fraud or error goes undetected. If a problem later emerges, the auditor may be held liable for negligence. How much auditing is enough?

This type of decision is common in economic life. Firms and professionals constantly choose how much to invest in safety, verification, and monitoring, knowing that accidents and mistakes are costly but prevention is also expensive. These choices are made ex ante and are shaped by expectations about legal responsibility and liability.

Tort law plays a central role in structuring these incentives. From a private perspective, it affects how firms design production processes, how much they invest in precaution, and how detailed audits, inspections, and other safety measures should be. From a public perspective, it raises questions about deterrence, compensation, and how responsibility for harm should be allocated.

A central feature of tort law is its reliance on open-ended standards such as "reasonable care" and "due care." Law and economics provides a way to interpret these standards systematically, by viewing negligence as a failure to take cost-justified precautions. This perspective is useful both for understanding private decision-making and for analyzing how negligence is argued and assessed in court.

Tort Law as an Application of the Legal Incentives Model

This chapter applies the Legal Incentives Model to precaution decisions. The central insight remains the same: actors choose behavior by comparing private costs against expected legal consequences.

In the tort context, the agent chooses how much care to take, trading off the private cost of precaution against the expected legal cost of accidents, which equals accident probability times damages.

The baseline Legal Incentives Model (Chapter 6) used a binary choice framework: engage in a harmful action or refrain. Tort law extends this logic to precaution, where the same trade-off applies but can be analyzed first in binary form (careful vs. careless) and then refined to continuous care choices.

Tort Law as a System of Civil Liability

Tort law is a system of civil liability designed to address harmful behavior. Its most visible function is compensation: when an individual suffers harm, the injurer may be required to pay damages. From an economic perspective, however, the central role of tort law is not compensation itself, but the way liability rules affect behavior before harm occurs.

Tort law operates through private enforcement. Victims initiate lawsuits, courts determine liability, and damages are paid to the injured party. Unlike criminal law, sanctions are compensatory rather than punitive, and enforcement does not rely on public prosecution.

Liability rules determine when damages are paid and thereby shape incentives ex ante. Understanding these incentives requires analyzing how potential injurers respond to the prospect of legal liability.

Binary Precaution: Careful vs. Careless (Bridge from Foundations)

We begin with the simplest version of the precaution problem: an actor faces a binary choice between being careful and being careless.

The decision problem

An individual or firm must choose between two actions:

  • Take precaution (be "careful")
  • Do not take precaution (be "careless")

Taking precaution is costly but reduces the probability of an accident.

Let:

  • \(w\) = cost of taking precaution (cost of being careful)
  • \(p\) = probability of an accident if careless
  • \(q\) = probability of an accident if careful (where \(q < p\))
  • \(A\) = harm caused by an accident

For simplicity, assume \(q = 0\): being careful eliminates accident risk entirely. This makes the comparison clearest.

Expected costs under strict liability

Under strict liability, the actor pays damages \(A\) whenever an accident occurs.

Expected cost if careless: \(p \cdot A\)

Expected cost if careful: \(w + 0 = w\)

The actor takes precaution if and only if:

\[ w < p \cdot A \]

That is, take care when the cost of precaution is less than the expected accident loss.

Connection to the baseline model

This has exactly the same logic as the baseline Legal Incentives Model:

  • Private cost of action = \(w\) (cost of being careful)
  • Expected legal consequence = \(p \cdot A\) (probability × damages)
  • Decision: act if private cost < expected legal consequence

The only difference is interpretation: here the "action" is taking precaution (avoiding harm) rather than engaging in harmful behavior. The incentive logic is identical.

Example: safety inspection

A manufacturing firm can conduct a thorough safety inspection at cost \(w = 5000\). Without the inspection, there is a \(p = 0.10\) probability of a workplace accident causing harm \(A = 80000\).

Under strict liability:

  • Expected cost without inspection: \(0.10 \times 80000 = 8000\)
  • Cost with inspection: \(5000\)

The firm inspects, since \(5000 < 8000\).

If the inspection cost were \(w = 10000\), the firm would not inspect, since \(10000 > 8000\).

Continuous Precaution: Choosing the Level of Care

In many settings, precaution is not binary but continuous. Firms choose how much to invest in safety, professionals choose how thoroughly to review work, and drivers choose how cautiously to operate vehicles.

We now extend the binary model to allow continuous choice of care level.

The continuous precaution problem

An actor chooses a level of precaution \(x \geq 0\).

Higher precaution reduces accident risk but is costly:

  • \(c(x)\) = private cost of precaution level \(x\)
    • Assume \(c'(x) > 0\): higher care is more costly
    • Often linear: \(c(x) = w x\) where \(w\) is cost per unit of care
  • \(p(x)\) = probability of an accident given care level \(x\)
    • Assume \(p'(x) < 0\): more care reduces accident risk
    • Assume \(p''(x) > 0\): diminishing returns to care
  • \(A\) = harm caused by an accident (assumed constant)
Expected costs under strict liability

Under strict liability, the actor bears the full expected cost of accidents.

The actor chooses \(x\) to minimize total expected cost:

\[ \min_{x \geq 0} \quad c(x) + p(x) \cdot A \]

The optimal care level \(x^*\) satisfies the first-order condition:

\[ c'(x^*) = -p'(x^*) \cdot A \]

In words: choose care such that the marginal cost of additional precaution equals the marginal reduction in expected harm.

Intuition: marginal cost vs. marginal benefit

At the optimal care level:

  • Marginal cost of one more unit of care = \(c'(x^*)\)
  • Marginal reduction in expected harm = \(-p'(x^*) \cdot A\)

If care is below optimal (\(x < x^*\)), marginal benefit exceeds marginal cost: increasing care reduces expected total cost.

If care exceeds optimal (\(x > x^*\)), marginal cost exceeds marginal benefit: reducing care lowers expected total cost.

Social optimum

The socially efficient level of care minimizes the sum of precaution costs and expected harm:

\[ \min_{x \geq 0} \quad c(x) + p(x) \cdot A \]

This is exactly the same problem the private actor solves under strict liability. Therefore, strict liability induces socially efficient precaution.

Example: audit intensity

An auditing firm chooses how many hours \(x\) to spend reviewing a client's financial statements.

Cost of auditing: \(c(x) = 500x\) (500 per hour)

Probability of undetected fraud: \(p(x) = 0.20 e^{-0.1x}\)

Harm if fraud is undetected: \(A = 200000\)

Under strict liability, the firm minimizes: \[ 500x + 0.20 e^{-0.1x} \cdot 200000 \] \[ = 500x + 40000 e^{-0.1x} \]

First-order condition: \[ 500 = 4000 e^{-0.1x^*} \] \[ e^{-0.1x^*} = 0.125 \] \[ x^* \approx 20.8 \text{ hours} \]

The firm chooses approximately 21 hours of audit work.

Strict Liability vs. Negligence

The key difference between liability rules lies in when damages are paid.

Strict liability

Under strict liability:

  • Injurer pays damages \(A\) whenever an accident occurs
  • Expected legal cost = \(p(x) \cdot A\)
  • Injurer minimizes: \(c(x) + p(x) \cdot A\)

Result: Injurer chooses socially optimal care level.

Negligence

Under negligence:

  • Courts define a standard of care \(x^*\) (the "due care" level)
  • Injurer is liable only if care falls below this standard
  • If \(x \geq x^*\): no liability (even if accident occurs)
  • If \(x < x^*\): full liability for harm

Damages as a function of care:

\[ S(x) = \begin{cases} A & \text{if } x < x^* \\ 0 & \text{if } x \geq x^* \end{cases} \]

Expected legal cost under negligence:

\[ \text{Expected legal cost} = \begin{cases} p(x) \cdot A & \text{if } x < x^* \\ 0 & \text{if } x \geq x^* \end{cases} \]

Injurer incentives under negligence

The injurer chooses \(x\) to minimize total expected cost:

\[ c(x) + \begin{cases} p(x) \cdot A & \text{if } x < x^* \\ 0 & \text{if } x \geq x^* \end{cases} \]

If the standard \(x^*\) is set at the socially efficient level, the injurer has a strong incentive to meet it:

  • Below the standard: faces full liability
  • At or above the standard: avoids liability entirely

Result: Injurer chooses \(x = x^*\) (meets the standard, does not exceed it).

Key difference: strict liability vs. negligence

Strict liability:

  • Continuous incentive to reduce risk
  • Injurer balances marginal cost against marginal risk reduction
  • Care level responds smoothly to changes in costs or risks

Negligence:

  • Discrete incentive to meet the standard
  • Strong incentive to reach \(x^*\), no incentive to exceed it
  • Care level jumps discontinuously at the standard

Reasonable Care and the Hand Formula

Reasonable Care and the Hand Formula

Negligence relies on legal standards such as "reasonable care" or "due care." From an economic perspective, these standards can be interpreted using cost–benefit logic.

Although "reasonable care" is an open-ended standard, courts do not verify care or effort directly. Instead, negligence determinations rely on verifiable proxies—such as observable precautions taken, compliance with industry standards, documented procedures, expert testimony, and the occurrence of harm—which allow courts to approximate cost-justified care while operating under evidentiary and institutional constraints (see Chapter 10 on observability and verifiability).

An influential formulation in U.S. tort law is the Hand formula, named after Judge Learned Hand, which states that an actor is negligent if the cost of precaution is less than the expected harm it would prevent.

Formally, care level \(x\) is negligent if:

\[ c(x') - c(x) < [p(x) - p(x')] \cdot A \]

for some higher care level \(x' > x\).

In other words, failing to increase care is negligent when the marginal cost of additional care is less than the marginal reduction in expected harm.

This maps directly to the efficiency condition: reasonable care corresponds to socially efficient care.

While courts rarely apply formal cost-benefit calculations, the Hand formula clarifies the economic logic underlying negligence standards.

Victim Precaution and Comparative Negligence

So far, we have focused on injurer precaution. In many settings, however, both the potential injurer and the potential victim can take precaution.

Victim incentives under strict liability

Under strict liability, victims are fully compensated for harm. As a result, they have no incentive to take precaution themselves.

This is inefficient when victim precaution is valuable.

Victim incentives under negligence

Under negligence, victims bear losses when the injurer meets the standard of care. This gives victims an incentive to take appropriate precaution.

Result: Negligence can provide efficient incentives for both injurers and victims, while strict liability efficiently motivates only injurers.

Comparative negligence

Under comparative negligence, damages are allocated based on the relative fault of the parties. This can provide continuous incentives for both parties to take precaution, though implementation requires courts to assess care levels for both injurer and victim.

Comparing Liability Rules

Different liability rules create different incentive structures.

Strict liability:

  • Places full accident costs on injurers
  • Provides continuous incentives to reduce risk
  • Works well when injurers are the primary risk reducers
  • Does not motivate victim precaution

Negligence:

  • Provides discrete incentives to meet a behavioral standard
  • Can motivate both injurer and victim precaution
  • Requires courts to assess care levels
  • Works well when both parties can reduce risk

The choice between liability rules involves tradeoffs related to information, enforcement costs, and the nature of the activity.

Tort Law in Perspective

Tort law uses the prospect of liability to deter harmful behavior and encourage precaution. Its effectiveness depends on the ability of courts to observe harm and, in negligence cases, to evaluate care.

When private enforcement works well and damages reflect harm, tort law can provide strong and finely tuned incentives. When these conditions fail—such as when harm is difficult to prove or injurers are judgment-proof—other legal responses may be required.

The analysis in this chapter demonstrates how the Legal Incentives Model extends from binary to continuous actions while preserving its core logic: private actors respond to legal rules by comparing the private cost of their choices against the expected legal consequences.

Exam Questions

▶ Question 12.1: BankID Fraud: Liability Allocation Between Banks and Customers

Case Background:

The number of BankID fraud cases has increased dramatically in recent years. Fraudsters often impersonate bank employees or police and convince victims to share passwords or approve bank logins. Money is transferred from victims' accounts and moved onward in ways that make it difficult to trace or confiscate.

In practice, it has been unclear who should bear the losses from such fraud. Banks argue that customers themselves must take responsibility if someone misuses their BankID. They point to the fact that the system is built on personal responsibility, and that increased bank liability will make digital services more expensive and less user-friendly. Consumer organizations argue that banks should bear primary responsibility, since they have better capabilities to detect and stop fraud, and because ordinary customers cannot be expected to protect themselves against increasingly sophisticated methods.

Question:

What factors determine who should bear the costs of BankID fraud? Discuss using economic theory.

▶ What a Good Answer Should Contain
  1. Use cost-benefit analysis framework to evaluate liability allocation
  2. Apply least-cost avoider principle
  3. Analyze how liability affects customer incentives to protect BankID
  4. Analyze how liability affects bank incentives to invest in fraud detection systems
  5. Use numerical examples to compare total costs under different liability rules
  6. Discuss moral hazard problems when liability is misallocated
  7. Consider role of risk preferences and ability to bear risk
  8. State assumptions and how conclusions change if they fail
▶ Example solution

Framework: Cost-Benefit Analysis and Least-Cost Avoider

The question asks who should bear costs of BankID fraud. The economic answer is that liability should be allocated to the party who can prevent fraud at lowest total cost, considering both prevention costs and remaining fraud costs.

Step 1: General Principle - Least-Cost Avoider

According to cost-benefit analysis, liability should be allocated to minimize total social costs:

  • Costs of fraud prevention (customer caution + bank systems)
  • Costs of fraud that still occurs
  • Administrative costs

The party who can most cost-effectively reduce fraud should bear liability, creating incentives to take optimal precautions.

Step 2: Numerical Example - Comparing Liability Rules

Assume fraud probability is 1% if neither party takes precautions, with losses of 100,000 per fraud incident.

Prevention options:

  • Customer can reduce probability to 0.2% through caution, at cost 100
  • Bank can reduce probability to 0.3% through detection systems, at cost 80
  • If both take precautions, probability becomes 0.1%

Scenario A: Bank bears full liability

Bank's decision:

  • Investment benefit: 100,000 × (0.01 - 0.003) = 700
  • Investment cost: 80
  • Bank invests (700 > 80) ✓

Customer's decision:

  • Customer bears no fraud cost
  • Caution cost: 100
  • Customer doesn't exercise caution ✗

Result: 0.3% fraud probability

Assume social cost of fraud is 200,000 per incident (includes deadweight loss beyond transfer): Total social cost: 200,000 × 0.003 + 80 = 680

Scenario B: Customer bears full liability

Customer's decision:

  • Caution benefit: 100,000 × (0.01 - 0.002) = 800
  • Caution cost: 100
  • Customer exercises caution (800 > 100) ✓

Bank's decision:

  • Bank bears no fraud cost
  • System cost: 80
  • Bank doesn't invest ✗

Result: 0.2% fraud probability Total social cost: 200,000 × 0.002 + 100 = 500

In this example, customer liability is more efficient (500 < 680).

Step 3: Factors Determining Optimal Liability Allocation

Factor 1: Relative cost of prevention

  • Low cost for customer caution → favors customer liability
  • Low cost for bank systems → favors bank liability

Factor 2: Relative effectiveness of prevention

  • High effectiveness of customer caution → favors customer liability
  • High effectiveness of bank detection → favors bank liability

Factor 3: Bilateral precaution If both parties' actions matter, might need:

  • Shared liability (each bears portion)
  • Negligence rule (customer liable if careless, bank otherwise)

Step 4: Analysis of Shared Liability

If liability is split 50-50:

Customer's caution decision:

  • Benefit: 100,000 × 0.5 × (0.01 - 0.002) = 400
  • Cost: 100
  • Customer exercises caution (400 > 100) ✓

Bank's investment decision:

  • Benefit: 100,000 × 0.5 × (0.01 - 0.003) = 350
  • Cost: 80
  • Bank invests (350 > 80) ✓

Result: 0.1% fraud probability (both take precautions) Total social cost: 200,000 × 0.001 + 100 + 80 = 380

Shared liability achieves lowest total cost!

However, incentives are weaker than optimal:

  • Customer should exercise caution if benefit > 100, which requires reduction of at least 0.001 (= 100/100,000)
  • Under shared liability, customer only gets 50% of benefit, so requires reduction of 0.002
  • Similarly for bank

Step 5: Negligence Rules

Alternative: Customer bears loss if "negligent" (failed to exercise reasonable care), bank otherwise.

Define "reasonable care" as the precautions that minimize total costs.

Result:

  • Customer exercises caution (to avoid bearing full loss)
  • Bank invests (bears loss if customer was careful)
  • Achieves 0.1% fraud probability with full incentives

This can achieve the efficient outcome without split liability.

Step 6: Additional Factors

Risk preferences:

  • Customers are more risk-averse than banks
  • Small probability of large loss feels more severe to individual than to diversified bank
  • Argues for bank bearing more liability (insurance function)

Market mechanisms:

  • Even if bank covers losses, costs passed to customers through fees/prices
  • Depends on supply and demand elasticities
  • Doesn't change efficiency analysis, but affects distribution

Information asymmetries:

  • Banks have better information about fraud patterns
  • Banks can aggregate data across customers
  • Favors bank responsibility for system-level detection

Heterogeneous customers:

  • Tech-savvy customers: low cost of caution → customer liability efficient
  • Vulnerable customers (elderly, cognitively impaired): high cost of caution → bank liability efficient
  • Uniform rule may be inefficient across customer types

Step 7: Numerical Example with Heterogeneity

Customer type A (tech-savvy):

  • Caution cost: 50
  • Caution reduces probability from 1% to 0.1%
  • Benefit of customer caution: 100,000 × 0.009 = 900 >> 50
  • Efficient: Customer exercises caution

Customer type B (vulnerable):

  • Caution cost: 1,500 (difficulty understanding scams)
  • Caution reduces probability from 1% to 1.2%
  • Benefit: 100,000 × (-0.002) < 0 (caution doesn't help!)
  • Bank systems reduce to 0.5% at cost 200
  • Efficient: Bank bears liability for type B

Step 8: Market Failure Arguments

If customers can't observe which banks have good fraud prevention:

  • No willingness to pay for better protection
  • Banks have weak incentives to invest in security
  • Market provides too little security
  • Regulation allocating bank liability can improve efficiency

Step 9: Deterring Fraudsters

Fraud prevention also affects fraudster incentives. Fraudster commits fraud if:

Expected gain > Expected cost G(1 - P) > TP

Where:

  • G = gain from fraud
  • P = probability of detection
  • T = punishment

More bank liability → more bank investment → higher P and lower G (frauds stopped) → fewer profitable fraud attempts

This indirect deterrence effect multiplies the direct benefit of fraud prevention.

Step 10: Summary of Determinants

Liability should be allocated based on:

  1. Least-cost avoider: Who can prevent fraud most cheaply?
  2. Effectiveness: Whose precautions most reduce fraud?
  3. Bilateral care: Do both parties' actions matter?
  4. Risk tolerance: Who can better bear risk?
  5. Information: Who has better information about fraud patterns?
  6. Heterogeneity: Do optimal rules vary by customer type?
  7. Deterrence: Which allocation best deters fraudsters?

Key Assumptions:

  • Parties respond rationally to liability incentives
  • Precaution costs and effectiveness are as estimated
  • Social cost of fraud exceeds private transfer (deadweight loss)
  • Banks can't perfectly monitor individual customer behavior
  • Enforcement of liability rules is feasible and not too costly

How conclusions change:

If customer caution is very cheap and effective:

  • Customer liability becomes more efficient
  • Current bank liability rules may create moral hazard

If banks can costlessly detect all fraud:

  • Bank liability clearly efficient
  • Banks can achieve near-zero fraud at low cost

If shared liability creates sufficient bilateral incentives:

  • May dominate pure liability rules
  • But creates weaker incentives than optimal

If customers are extremely heterogeneous:

  • Uniform rule is inefficient
  • Might need tiered system or customer choice of contract

If fraud prevention has public good aspects:

  • Bank investment benefits all customers collectively
  • Favors bank liability to internalize spillovers

Conclusion

Efficient liability allocation depends on who is the least-cost avoider. If banks can prevent fraud more cheaply through automated systems while customer caution is costly and less effective, bank liability is efficient. If customer caution is cheap and effective, customer liability may be better. For many fraud types, both parties' actions matter, suggesting shared liability or negligence rules. Risk aversion and information advantages generally favor bank liability, but this must be balanced against incentive effects and total costs.

▶ Question 12.2: Auditor Wealth and the Big Four: Judgment-Proof Problem

Question:

Part 1: Your firm is considering hiring audit firm A or audit firm B to audit the firm's accounts. The two audit firms are equally competent and charge the same price. The only difference is that audit firm A is wealthier. Firm A has more assets and less debt than firm B. Should this fact matter for which auditor you choose? Why? Explain using economic theory.

Part 2: Use your answer to Part 1 to provide an economic argument for why auditing is dominated by four large firms (the "Big Four").

▶ What a Good Answer Should Contain

A good answer should use the economic theory of tort liability, specifically the judgment-proof problem. Your analysis should explain why liability rules only create efficient incentives when defendants can pay damages, show how wealthier audit firms face stronger incentives to conduct high-quality audits due to higher potential liability, demonstrate with numerical examples how limited wealth caps effective liability below actual damages, explain how investor confidence depends on auditor's ability to compensate for losses, and extend the logic to market structure showing how wealth requirements create barriers to entry favoring large firms. A sophisticated answer will discuss the interaction between negligence standards and wealth constraints.

▶ Example solution

Baseline and Change:

Baseline: Two audit firms, A and B:

  • Firm A: Wealthy (assets: €1,000 million, low debt)
  • Firm B: Less wealthy (assets: €100 million, higher debt)
  • Both equally competent, same price

Question: Which should you hire, and why does this lead to Big Four dominance?

Key insight: Effective liability is capped at defendant's wealth, creating different incentive structures.

Actors and Incentives:

Key actors:

  1. Your firm: Choosing auditor to maximize investor confidence and investment
  2. Investors: Deciding whether to invest based on audit quality
  3. Audit firms A and B: Choosing audit quality (effort, following standards)

Relevant margins:

  • Audit firm's choice: High-quality audit (costly) vs. low-quality audit (saves costs)
  • Investor's choice: How much to invest given auditor's incentives
  • Your firm's choice: Which auditor creates more investor confidence

Mechanism: Liability creates incentives, but only up to defendant's wealth.

Economic Mechanism:

Framework: Judgment-proof problem in tort liability

Perfect Liability World (Benchmark)

If audit firms had unlimited wealth:

  • Firm liable for all damages caused by negligent audit
  • If investors lose €500 million due to undetected fraud, audit firm pays €500 million
  • Perfect deterrence: Audit firm has full incentive to avoid negligence
  • Cost of negligence to firm = Social cost of negligence
  • Efficient incentives

Realistic World with Wealth Constraints

Scenario 1: Firm B (€100 million wealth) as auditor

Investors invest €500 million. Firm B conducts substandard audit that fails to detect fraud.

If fraud detected and firm B was negligent:

  • Investors' losses: €500 million
  • Firm B's maximum liability: €100 million (then bankrupt)
  • Firm B's effective punishment: Only €100 million

Firm B's cost-benefit of cutting audit quality:

  • Benefit: Save €10 million in audit costs
  • Cost if caught: Maximum €100 million (probability p)
  • Expected cost: p × €100 million

Firm B cuts quality if: €10 million > p × €100 million

  • If p = 5%: €10 million > €5 million → Cuts quality
  • If p = 15%: €10 million < €15 million → Maintains quality

Scenario 2: Firm A (€1,000 million wealth) as auditor

Same situation, but Firm A can pay full damages.

If fraud detected and Firm A was negligent:

  • Investors' losses: €500 million
  • Firm A's maximum liability: €500 million (has enough wealth)
  • Firm A's effective punishment: Full €500 million

Firm A's cost-benefit:

  • Benefit: Save €10 million in audit costs
  • Cost if caught: €500 million (probability p)
  • Expected cost: p × €500 million

Firm A cuts quality if: €10 million > p × €500 million

  • If p = 5%: €10 million < €25 million → Maintains quality
  • If p = 2.5%: €10 million < €12.5 million → Maintains quality
  • Need p < 2% for Firm A to cut quality

Key result: Firm A has much stronger incentives to maintain audit quality.

Investor Response

Investors understand these incentives:

  • With Firm B: Only willing to invest up to ~€100 million (matching Firm B's max liability)
  • With Firm A: Willing to invest up to €1,000 million (matching Firm A's wealth)

Your firm's choice:

Hiring Firm A enables you to raise more capital from investors because:

  1. Firm A has better incentives (faces higher effective liability)
  2. Investors are better protected (can recover more if fraud occurs)
  3. Audit opinion by Firm A is more credible

You choose Firm A even though both firms charge same price and have same competence.

Illustration:

Numerical Example: Audit Quality Decision

Setup:

  • Following audit standards costs €10 million
  • Cutting corners saves €10 million
  • If fraud occurs and audit was substandard, detected with probability p = 10%
  • Investor losses from undetected fraud: €500 million

Firm B's calculation (wealth = €100 million):

Cost-benefit of maintaining standards:

  • Benefit: Avoid p × min(€500M, €100M) = 0.10 × €100M = €10M
  • Cost: €10M

Net benefit: €10M - €10M = €0

Firm B is indifferent! Even might cut quality if there's any uncertainty.

Firm A's calculation (wealth = €1,000 million):

Cost-benefit of maintaining standards:

  • Benefit: Avoid p × min(€500M, €1,000M) = 0.10 × €500M = €50M
  • Cost: €10M

Net benefit: €50M - €10M = €40M

Firm A clearly maintains standards.

Market Equilibrium Effect

Because investors know this:

Auditor Max Protection Willing Investment Firm's Market Value
Firm B €100M ~€100M Lower
Firm A €1,000M ~€500M+ Higher

Your firm chooses Firm A because it enables raising more capital, increasing firm value.

Big Four Dominance Mechanism

  1. Wealth requirement creates barrier to entry
    • Need substantial assets to credibly audit large corporations
    • Small firm can't credibly audit €10 billion company
    • Effective liability capped at small firm's wealth
  2. Network effects and reputation
    • Large size → More wealth → Better incentives → Better reputation
    • Better reputation → More clients → Larger scale → More wealth
    • Positive feedback loop
  3. Historical example:
    • KPMG paid NOK 600 million in Finance Credit case
    • Small audit firm could never pay such damages
    • Would be bankrupt, leaving investors uncompensated
  4. Market equilibrium:
    • Large corporations need auditors with wealth matching their scale
    • Only Big Four have sufficient wealth for largest audits
    • Natural oligopoly structure

Assumptions and Robustness:

Key Assumption 1: Auditors are only liable if negligent

Under strict liability:

  • Even following standards perfectly, might face liability
  • Wealth still matters (same judgment-proof logic)
  • But both firms would demand higher fees to compensate for risk
  • Might discourage auditing altogether if risk too high

Under no liability:

  • Wealth irrelevant (can't be sued anyway)
  • Only reputation mechanisms matter
  • Audit quality likely lower overall

Conclusion: Negligence rule with wealth-constrained liability is middle ground.

Key Assumption 2: Perfect information about audit firm wealth

If investors can't observe audit firm wealth:

  • Can't discriminate based on incentive quality
  • Market for lemons problem: Low-quality auditors not driven out
  • Need disclosure requirements or certification
  • Credit ratings, financial transparency become important

Key Assumption 3: Limited liability protection for individuals

If personal liability for auditors:

  • Individual partners' wealth also matters
  • Can pierce corporate veil in fraud cases
  • Stronger incentives even for smaller firms
  • But might discourage talented people from becoming auditors

Key Assumption 4: Bankruptcy law allows full liability payment

If bankruptcy protections allow audit firm to continue operating:

  • Effective penalty less than full wealth
  • Weakens incentive mechanism
  • Need special rules for professional service firms

Key Assumption 5: Reputation effects are secondary

If reputation effects dominate:

  • Even small firms might have strong incentives
  • Fear of losing all future business
  • But reputation mechanisms require:
    • Transparent information about past failures
    • Long-term repeated interactions
    • Ability to credibly commit to quality
  • Wealth still matters as backstop

Key Assumption 6: Audit standards are optimal

If standards too strict or too lax:

  • Negligence threshold affects incentives
  • Very strict standards: Even wealthy firms might not comply (too costly)
  • Very lax standards: Wealth irrelevant (everyone meets standard)
  • Optimal standard: Marginal cost = Marginal benefit

Conditional Conclusion:

You should hire the wealthier audit firm (Firm A) when:

  1. Investment amounts are large
    • If you're raising €500 million, need auditor with at least that much wealth
    • Otherwise judgment-proof problem undermines incentives
  2. Detection probability is modest
    • If detecting substandard audits is certain (p = 100%), wealth less important
    • If detection uncertain (p = 10%), wealth crucial for maintaining incentives
  3. Potential fraud losses are substantial
    • Small potential losses → Even small firm faces adequate liability
    • Large potential losses → Need wealthy firm to maintain full incentives
  4. Reputation mechanisms are imperfect
    • If reputation perfectly deters fraud, wealth irrelevant
    • In reality, reputation incomplete → Wealth provides backstop

Why Big Four dominate:

  1. Scale economies in wealth
    • Need €1 billion wealth to credibly audit €5 billion company
    • Fixed cost of building that wealth
    • Natural oligopoly
  2. Positive feedback loop
    • Wealth → Better incentives → Better reputation → More clients → More wealth
    • Hard for small firms to break in
  3. Regulatory reinforcement
    • Some regulations require Big Four audits
    • Especially for public companies, banks, regulated entities
    • Creates lock-in effect
  4. Insurance and risk-pooling
    • Large firms can better pool risks across clients
    • Self-insure against occasional failures
    • Small firms might need expensive insurance

Market structure implications:

Feature Small Audit Firms Big Four
Wealth €10-100M €500M-1000M+
Max credible audit €100M companies €10B+ companies
Effective liability Capped low Near-full
Incentive quality Weak Strong
Market position Small clients Large clients

Policy implications:

  1. Can't easily break Big Four oligopoly
    • Fundamental economics of judgment-proof problem
    • Not just anticompetitive behavior
  2. Might need regulation
    • Mandatory insurance for smaller audit firms
    • Joint and several liability arrangements
    • Public backstop for audit failures
  3. Trade-off: Competition vs. Quality
    • More competition → Lower prices
    • But: Fragmented market → More judgment-proof firms → Weaker incentives
    • Concentration may be efficient in audit industry

Conclusion: Wealthier audit firms provide better investor protection not because they're more competent, but because they face stronger incentives to maintain quality. The judgment-proof problem creates a natural barrier to entry, explaining Big Four dominance through economic fundamentals rather than anticompetitive behavior.

▶ Question 12.3: McDonald's Coffee Case: Negligence vs. Strict Liability

Case Background:

Stella Liebeck, a 79-year-old US woman, suffered third-degree burns when she accidentally spilled coffee in her lap after purchasing it from a McDonald's restaurant in 1992. She was hospitalized for eight days followed by two years of medical treatment. Liebeck sought to settle with McDonald's for $20,000 to cover her medical expenses. When McDonald's refused, Liebeck's attorney filed suit accusing McDonald's of gross negligence.

Liebeck's attorneys argued that, at 180–190 °F (82–88 °C), McDonald's coffee was defective, and more likely to cause serious injury than coffee served at any other establishment.

Questions:

  1. Assume "negligent" means socially inefficient. Which factors then determine whether McDonald's should be considered negligent? Use economic theory.
  2. Assume McDonald's had strict liability for any harm caused by their coffee. Would that be a good rule? Why/why not? Explain using economic theory.
▶ What a Good Answer Should Contain

A good answer should use the Hand formula (cost-benefit analysis) to define negligence as inefficient precaution. Your analysis should identify the relevant costs (customer value of hot coffee) and benefits (reduced accident probability and severity), use numerical examples to show when serving very hot coffee is socially inefficient, discuss the role of information asymmetry (customers may not know the burn risk), and compare negligence versus strict liability for bilateral care situations. The key economic insight is that negligence gives both parties efficient incentives when customers can observe risks, but strict liability creates moral hazard by removing victim incentives to avoid spills.

▶ Example solution

Baseline and Change:

Question 1: When is McDonald's negligent?

Baseline: McDonald's serves coffee at 85°C (185°F) Alternative: McDonald's could serve coffee at 65°C (149°F)

Temperature choice affects:

  • Probability and severity of burns if spilled
  • Customer enjoyment of coffee
  • Cost of production (assume same for simplicity)

Question 2: Negligence vs. strict liability

Negligence rule: McDonald's liable only if coffee temperature was inefficiently high Strict liability: McDonald's liable for all coffee burns regardless of precaution level

Actors and Incentives:

Key actors:

  1. McDonald's: Chooses coffee temperature
  2. Customers: Choose whether to buy and how carefully to handle coffee

Relevant margins:

  • McDonald's: Temperature setting (65°C vs. 85°C)
  • Customers: Precaution level when handling coffee (careful vs. reckless)

Bilateral care situation: Both parties can affect accident probability and severity.

Economic Mechanism:

Framework: Hand formula for negligence (cost-benefit analysis)

McDonald's is negligent if: Cost of precaution < Benefit in terms of reduced expected harm

Or equivalently: B < PL

Where:

  • B = Burden of precaution (cost of serving cooler coffee)
  • P = Probability of accident
  • L = Loss if accident occurs

Numerical Example for Question 1

Assume:

  • McDonald's can serve coffee at 65°C or 85°C
  • Production costs are identical
  • McDonald's sells 1 million cups per year

Customer preferences:

  • Value 85°C coffee at $3.10 per cup
  • Value 65°C coffee at $3.00 per cup
  • Difference: $0.10 per cup benefit from hotter coffee

Accident risk:

  • At 65°C: Probability of third-degree burn ≈ 0% (coffee cools quickly, less severe if spilled)
  • At 85°C: Probability of third-degree burn = 0.005% = 0.00005

Cost of third-degree burn: $1,000,000

  • Includes: Medical expenses, pain and suffering, lost work
  • Amount that makes victim indifferent between burn + compensation vs. no burn

Social cost-benefit analysis:

Benefit of 85°C coffee (B):

  • Customer value: $0.10 × 1,000,000 cups = $100,000/year

Cost of 85°C coffee (PL):

  • Expected harm: 0.00005 × 1,000,000 × $1,000,000 = $50,000/year

Hand formula:

  • B ($100,000) > PL ($50,000)?
  • No! Cost ($50,000) < Benefit ($100,000)

Wait, let me recalculate more carefully for per-cup analysis.

Per-cup analysis:

For each cup sold at 85°C:

  • Customer benefit: $0.10
  • Expected harm: 0.00005 × $1,000,000 = $50

McDonald's is negligent if: $0.10 < $50

Since $50 >> $0.10, serving 85°C coffee is socially inefficient → Negligent.

Key determinants of negligence:

  1. Probability of burn at different temperatures
    • Large difference (0% vs. 0.005%) → More likely negligent
    • Small difference → Less likely negligent
  2. Severity of harm
    • Third-degree burns ($1M cost) → More likely negligent
    • Minor burns ($10K cost) → Less likely negligent
  3. Customer value of hot coffee
    • If customers value 85°C much more (say $100 more) → Less likely negligent
    • If minimal value difference ($0.10) → More likely negligent
  4. Information and rationality
    • Do customers know the burn risk?
    • Do they correctly assess $50 expected harm?
    • If not, market failure argument

Complication: Why would customers buy dangerous coffee?

If 85°C coffee imposes $50 expected harm but only provides $0.10 benefit, why do customers buy it?

Possible explanations:

  1. Information asymmetry:
    • Customers don't know coffee is 85°C
    • Don't know third-degree burns can occur in <3 seconds
    • Don't know how severe third-degree burns are
    • McDonald's serves much hotter coffee than competitors
  2. Irrational risk assessment:
    • Customers underestimate low-probability high-consequence risks
    • Focus on immediate benefit ($0.10) versus abstract risk
  3. Revealed preference counter-argument:
    • If customers buy despite risk, maybe they actually value it at >$50?
    • First Welfare Theorem: Market outcome is efficient if no externalities, perfect info, competition
    • Negligence finding requires showing market failure (likely information asymmetry)

Information asymmetry argument:

If customers don't know McDonald's coffee is unusually hot and dangerous:

  • Can't make informed trade-off
  • Might buy 85°C coffee thinking it's normal temperature
  • Market outcome inefficient
  • Negligence finding justified

Illustration:

Question 2: Strict Liability Analysis

Strict liability rule: McDonald's pays $1,000,000 for any third-degree burn, regardless of whether 85°C was negligent.

Assume compensation is perfect: $1,000,000 makes victim exactly indifferent between burn+payment vs. no burn.

McDonald's incentives under strict liability:

Expected cost of selling at 85°C:

  • Per cup: 0.00005 × $1,000,000 = $50
  • Must charge customers at least $50 more to break even
  • But customers only value it at $0.10 more
  • McDonald's switches to 65°C (efficient!)

Customer incentives under strict liability:

Customer knows: "If I spill coffee and get burned, McDonald's pays $1,000,000."

Customer's payoff from burn:

  • Suffering from burn: -$1,000,000 (pain, medical treatment, lost work)
  • Compensation from McDonald's: +$1,000,000
  • Net: $0 (indifferent)

Moral hazard problem:

Since customer is fully compensated:

  • No incentive to avoid spills
  • Might drive recklessly with coffee in lap
  • Might fail to secure cup properly
  • Might multitask while holding hot beverage

Numerical example:

Customer can choose:

  • Careful: Costs $5 in effort (secure cup, focus on coffee), probability of spill = 0.00005
  • Reckless: Costs $0 in effort (coffee in lap while driving), probability of spill = 0.0005 (10x higher)

Under negligence (customer bears cost of own carelessness):

  • Careful: Cost = $5 + 0.00005 × $1,000,000 = $5 + $50 = $55
  • Reckless: Cost = $0 + 0.0005 × $1,000,000 = $0 + $500 = $500
  • Choose careful (efficient!)

Under strict liability (McDonald's bears all costs):

  • Careful: Cost = $5 + 0.00005 × $0 = $5
  • Reckless: Cost = $0 + 0.0005 × $0 = $0
  • Choose reckless (inefficient!)

Result: Strict liability leads to:

  • Too many spills (0.0005 instead of 0.00005)
  • Higher social cost
  • Inefficient outcome

Strict liability is not a good rule for bilateral care cases.

Assumptions and Robustness:

Key Assumption 1: Customers can take cost-effective precautions

If customers cannot affect spill probability:

  • Only McDonald's controls risk (temperature choice)
  • No bilateral care problem
  • Strict liability is efficient
  • Customer moral hazard doesn't arise

Example: Burns from manufacturing defect (exploding cup)

  • Customer can't prevent explosion
  • Strict liability appropriate

Key Assumption 2: Perfect compensation is possible

If compensation over-compensates:

  • Customers might prefer burn + payment
  • Even worse moral hazard
  • Deliberately cause accidents

If compensation under-compensates:

  • Customers have some incentive to avoid burns
  • Partially mitigates moral hazard
  • But creates unfairness (victims not made whole)

Key Assumption 3: Customers can observe and assess risks

Under perfect information:

  • Customers know coffee is 85°C and burn risk
  • Market outcome efficient (if they buy, they value >$50)
  • No negligence finding warranted
  • First Welfare Theorem applies

Under information asymmetry:

  • Customers don't know unusual danger
  • Market failure
  • Negligence finding justified
  • This is likely the realistic case

Key Assumption 4: Courts can determine efficient temperature

If courts cannot assess optimal temperature:

  • Hard to apply negligence standard correctly
  • Might over- or under-deter
  • Strict liability might be second-best despite moral hazard

If courts can use industry standards:

  • Other restaurants serve at 65-70°C
  • McDonald's at 85°C is outlier
  • Industry standard proxy for efficient level

Key Assumption 5: No contributory negligence defense

With contributory negligence:

  • McDonald's not liable if customer was also negligent
  • Customer has incentive to be careful
  • Can achieve efficiency even under strict liability for manufacturer
  • Requires court to assess customer care level

Key Assumption 6: Transaction costs prevent contracting

If transaction costs were zero (Coase Theorem):

  • Customers and McDonald's could contract around liability rule
  • Efficient outcome regardless of legal rule
  • "Buy this coffee: $3.10, we're not liable for burns if you're careless"
  • Reality: Contract costs too high for coffee purchase

Conditional Conclusion:

Question 1: McDonald's is negligent when:

  1. Expected harm exceeds customer benefit
    • Probability × Severity × Customer count > Value difference × Customer count
    • 0.00005 × $1M × 1M > $0.10 × 1M
    • $50M > $100K → Negligent
  2. Information asymmetry prevents efficient market outcome
    • Customers don't know coffee is unusually hot
    • Can't make informed choice
    • Market failure justifies negligence finding
  3. Industry standards show feasible safer alternative
    • Other restaurants serve at 65-70°C
    • Demonstrates 85°C not necessary
    • McDonald's is outlier

Key factors:

  • Magnitude of increased risk (probability and severity)
  • Customer value of higher temperature
  • Customer information about risks
  • Availability of safer alternatives

Question 2: Strict liability is not a good rule when:

  1. Bilateral care problem exists
    • Both McDonald's (temperature) and customer (handling care) affect accident risk
    • Strict liability eliminates customer incentive
    • Leads to excessive accidents
  2. Customers can take cost-effective precautions
    • Careful handling costs $5, prevents $45 in expected harm
    • Under strict liability, customer doesn't take precaution
    • Social waste
  3. Perfect compensation is feasible
    • Makes moral hazard problem worse
    • Customer indifferent to accidents

When strict liability might be preferred:

  1. Unilateral care:
    • Only manufacturer can prevent harm
    • Customers can't affect risk
    • Example: Manufacturing defects
  2. Information asymmetry is severe:
    • Customers can't assess optimal care
    • Manufacturer has much better information
    • Places incentive on informed party
  3. Administrative costs of negligence are high:
    • Hard for courts to determine efficient temperature
    • Strict liability avoids need for cost-benefit analysis

Optimal rule: Negligence with contributory negligence defense

  • McDonald's liable if temperature inefficiently high (negligence)
  • But not liable if customer was careless (contributory negligence)
  • Gives both parties efficient incentives
  • Achieves efficient temperature and efficient customer care

The economic logic: Liability rules should place risk on the party best able to prevent it at lowest cost. In bilateral care situations, negligence rules (with defenses) give both parties proper incentives. Strict liability removes victim incentives, leading to moral hazard and excessive accidents. The McDonald's case involves bilateral care (temperature choice and handling care), making negligence the appropriate standard.

Contracts

Why Contracts Exist

Foundations used:

  • Legal Incentives Model
  • Transaction Costs
  • Self-Enforcement

Learning goal: apply commitment logic to understand when contracts enable cooperation.

Types of Questions You Should Be Able to Answer

  • Why do mutually beneficial transactions sometimes fail to occur?
  • When can legal enforcement solve commitment problems?
  • When is self-enforcement sufficient without courts?

Many economically valuable transactions involve cooperation over time. One party must take an action today—invest, deliver, or exert effort—while the other party’s reciprocal action occurs later. Examples include public procurement, supplier contracts with delayed payment, employment relationships, and credit agreements.

From a social perspective, many such interactions are efficient: if both parties perform as promised, total benefits exceed total costs. In principle, there exists a division of surplus that makes both sides better off.

Yet in practice, these mutually beneficial transactions often fail to occur or are carried out inefficiently. The central reason is not disagreement about prices or values, but the inability of parties to credibly commit to future behavior.

Promises alone are often insufficient. Once one party has acted, the other may have an incentive to behave opportunistically—to withhold performance, cut quality, or refuse payment. Anticipating this, rational parties may refuse to cooperate in the first place, even when cooperation would create surplus.

This is not a moral problem, but an incentive problem. Law and economics treats distrust as a predictable outcome of rational behavior under weak commitment. The economic role of contracts is therefore not merely to record promises, but to change incentives by making commitments credible.

A Simple Example: Public Procurement Without Contracts

To see how cooperation can fail even when it is efficient, consider a simplified public procurement example.

The state wants a private contractor to build a new highway and railway:

  • Construction costs the contractor 1.5 (billion NOK).
  • The state values the completed project at 2.
  • Total surplus from completion is therefore 0.5.

From a social perspective, the project should go ahead. There exists a payment—say 1.8— that gives the contractor a payoff of 0.3 and the state a payoff of 0.2.

Now consider the interaction without a contract. The timing is as follows:

  1. The state decides whether to pay.
  2. After observing the state’s decision, the contractor decides whether to build.

The payoffs are:

  • If the state pays and the contractor builds: (0.2, 0.3).
  • If the state pays and the contractor does nothing: the contractor keeps the payment.
  • If the state does not pay: nothing happens and both receive 0.

Only one outcome creates positive total surplus: payment followed by construction. However, this outcome is not stable when promises are unenforced.

If the state were to pay first, the contractor would then face a choice between building and keeping the payment without building. Because construction costs are incurred after payment and there is no penalty for non-performance, the contractor prefers not to build. Anticipating this incentive, the state rationally refuses to pay in the first place.

The outcome is therefore no payment, no construction, and zero surplus—despite the existence of a mutually beneficial transaction.

The Commitment Problem

The failure of cooperation in the example reflects a general commitment problem. At the moment performance is required, the other party’s obligation is already sunk. Promises about future actions are therefore cheap to make but costly to keep.

This logic is symmetric. If the contractor were required to build first and receive payment only after completion, the state would face the temptation not to pay. Anticipating this, the contractor would refuse to invest.

The key insight is that rational parties cannot rely on unenforced promises when actions are separated in time. Inefficient outcomes arise not because parties are irrational, but because incentives make non-cooperation individually optimal.

Contracts as Commitment Devices

Contracts solve the commitment problem by making promises costly to break. Their central economic function is to reshape incentives so that performance becomes individually optimal.

Return to the procurement example. Suppose the parties sign a legally enforceable contract specifying payment of 1.8 in exchange for construction. If the contractor fails to perform, the state can sue for breach of contract.

Assume that:

  • Compensatory damages equal the state’s lost benefit of 2.
  • Legal costs are 0.1 and borne by the losing party.

After receiving payment, the contractor now faces the following choice:

  • Build: incur a cost of 1.5 and earn 0.3.
  • Breach: pay damages of 2 plus legal costs of 0.1, yielding –0.3.

Because breach is now more costly than performance, construction becomes the contractor’s optimal action. Anticipating this, the state is willing to pay. Legal enforcement transforms the cooperative outcome into the stable outcome of rational behavior.

Importantly, this result does not depend on trust or moral motivation. Even a purely self-interested party will perform when breach is sufficiently costly. Contract law substitutes for trust by providing external enforcement.

Contracts and Self-Enforcement

Contracts are not always enforced primarily through courts. The same commitment problems can sometimes be addressed through private enforcement mechanisms such as reputation, repeated interaction, and the value of future trade.

Summary

This chapter has explained why contracts are necessary from an economic perspective. Many mutually beneficial transactions involve delayed exchange and relationship-specific investments. Without enforceable commitments, rational parties anticipate opportunistic behavior and may refuse to cooperate, even when cooperation would create surplus.

Contracts solve this problem by changing incentives. By making promises legally enforceable, contract law makes breach costly and performance privately optimal. In doing so, contracts convert interactions where cooperation breaks down into interactions where cooperation is sustainable.

This chapter focuses on why contracts exist and how enforcement enables cooperation. Questions about when private contracts should be constrained or overridden are taken up in the next chapter on the limits of private ordering.

Exam Questions

▶ Question 13.1: Startup Payment Structure: Is Customer Right to Worry?

Case Description:

A start-up tech company develops AI-powered apps for its customers. Since the company is low on cash, it prefers to receive half the payment before it starts developing the app. The remaining half of the payment is paid when the company delivers the app to the customer.

Question:

A potential customer wants to develop an app but is worried that the company will break its promise and not deliver the app after receiving the first half of the payment. Is the potential customer right to worry? Explain using economic theory.

▶ What a Good Answer Should Contain
  1. Model the situation as a sequential game
  2. Use backward induction to determine the company's incentives at each stage
  3. Use a numerical example to show when the company has incentives to breach
  4. Explain the conditions under which the customer's worry is justified
  5. Identify what additional mechanisms (legal, reputational) might change the analysis
▶ Example solution

Framework: Sequential Game and Incentive Analysis

The question asks whether the customer is right to worry about non-delivery. We can model this as a sequential game and use backward induction to determine the company's incentives.

Step 1: Model as Sequential Game

Sequence of moves:

  1. Customer pays first half (50% of total price)
  2. Company decides: develop app or keep money and breach
  3. If company delivers, customer pays second half

Numerical example:

  • Total price: 100
  • First payment: 50 (paid upfront)
  • Second payment: 50 (paid on delivery)
  • Development cost: 80
  • Customer's value: 100 (or higher)

Step 2: Backward Induction Analysis

Stage 3: Customer's decision after receiving app If the company delivers the app, will the customer pay the remaining 50?

  • Customer gets app worth 100
  • Customer has already paid 50
  • If customer pays remaining 50: Net value = 100 - 100 = 0
  • If customer refuses to pay: Net value = 100 - 50 = 50

Assume the customer can be legally forced to pay (enforceable contract) or values the relationship → Customer pays.

Stage 2: Company's decision after receiving first payment Company has received 50 and must decide whether to develop app.

Option A: Develop the app

  • Cost: 80
  • Receive second payment: 50
  • Net from this point: 50 - 80 = -30
  • Total net (including first payment): 50 + 50 - 80 = 20

Option B: Keep the money and breach

  • No development cost
  • No second payment
  • Net: 50 (the first payment received)
  • If no legal or reputational consequences

Comparison:

  • Developing: Net 20 from the full transaction
  • Breaching: Net 50 from keeping first payment

If the company faces no legal or reputational consequences, the company maximizes profit by breaching: 50 > 20 → Company keeps the money and doesn't develop the app

Conclusion: The customer is RIGHT to worry. Without additional enforcement mechanisms, the company has incentives to take the first payment and not deliver.

Step 3: General Condition

Let:

  • P = Total price
  • α = Fraction paid upfront (0.5 in this case)
  • C = Development cost
  • D = Damages if company breaches (assume 0 if no enforcement)

Company delivers if: (1-α)P - C ≥ αP - D

Simplifying: P - C ≥ αP - D (1-α)P - C ≥ -D C ≤ P - αP + D

In our example:

  • C = 80, P = 100, α = 0.5, D = 0
  • Company delivers if: 80 ≤ 100 - 50 + 0 = 50
  • This is FALSE (80 > 50) → Company breaches

Step 4: When Would the Company Deliver?

The company would deliver without legal/reputational consequences only if:

  • Development cost is very low: C ≤ (1-α)P
  • In our example: C ≤ 50

With C = 80, the second payment (50) is insufficient to cover the remaining cost after accounting for the first payment already received.

Step 5: What Changes the Analysis?

The customer's worry is justified UNLESS:

1. Legal enforcement If customer can sue and recover damages D ≥ customer's value (e.g., D = 120):

  • Company's choice: Develop and get 20, or breach and pay 120 in damages → net -70
  • Company delivers (20 > -70)

2. Reputational concerns If breach destroys company's reputation and future business worth V:

  • Company's choice: Develop and get 20 + V (future business), or breach and get 50 + 0
  • Company delivers if 20 + V > 50 → V > 30

3. Relationship value If customer is a repeat customer worth future profits F:

  • Company delivers if 20 + F > 50 → F > 30

4. Different payment structure If payment was 20 upfront and 80 on delivery:

  • Develop: 20 + 80 - 80 = 20
  • Breach: 20
  • Indifferent (or slightly prefer delivery to avoid legal/reputational issues)

Key Insight

The payment structure creates a commitment problem. By asking for 50% upfront, the company:

  • Reduces its own financial risk (gets cash to fund development)
  • But creates incentive to breach (since it has already captured value exceeding its net profit from completion)

This is why the customer is right to worry without additional enforcement mechanisms.

Key Assumptions:

  • Company is purely profit-maximizing
  • No legal enforcement (or weak enforcement with D = 0)
  • No reputational or relationship concerns
  • Customer will pay second half if app is delivered

How conclusions change:

  • If company values reputation or repeat business → worry is reduced
  • If contract is legally enforceable with high damages → worry is reduced
  • If payment structure is more back-loaded → worry is reduced
  • If development cost is lower → worry is reduced (company might deliver even without enforcement)
▶ Question 13.2: Startup Trust Mechanisms: Multiple Solutions to Commitment Problems

Case Description:
