A start-up tech company develops AI-powered apps for its customers. Since the company is low on cash, it prefers to receive half the payment before it starts developing the app. The remaining half of the payment is paid when the company delivers the app to the customer.

A potential customer wants to develop an app but is worried that the company will break its promise and not deliver the app after receiving the first half of the payment.

Question:

To attract customers, the company needs to convince customers that its promises of delivering apps are trustworthy. How can the company increase its customers' trust in the promises? Think of as many solutions as possible. For each solution, explain which factors determine whether the solution is likely to work and why these factors matter. Use economic theory and give concrete examples.

▶ What a Good Answer Should Contain
  1. Identify multiple mechanisms: formal contracts, reputation, relational contracts, payment restructuring, escrow
  2. For each mechanism, explain:
    • How it works (economic logic)
    • When it is likely to be effective
    • Numerical examples illustrating the conditions
  3. Use explicit economic theory: self-enforcement, repeated games, litigation incentives, Nash bargaining
  4. Be comprehensive (this is worth 40 points, so provide multiple solutions with depth)
▶ Example solution

Framework: Multiple Solutions to Commitment Problems

The question asks how the company can make its promises credible. I will analyze four main solutions:

  1. Formal contracts enforceable in court
  2. Reputational mechanisms
  3. Relational contracts (repeated interaction)
  4. Payment restructuring and escrow

Solution 1: Formal Contracts Enforceable in Court

How it works: If the company fails to deliver, the customer can sue for damages. The threat of litigation gives the company incentives to perform.

Economic analysis:

Assume:

  • App development cost: 80
  • Total payment: 100 (50 upfront, 50 on delivery)
  • Customer's value: 120
  • Litigation cost: 20 (paid by loser)
  • Probability customer wins if company breaches: 90%
  • Expectation damages if customer wins: 120

Customer's expected value from lawsuit: 0.9 × 120 - 0.1 × 20 = 108 - 2 = 106

Company's expected cost of lawsuit: 0.9 × (120 + 20) = 0.9 × 140 = 126

Nash bargaining settlement: Settlement = (106 + 126) / 2 = 116

Company's decision:

  • Deliver app: Revenue 100, Cost 80, Net = 20
  • Breach and settle: Keep first payment 50, Pay settlement 116, Net = 50 - 116 = -66

Company delivers! (20 > -66)

When formal contracts work:

The threat of litigation is credible when:

  1. Customer has incentive to sue: Expected damages > litigation costs
  2. Damages are sufficiently high: Settlement or judgment exceeds company's gain from breach
  3. Probability of winning is high: Customer can prove breach
  4. Legal system is functional: Courts enforce contracts

When formal contracts might fail:

Example 1: High time costs and impatience If lawsuit takes 4 years and parties are impatient (high discount rate):

  • Present value of 120 in 4 years: 60 (for both parties)
  • Customer's expected value: 0.9 × 60 - 0.1 × 20 = 52
  • Company's expected cost: 0.9 × 80 = 72
  • Settlement: (52 + 72) / 2 = 62

Company's decision:

  • Deliver: 20
  • Breach and settle: 50 - 62 = -12

Still delivers, but margin is smaller. With slightly different numbers, breach could be profitable.

Example 2: Customer pays own legal fees (American rule)

  • Customer's expected value: 0.9 × 120 - 20 = 88
  • Company's expected cost: 0.9 × 120 + 20 = 128
  • Settlement: (88 + 128) / 2 = 108

Company still delivers (20 > 50 - 108), but if app value is lower, customer might not sue.

Example 3: Small claims If app value is only 10:

  • Customer's lawsuit value: 0.9 × 10 - 10 = -1 (negative!)
  • Customer won't sue → threat not credible
  • Company can breach with impunity

Conclusion for formal contracts: Works when damages are high relative to litigation costs and legal system is efficient.

—

Solution 2: Reputational Mechanisms

How it works: If the company cheats one customer, future customers learn about this and refuse to do business. The company has incentives to maintain reputation by delivering.

Economic analysis:

Let:

  • Profit from one customer: 20 (if delivers)
  • Short-term gain from cheating: 50 (keep first payment) - 20 (forgone second payment and future) = 30
  • Number of future customers lost if reputation destroyed: N
  • Profit per future customer: 20

Company delivers if: Value of reputation ≥ Gain from cheating 20N ≥ 30 N ≥ 1.5

If company expects to lose at least 2 future customers due to bad reputation, it delivers.

Making reputation work:

Mechanism 1: Online review platforms Company operates on platform where customers can rate and review.

When this works:

  • Platform is widely used (potential customers see reviews)
  • Reviews are credible (verified purchases, hard to fake)
  • Platform persists (company can't rebrand easily)
  • Customers check reviews before hiring

Mechanism 2: Industry reputation In specialized markets (e.g., healthcare AI apps), word spreads through professional networks.

When this works:

  • Small, interconnected market
  • High value per customer (so losing future business is costly)
  • Customers communicate with each other

When reputation might fail:

Example: Short-lived company If company plans to exit market soon:

  • Expected future customers N = 0
  • Value of reputation = 0
  • Company breaches

Example: Low temptation If profit per customer is high (say 50) but gain from cheating is low (say 10):

  • Need to lose only 10/50 = 0.2 future customers
  • Reputation mechanism very strong

Conclusion for reputation: Works when (1) future business is valuable, (2) information about past behavior is observable, (3) temptation to cheat is low relative to relationship value.

—

Solution 3: Relational Contracts (Repeated Interaction)

How it works: If customer expects to need multiple apps over time, the company has incentive to deliver to preserve the ongoing relationship.

Economic analysis:

Assume:

  • Probability customer needs another app next period: 90%
  • Profit per app: 20
  • Discount factor: δ = 0.9 (values future)

Value of relationship (infinite horizon): V = 20 + 0.9 × 20 + 0.9² × 20 + … = 20 / (1 - 0.9) = 200

Company delivers if: Value of relationship > Gain from cheating 200 > 30 ✓

Company delivers!

When relational contracts work:

  1. High probability of future interaction
    • If probability drops to 50%: V = 20/(1-0.5×0.9) = 36.4
    • Still delivers (36.4 > 30), but closer
  2. Long time horizon (patience)
    • If δ = 0.5: V = 20/(1-0.45) = 36.4
    • Still delivers
  3. High profit per transaction
    • If profit is 50: V = 50/(1-0.81) = 263
    • Very strong incentive
  4. Low temptation to cheat
    • Better if structured as smaller projects (reduces gain from cheating on any one)

Practical implementation:

Approach 1: Split into milestones Instead of one big app, break into phases:

  • Phase 1 (cost 20, payment 25): Gain from breach = 5
  • Phase 2 (cost 20, payment 25): Gain from breach = 5
  • Phase 3 (cost 20, payment 25): Gain from breach = 5
  • Phase 4 (cost 20, payment 25): Gain from breach = 5

For company to cheat on any phase, would need: 5 > V_remaining

Much harder to breach when each temptation is small!

Approach 2: Volume commitments Customer commits to 10 apps per year for 3 years.

Conclusion for relational contracts: Works when future interaction is likely, valuable, and temptation per transaction is low.

—

Solution 4: Payment Restructuring and Escrow

How it works: Change payment structure so company has incentive to deliver without relying on legal/reputational mechanisms.

Approach A: Back-load payment Pay 20 upfront, 80 on delivery.

Company's decision:

  • Deliver: 20 + 80 - 80 = 20
  • Breach: 20

Indifferent or slightly prefers delivery!

Problem: Company might not have cash to fund development. This is why company wanted 50% upfront in the first place.

Approach B: Escrow

  1. Customer pays full 100 to escrow agent (trusted third party)
  2. Company develops app
  3. When company delivers, escrow releases 100 to company
  4. If company doesn't deliver, escrow returns 100 to customer

Company's decision:

  • Deliver: Get 100, cost 80, net = 20
  • Breach: Get 0

Company delivers!

When escrow works:

  1. Trusted third party exists
    • Bank, law firm, online escrow service
    • Must be credible and secure
  2. Cost of escrow is low
    • Typical escrow fees: 1-3%
    • Worth it if prevents breach
  3. Clear delivery criteria
    • Escrow agent can verify delivery
    • No ambiguity about whether app meets specifications

Variations:

Milestone-based escrow:

  • 25 released after design phase
  • 25 released after development
  • 25 released after testing
  • 25 released after deployment

Provides company with cash flow while maintaining incentives.

Conclusion for restructuring: Escrow solves the commitment problem but requires trusted third party and clear verification.

—

Summary: Which Solution to Use?

Mechanism Best When Limitations
Formal contracts Legal system is efficient, damages > litigation costs Fails for small claims, slow justice
Reputation Many future customers, information visible Fails if company is short-lived
Relational contracts Repeat customer, long horizon Requires ongoing relationship
Escrow Clear deliverables, trusted intermediary available Costs 1-3%, needs verification

Recommendation for the startup:

Combine multiple mechanisms:

  1. Use escrow for new customers (establishes trust)
  2. Build reputation through reviews/testimonials
  3. Develop relational contracts with good customers
  4. Use formal contracts as backup enforcement

Key Assumptions:

  • Parties are rational and forward-looking
  • Information about past behavior is eventually observable
  • Legal system provides some enforcement
  • Transaction costs of mechanisms are reasonable

How conclusions change:

  • If legal system is weak → rely more on reputation and escrow
  • If customer is one-time → formal contract or escrow essential
  • If industry is small and tight-knit → reputation very powerful
  • If company has cash flow → can use back-loaded payment
▶ Question 13.3: Investment Adviser Trust Problem: American Rule and Deterrence

Question:

An investment adviser in the United States offers to help invest your money. But you are worried that the investment adviser might steal your money instead of investing it. Assume the burden of proof is so high in criminal cases that it is very unlikely that the adviser will suffer any criminal punishment for stealing the money. You can, however, sue the adviser in a US court for damages to get back your funds. Should you trust the investment adviser with your money? Which factors matter for your answer? Use economic theory and give concrete examples.

Note: In Norway the loser of the lawsuit typically pays for the winner's legal costs. This is not the case in the United States. There, each party to a lawsuit bears its own costs, independent of the outcome of the lawsuit.

▶ What a Good Answer Should Contain

A good answer should use sequential game theory (backward induction) to analyze whether the threat of a lawsuit can deter theft. Your analysis should identify the relevant actors (investor, adviser), explain the American rule's impact on litigation incentives, determine when you have credible incentives to sue after detecting theft, use numerical examples to show when the adviser will choose honesty versus theft, and identify the key factors (amount invested, litigation costs, detection probability, reputational costs) that determine whether trust is rational. A sophisticated answer will identify the non-monotonicity problem where very low or very high litigation costs can both undermine deterrence.

▶ Example solution

Baseline and Change:

Baseline: You are considering whether to trust an investment adviser with your money. The adviser can choose to (1) invest honestly or (2) steal your funds.

Sequential structure:

  1. You decide: Trust adviser with money (invest) or don't trust
  2. If you trust: Adviser decides to steal or be honest
  3. If adviser steals: You discover theft with probability p
  4. If you discover theft: You decide whether to sue

Key institutional feature: American rule means both parties pay their own litigation costs regardless of outcome.

Actors and Incentives:

Key actors:

  1. You (investor): Deciding whether to trust adviser and whether to sue if theft detected
  2. Investment adviser: Deciding whether to steal or be honest

Relevant margins:

  • Investor's trust decision: Will I get positive expected return?
  • Investor's litigation decision: Is suing better than accepting loss?
  • Adviser's honesty decision: Does expected benefit of stealing exceed expected cost?

Backward induction: Start from litigation decision, work backward to trust decision.

Economic Mechanism:

Framework: Sequential game with credible threats

Step 1: Your litigation decision (after detecting theft)

Numerical example: You invest €100,000, litigation costs €20,000 per side.

If you sue:

  • Court orders adviser to repay €100,000
  • But you pay €20,000 in legal costs
  • Net recovery: €100,000 - €20,000 = €80,000
  • Better than accepting full loss of €100,000

You sue if: Amount stolen - Litigation cost > -Amount stolen

  • €100,000 - €20,000 > -€100,000
  • €80,000 > -€100,000 ✓ True

Critical insight: Under American rule, you only sue if amount stolen exceeds litigation costs.

Step 2: Adviser's theft decision (anticipating your litigation response)

Adviser's payoff from stealing:

  • If detected (probability p): Must repay + pay own litigation costs = -€20,000
  • If not detected (probability 1-p): Keep stolen amount = +€100,000
  • Expected payoff: p × (-€20,000) + (1-p) × €100,000

Adviser's payoff from honesty: €0

Adviser steals if: p × (-€20,000) + (1-p) × €100,000 > €0 -€20,000p + €100,000 - €100,000p > €0 €100,000 - €120,000p > €0 p < €100,000/€120,000 p < 0.833

Conclusion: With 90% detection probability (p = 0.9), adviser stays honest:

  • Expected payoff from stealing: 0.9 × (-€20,000) + 0.1 × €100,000 = -€18,000 + €10,000 = -€8,000
  • This is worse than €0 from honesty → Don't steal

With 50% detection probability (p = 0.5), adviser steals:

  • Expected payoff from stealing: 0.5 × (-€20,000) + 0.5 × €100,000 = -€10,000 + €50,000 = +€40,000
  • This is better than €0 from honesty → Steal

Step 3: When litigation threat is not credible

If you invest €30,000 and litigation costs €50,000:

Your litigation decision:

  • If you sue: €30,000 - €50,000 = -€20,000
  • If you don't sue: -€30,000
  • Suing makes you worse off! You won't sue.

Adviser anticipates this:

  • Detection doesn't matter if you won't sue anyway
  • Expected payoff from stealing: €30,000 (with certainty)
  • Adviser always steals

You should not trust adviser when litigation threat is not credible.

Illustration:

Scenario A: High detection, credible litigation

  • Investment: €100,000
  • Litigation costs: €20,000 per side
  • Detection probability: 90%

Adviser's calculation:

  • Steal: 0.9 × (-€20,000) + 0.1 × €100,000 = -€8,000
  • Honest: €0
  • Adviser chooses honesty → You can trust

Scenario B: Low detection, credible litigation

  • Investment: €100,000
  • Litigation costs: €20,000 per side
  • Detection probability: 50%

Adviser's calculation:

  • Steal: 0.5 × (-€20,000) + 0.5 × €100,000 = +€40,000
  • Honest: €0
  • Adviser chooses theft → Don't trust

Scenario C: Non-credible litigation threat

  • Investment: €30,000
  • Litigation costs: €50,000 per side
  • Detection probability: 100%

Your litigation decision:

  • Sue: €30,000 - €50,000 = -€20,000
  • Accept loss: -€30,000
  • You won't sue (suing is worse)

Adviser's response:

  • Knows you won't sue
  • Always steals → Don't trust

Scenario D: Very high litigation costs

  • Investment: €100,000
  • Litigation costs: €90,000 per side
  • Detection probability: 90%

Adviser's calculation if you would sue:

  • Steal: 0.9 × (-€90,000) + 0.1 × €100,000 = -€81,000 + €10,000 = -€71,000
  • Seems to deter!

But check your litigation incentive:

  • Sue: €100,000 - €90,000 = €10,000
  • Accept loss: -€100,000
  • You would sue (€10,000 > -€100,000)

So with very high litigation costs:

  • Threat is credible (you'd sue)
  • Deterrence is strong (adviser loses -€71,000 if detected)
  • You can trust

Key factors determining trust:

Factor Effect on Trust Mechanism
Higher investment amount ✓ Increases trust (up to point) Makes litigation more worthwhile
Higher litigation costs ✗ Reduces trust (if too high) Can make threat non-credible
Higher detection probability ✓ Increases trust Increases expected cost of stealing
Reputational costs of fraud ✓ Increases trust Additional deterrent beyond litigation

Assumptions and Robustness:

Key Assumption 1: American rule applies

Under English rule (loser pays winner's costs):

  • If you sue and win: Adviser pays your litigation costs
  • Net recovery: €100,000 (full compensation)
  • Stronger litigation incentive → Broader credibility of threat
  • Adviser faces worse punishment: -(own costs + your costs)
  • Deterrence effect stronger

Conclusion: Trust more feasible under English rule.

Key Assumption 2: No reputational costs

If adviser faces reputational costs from fraud:

  • Example: Public registry of fraud, loss of future clients worth €50,000
  • Effective punishment: €20,000 (litigation) + €50,000 (reputation) = €70,000
  • Deterrence threshold: p × (-€70,000) + (1-p) × €100,000 > €0 → p < 0.588
  • Can trust with lower detection probabilities (59% instead of 83%)

If reputation matters: Trust is more feasible even with lower detection.

Key Assumption 3: One-shot game

With repeat interactions:

  • Building trust over small transactions first
  • Reputation effects compound
  • Tit-for-tat strategies available
  • Trust can emerge even with weak legal enforcement

Key Assumption 4: Perfect detection once you investigate

With imperfect detection:

  • Even if you investigate (pay to search for evidence), might not find proof
  • Let q = probability of finding proof conditional on theft
  • Effective detection: p × q
  • Need higher base detection rate for same deterrence

Key Assumption 5: Risk neutrality

If adviser is risk-averse:

  • Dislikes uncertainty in payoffs
  • 90% chance of -€20,000 more painful than expected value suggests
  • Deterrence stronger than calculation shows
  • Can trust with lower detection rates

If adviser is risk-loving:

  • Attracted to gamble aspect
  • Deterrence weaker
  • Need higher detection for trust

Conditional Conclusion:

You can trust the investment adviser when:

  1. Litigation threat is credible
    • Amount invested > Litigation costs
    • Otherwise you won't sue even if you detect theft
    • Adviser knows this and will steal
  2. Detection probability is sufficiently high
    • Threshold depends on: p > (Amount stolen)/(Amount stolen + Litigation cost)
    • Example: p > €100,000/€120,000 ≈ 83%
    • Higher amounts require higher detection for same litigation cost
  3. Reputational mechanisms reinforce legal deterrence
    • Public information about past fraud
    • Future business depends on reputation
    • Industry networks punish fraud
  4. Fee structure aligns incentives
    • Assets under management fees better than performance fees for honesty
    • Custody separation (adviser doesn't hold assets directly)

Non-monotonicity problem:

Trust is NOT monotonic in litigation costs:

  • Very low costs (€1,000): Threat credible but weak deterrent → Need very high detection
  • Moderate costs (€20,000): Threat credible with reasonable deterrent → Trust possible with 83% detection
  • Very high costs (€90,000): Threat credible with strong deterrent → Trust possible with 59% detection
  • Too high costs (€110,000): Threat not credible → No trust possible

Optimal litigation cost for trust: Neither too low nor too high.

Comparative institutional analysis:

Legal System Fee Rule Credibility Region Deterrence Strength Trust Feasibility
United States American rule Narrower Weaker Lower
Norway English rule Broader Stronger Higher
No legal system Reputation only N/A Depends on industry Varies widely

When you should trust:

  • High detection technology (monitoring, audits)
  • English rule jurisdiction (if possible)
  • Strong reputational mechanisms (transparent industry)
  • Repeat interaction expected
  • Third-party custody of assets
  • Amount invested >> litigation costs

When you should not trust:

  • Low detection probability
  • Amount invested ≈ litigation costs (non-credible threat)
  • Opaque industry (no reputational punishment)
  • One-shot interaction
  • No criminal prosecution possible

The investment adviser problem illustrates fundamental limits of legal deterrence: Law can only deter when legal threats are credible, and under American rule, credibility requires sufficient stake size relative to litigation costs.

Limits of private ordering

Foundations used:

  • Cost–Benefit Analysis
  • Transaction Costs
  • Information, Evidence, and Verifiability

Learning goal: identify when private agreements fail to produce efficient outcomes and assess when law should facilitate or constrain private ordering.

Types of Questions You Should Be Able to Answer

  • How can legal rules facilitate efficient private agreements?
  • When and how should law intervene in private contracting?

Enforceable contracts allow parties to solve commitment problems and cooperate over time. When contracting is cheap and effective, voluntary agreement is a powerful mechanism for creating value and allocating risk.

However, private ordering does not always work. In many settings, transaction costs, information problems, or institutional constraints prevent contracts from forming or from reliably reflecting efficient incentives. In such cases, private consent alone is not a sufficient guide to welfare.

This chapter examines when and why private contracting fails, and how contract law responds to these failures. The central organizing principle is transaction costs. When transaction costs prevent efficient agreements from forming, the law plays a facilitative role by supporting private ordering. When contracts exist but their terms cannot be trusted to reflect social costs and benefits, the law plays a corrective role by shaping or constraining contractual outcomes.

The Facilitative Role of Contract Law

As discussed in the transaction costs chapter, private bargaining can sustain efficient outcomes only when the costs of identifying trading partners, negotiating terms, and enforcing agreements are sufficiently low. When these costs are low, voluntary contracts are a reliable mechanism for enabling cooperation and creating value.

In such cases, the primary role of the law is facilitative: to make efficient contracting possible when transaction costs make mutually beneficial agreements too costly to create or enforce without legal support.

A common reason efficient contracts fail to exist, or exist only in an incomplete and fragile form, is high transaction costs. Negotiating terms, drafting detailed agreements, monitoring performance, and enforcing obligations all require time and resources. Anticipating and specifying all relevant future contingencies may be impossible or prohibitively expensive.

When transaction costs are high, parties may under-contract, rely on vague or informal arrangements, or avoid mutually beneficial transactions altogether. The problem in these cases is not that private contracts are undesirable, but that efficient contracts are too costly to create or enforce given the available institutions.

Contract law can improve outcomes by reducing these costs and making commitments credible. Default rules, standardized terms, and judicial gap-filling allow parties to economize on negotiation and drafting by supplying terms they would likely have chosen themselves if contracting were cheaper. Predictable enforcement is especially important: parties must be able to anticipate the consequences of breach with reasonable certainty. Unpredictable or highly discretionary enforcement undermines the credibility of commitments and discourages efficient investment.

In this facilitative role, contract law does not design outcomes or redistribute surplus. Its function is to remove impediments to private agreement and to support private ordering. This idea is often summarized as a Normative Coasean design principle: to promote efficiency, legal rules should be structured so as to reduce the costs of contracting and enforcement, thereby allowing mutually beneficial agreements to occur.

When Should Law Intervene in Private Contracts?

Private contracts are generally respected because voluntary agreement is a powerful mechanism for aligning incentives and creating surplus. However, private agreement is not always a reliable guide to efficient outcomes. Even when contracts are formally voluntary and legally enforceable, institutional constraints may prevent private bargaining from internalizing all relevant costs and incentives.

The central question is therefore not whether parties consented, but whether consent reliably reflects surplus creation. When it does not, legal intervention can improve welfare by reshaping incentives or constraining contractual outcomes. The following subsections identify the main situations in which this occurs.

Information and Incentive Problems

Contracts often rely on information that one party cannot observe or verify. When quality, risk, or effort is privately known, contractual terms may be based on distorted beliefs rather than accurate assessments of costs and benefits. Even when agreement is voluntary, incentives may fail to induce efficient behavior if the informed party cannot be effectively disciplined through contracting.

These problems are especially acute when performance is difficult to verify or when actions are taken after the contract is signed. In such cases, private contracts may under-incentivize precaution, quality, or effort, or may encourage excessive risk-taking.

Legal intervention can respond to these failures by improving information flows or by directly reshaping incentives. Disclosure requirements, warranties, liability rules, and mandatory standards can reduce information asymmetries or substitute for missing contractual discipline. The economic justification for these interventions is not paternalism, but the need to align private incentives with the underlying risks and costs when information is imperfect or unverifiable.

Missing Affected Parties

Private contracts bind only those who are party to the agreement. When contractual performance imposes costs or risks on others who are not represented at the bargaining table, private consent is not sufficient to ensure efficiency.

The core problem in these cases is not bargaining failure among the contracting parties, but the absence of affected parties whose interests are not internalized. Even perfectly informed and voluntary agreements may lead to inefficient outcomes when third-party harms are significant.

Legal intervention can address these failures by expanding the scope of responsibility beyond the contracting parties. Liability to third parties, restrictions on certain agreements, or regulatory constraints on contract terms can force contracting parties to take account of costs they would otherwise ignore. In economic terms, these interventions aim to internalize external effects that private contracting alone cannot capture.

Distorted Choice Environments

In some settings, private agreement does not reliably reflect efficient outcomes because choice itself is distorted. The problem is not lack of enforcement or hidden information, but the absence of a meaningful alternative.

At the extreme, a contract signed under a credible threat of violence—for example, when one party is forced to sign at gunpoint—provides no reliable information about surplus creation. A signature exists, but consent is meaningless: the fact that the person signed does not tell us whether the terms are mutually beneficial. The key point is not that the terms must be inefficient, but that agreement is no longer evidence of efficiency.

Real-world cases are rarely this extreme, but similar logic applies whenever one party faces severe constraints on exit or switching. Take-it-or-leave-it contracts, lock-in, high switching costs, or urgent necessity can all weaken the informational content of consent. In these situations, agreement may reflect the lack of alternatives rather than genuine mutual gains from the specific terms.

The economic concern here is not inequality as such. Unequal bargaining power does not by itself imply inefficiency. The problem arises when constraints on choice prevent private agreement from serving as a reliable signal of surplus creation. When this occurs, legal doctrines such as mandatory terms, limits on enforcement, or unconscionability can improve outcomes by restoring the link between consent and efficient incentives.

Summary: The Limits of Private Ordering

Private contracts are a powerful mechanism for creating value because voluntary agreement often aligns incentives and internalizes costs. When contracting is cheap and effective, private ordering can sustain efficient outcomes with minimal legal intervention.

However, private ordering has limits. When transaction costs prevent efficient contracts from forming, the appropriate role of contract law is facilitative: to reduce the costs of contracting and enforcement so that mutually beneficial agreements can occur. When contracts exist but private agreement fails to internalize all relevant costs or incentives—due to information problems, missing affected parties, or distorted choice environments—private consent is no longer a reliable guide to efficiency.

In such cases, legal intervention may improve welfare by reshaping incentives or constraining contractual outcomes. The central question is therefore not whether parties consented, but whether private agreement reliably reflects surplus creation. Where it does, freedom of contract should prevail. Where it does not, carefully designed legal intervention can enhance efficiency by restoring the link between private incentives and social costs and benefits.

Exam Questions

▶ Question 14.1: Home Insurance: Should It Be Mandatory?

Case Background:

The company TryggBolig AS offers home contents insurance to private individuals. In recent years, more customers have chosen to go without insurance, while the number of large damage payouts has increased. The industry also reports increasing insurance fraud, particularly in claimed thefts and fire damage. Some customers have been denied coverage because the company believes the damages are insufficiently documented.

TryggBolig AS management is now considering various measures to reduce fraud, strengthen profitability, and ensure trust in the company. At the same time, authorities are considering making home insurance mandatory for all households.

You are employed as an economic advisor and are asked to provide a professional assessment of the situation.

Question:

An increasing number of households are without home insurance. The authorities are therefore considering introducing a requirement for mandatory insurance for all. The proposal has created political debate: Parties on the right warn against government intervention in a private market, while parties on the left believe such an arrangement is necessary to ensure social security. Discuss the proposal using economic theory.

▶ What a Good Answer Should Contain

[TO BE COMPLETED]

▶ Example solution

[SOLUTION TO BE COMPLETED]

The solution should discuss:

  • Coase theorem and when private markets achieve efficiency
  • Adverse selection problem (high-risk individuals buy insurance, low-risk opt out)
  • Whether mandatory insurance solves market failure or creates inefficiency
  • Transaction costs and information asymmetries
  • Distributional vs efficiency considerations

Partial notes from source:

  • Apply Coase theorem: voluntary agreements lead to Pareto improvements when (i) voluntary, (ii) both parties rational and informed, (iii) no third party harmed
  • High transaction costs: Those who want insurance are high risk, so insurers must charge high premiums. Low-risk customers go without.
  • Consider whether market failure justifies intervention
▶ Question 14.2: BankID Fraud: When is Government Regulation Justified?

Case Background:

The number of cases involving BankID fraud has increased sharply in recent years. Scammers often pose as representatives of a bank or the police and manage to persuade victims to disclose passwords or approve logins to their bank accounts. The money is transferred from the victims' accounts and then moved onward in ways that make it difficult to trace or recover.

In practice, it has been unclear who should bear the loss resulting from such fraud. Banks argue that customers themselves must take responsibility if their BankID is misused. They point out that the system is based on personal responsibility, and that increased bank liability would make digital services more expensive and less user-friendly. Consumer organizations, on the other hand, argue that banks should bear the main responsibility, since they are better positioned to detect and stop fraud, and because ordinary customers cannot reasonably be expected to protect themselves against increasingly sophisticated methods.

Question:

The government is considering proposing new rules that give banks greater responsibility in cases of BankID fraud. Banks argue that the authorities should not intervene and regulate this area. They claim that such interventions will only make digital banking services more expensive for customers, and that banks themselves are best positioned to find the most effective solutions. Discuss, using economic theory, under which conditions public regulation may nevertheless be justified.

▶ What a Good Answer Should Contain

A good answer should use the Coase theorem as the analytical framework. The theorem states that if property rights are well-defined, transaction costs are low, and parties can bargain freely, private agreements will lead to efficient outcomes without government intervention. Your analysis should explain when these conditions hold (freedom of contract leads to efficiency), identify specific market failures that break the Coase theorem assumptions (information asymmetry preventing customers from distinguishing bank quality, transaction costs making individual contracts infeasible, adverse selection in protection markets, externalities to third parties like systemic trust), and use examples to show how regulation can improve efficiency when Coase conditions fail. The key insight is that regulation is justified precisely when voluntary private agreements cannot achieve efficient risk allocation due to market failures.

▶ Example solution

Baseline and Change:

Baseline: Private contracts between banks and customers allocate BankID fraud risk.

Proposed change: Government mandates greater bank responsibility through regulation.

Bank argument against regulation:

  • Private contracts are efficient (Coase theorem)
  • Regulation only increases costs
  • Banks know best how to allocate risk

Question: When can regulation improve on private contracts?

Actors and Incentives:

Key actors:

  1. Banks: Choose security investments and contract terms
  2. Customers: Choose banks and precaution levels
  3. Government: Decides whether to regulate liability allocation
  4. Third parties: Other banks, payment system users affected by trust

Relevant margins:

  • Banks: How much to invest in fraud detection vs. passing costs to customers
  • Customers: Which bank to choose based on fraud protection
  • Market: Whether information flows allow efficient sorting

Economic Mechanism:

Framework: Coase Theorem

Coase Theorem states: If (1) property rights are well-defined and transferable, (2) transaction costs are zero, and (3) parties can bargain freely, then private negotiations lead to Pareto-efficient outcomes regardless of initial rights allocation.

Alternative formulation: Private agreements lead to Pareto improvements when:

  1. Agreements are entered voluntarily
  2. Both parties are rational and understand the agreement
  3. No third party is harmed

Implication for BankID fraud: If Coase conditions hold, banks and customers will negotiate efficient fraud liability contracts without government intervention. Regulation is unnecessary and potentially harmful.

When regulation is justified: When Coase theorem conditions fail - market failures prevent efficient private contracting.

Market Failure 1: Information Asymmetry

Problem: Customers cannot distinguish between banks with different security levels.

Mechanism:

Assume two types of banks:

  • High-security banks: Invest 1,000 per customer in fraud detection, fraud rate 0.1%
  • Low-security banks: Invest 100 per customer in fraud detection, fraud rate 1%

Average customer loss from fraud: 100,000 per incident

Expected fraud cost:

  • High-security: 0.001 × 100,000 = 100
  • Low-security: 0.01 × 100,000 = 1,000

Socially efficient: Choose high-security if customer values fraud reduction at more than 900 (= 1,000 investment - 100 investment).

If customers can observe security levels:

  • High-security banks charge 100 more (reflecting investment)
  • Customers willing to pay 900 for 0.9% fraud reduction
  • Market provides efficient sorting
  • No regulation needed

If customers cannot observe security levels:

  • All banks look identical to customers
  • Customers not willing to pay premium for security they can't verify
  • High-security banks can't recoup investment
  • All banks choose low security (race to bottom)
  • Market failure: Socially valuable security not provided

Numerical example:

50% of customers value fraud reduction at 1,200 (willing to pay for high-security). 50% of customers value fraud reduction at 600 (prefer low-cost, low-security).

With observable quality:

  • High-security banks charge premium, serve high-value customers
  • Low-security banks charge less, serve low-value customers
  • Efficient sorting

Without observable quality:

  • High-security banks can't prove superiority
  • Customers assume average quality
  • Willing to pay based on average security (not high security)
  • High-security banks exit market (can't cover costs)
  • Only low-security banks remain
  • Adverse selection drives out good banks

Regulation response:

  • Mandate minimum security standards
  • All banks must provide high security
  • Corrects information problem
  • Efficiency improved

Market Failure 2: High Transaction Costs and Adverse Selection

Problem: Individual contracts are theoretically efficient but practically infeasible.

Coase theorem assumes: Banks can negotiate customized contracts with each customer.

Efficient individual contracts would vary by customer:

  • Young, tech-savvy customer: Low fraud risk, low protection cost → Customer bears liability
  • Elderly, less digital customer: High fraud risk, high protection cost → Bank bears liability

Why individual contracts fail:

Asymmetric information about customer type:

Assume customers know their own fraud risk, banks don't.

If bank offers two contracts:

  • Contract A: No fraud protection, low fee
  • Contract B: Full fraud protection, high fee

Adverse selection:

  • High-risk customers choose Contract B
  • Low-risk customers choose Contract A
  • Bank must price Contract B based on high-risk pool
  • Price becomes very high
  • Medium-risk customers (who would benefit from protection) opt out
  • Inefficient pooling or unraveling

Numerical example:

Customer types:

  • Low-risk (30%): Fraud probability 0.1%, protection cost 200
  • Medium-risk (50%): Fraud probability 0.5%, protection cost 500
  • High-risk (20%): Fraud probability 2%, protection cost 1,500

Expected fraud loss if unprotected: 100,000 per incident

Efficient allocation:

  • Low-risk: Should bear own risk (expected loss 100 < cost 200)
  • Medium-risk: Should buy protection (expected loss 500 = cost 500, risk aversion tips toward protection)
  • High-risk: Should buy protection (expected loss 2,000 >> cost 1,500)

If bank offers protection contract:

  • Only high-risk customers buy → Price must be 1,500+
  • Medium-risk customers see price 1,500, opt out (even though protection worth 500 to them)
  • Adverse selection destroys market for medium-risk protection

Regulation response:

  • Mandate bank liability for all customers
  • Eliminates adverse selection problem
  • Medium-risk customers get efficient protection through mandatory scheme

Transaction costs:

  • Negotiating individual contracts costs 500 per customer
  • Only economical for large accounts
  • Small account holders excluded from efficient contracting
  • Standard regulation more cost-effective than individual negotiation

Market Failure 3: Externalities to Third Parties

Problem: Individual bank's fraud policy affects systemic trust.

Mechanism:

When Bank A allows high fraud losses on its customers:

  • Customers lose trust in digital banking generally (not just Bank A)
  • Reduces willingness to use digital payments at Bank B, C, D
  • Network externality: Value of digital payment system depends on user trust
  • Bank A doesn't internalize cost to other banks

Numerical example:

Bank A saves 10 million by not investing in fraud protection. Resulting fraud losses at Bank A: 20 million.

If only Bank A customers affected:

  • Net social cost: 20M - 10M = 10M
  • Bank A internalizes this (loses customers)
  • Market discipline works

If fraud undermines system-wide trust:

  • Reduced digital banking usage across all banks
  • Lost consumer surplus: 50 million
  • Bank A saves 10M but society loses 50M
  • Negative externality not internalized

Regulation response:

  • Mandate minimum fraud protection across all banks
  • Maintains system-wide trust
  • Internalizes externality

Market Failure 4: Rational Ignorance and Bounded Rationality

Problem: Customers don't understand contracts or fraud risks.

Why customers can't evaluate contracts:

  • Complex terms and conditions (50+ pages)
  • Probabilistic risks difficult to assess
  • Optimism bias (underestimate own fraud risk)
  • Present bias (discount future fraud possibility)

Evidence of bounded rationality:

  • Customers don't read terms before signing
  • Overestimate ability to detect scams
  • Underestimate sophistication of modern fraud

If customers rational and informed:

  • Would demand appropriate fraud protection
  • Willingness to pay reflects true risk
  • Market provides efficient contracts

If customers boundedly rational:

  • Systematically undervalue fraud protection
  • Choose inadequate coverage
  • Ex-post regret after fraud occurs
  • Market provides inefficiently low protection

Paternalistic regulation:

  • Mandate minimum protection
  • Corrects for systematic cognitive biases
  • Improves ex-ante welfare (if correctly designed)

Illustration:

Comprehensive Example: Three Scenarios

Scenario 1: Coase Theorem Holds - No Regulation Needed

Assumptions:

  • Customers can observe bank security levels (ratings published)
  • Low transaction costs (standardized contracts)
  • No externalities
  • Rational customers

Market outcome:

  • High-security banks charge premium, attract risk-averse customers
  • Low-security banks charge less, attract risk-tolerant customers
  • Efficient sorting and risk allocation
  • No government intervention needed

Scenario 2: Information Asymmetry - Regulation Improves Efficiency

Assumptions:

  • Customers cannot observe bank security investments
  • Banks choose between high security (cost 1,000) and low security (cost 100)
  • High security reduces fraud from 1% to 0.1%

Without regulation:

  • All banks choose low security (can't recoup high security investment)
  • Total cost per 100 customers: 100 × 100 + 100 × 100,000 × 0.01 = 10,000 + 100,000 = 110,000

With regulation mandating high security:

  • All banks invest in high security
  • Total cost: 100 × 1,000 + 100 × 100,000 × 0.001 = 100,000 + 10,000 = 110,000

Wait, same cost! Let me recalculate assuming customer value…

Actually, let me reconsider. With high security:

  • Cost: 1,000 per customer investment
  • Fraud: 0.001 × 100,000 = 100 per customer
  • Total: 1,100 per customer

With low security:

  • Cost: 100 per customer investment
  • Fraud: 0.01 × 100,000 = 1,000 per customer
  • Total: 1,100 per customer

These are equal, so need different numbers. Let me adjust:

High security: Investment 800, fraud rate 0.1%, expected fraud cost 100, total 900 Low security: Investment 100, fraud rate 1%, expected fraud cost 1,000, total 1,100

Without regulation: All banks choose low security (can't differentiate), total cost 1,100 per customer With regulation: Mandate high security, total cost 900 per customer Efficiency gain: 200 per customer

Scenario 3: Transaction Costs - Regulation as Coordination Device

Assumptions:

  • Efficient contracts vary by customer type (3 types as above)
  • Individual negotiation costs 500 per customer
  • Average account balance 50,000

With individual contracts:

  • Benefit from customization: 100 per customer (better risk allocation)
  • Cost of negotiation: 500 per customer
  • Net: -400 per customer
  • Not worthwhile

With standard regulation:

  • One-size-fits-all: Bank liability for all
  • Loses 100 in customization benefit
  • Saves 500 in negotiation cost
  • Net: +400 per customer
  • Worthwhile despite imperfect fit

Assumptions and Robustness:

Key Assumption 1: Information asymmetry exists

If customers can observe bank security quality:

  • Market provides efficient sorting
  • No adverse selection
  • Regulation unnecessary (may reduce efficiency by preventing customization)

Robustness: In practice, security quality very difficult for customers to observe. Information problem likely significant.

Key Assumption 2: Transaction costs are high

If individual contracts have low transaction costs:

  • Coase theorem applies
  • Customized agreements efficient
  • Standard regulation inferior to tailored contracts

Robustness: With millions of customers, individual negotiation impractical. Standardization efficient despite heterogeneity.

Key Assumption 3: Externalities matter

If fraud only affects individual bank-customer pairs:

  • No systemic trust issues
  • Market discipline sufficient
  • No externality justification for regulation

Robustness: Digital payment systems exhibit network effects. Trust is public good. Externalities likely present.

Key Assumption 4: Government can design better rules than market

If regulators have worse information than market:

  • Regulatory mistake > Market failure
  • Regulation reduces efficiency

Robustness: Need to compare:

  • Cost of market failure (information asymmetry, adverse selection)
  • Cost of regulatory error (one-size-fits-all inefficiency)

Only regulate if: Market failure cost > Regulatory error cost

Key Assumption 5: Regulation doesn't have unintended consequences

If bank liability mandate causes:

  • Banks to exit market
  • Digital banking less available
  • Innovation reduced
  • Higher costs passed to all customers

Then regulation may reduce welfare despite correcting market failure.

Conditional Conclusion:

Public regulation of BankID fraud liability is justified when:

1. Information asymmetry prevents market from functioning

  • Customers cannot distinguish high-security from low-security banks
  • Leads to adverse selection and race to bottom
  • Mandating minimum standards corrects information problem

2. Transaction costs make individual contracts infeasible

  • Negotiating customized liability terms costs more than benefit from customization
  • Standard regulation more cost-effective
  • Particularly relevant for small account holders

3. Externalities to third parties

  • Individual bank's fraud policy affects system-wide trust in digital payments
  • Banks don't internalize effect on payment system network
  • Regulation needed to maintain systemic confidence

4. Bounded rationality leads to systematic under-protection

  • Customers systematically underestimate fraud risk
  • Present bias and optimism bias
  • Paternalistic regulation corrects for cognitive failures (if designed well)

Public regulation is NOT justified when:

1. Coase conditions hold

  • Customers can observe and evaluate bank security
  • Transaction costs low (standardized contracts work)
  • No externalities
  • Rational, informed customers
  • Market provides efficient outcome

2. Regulatory costs exceed market failure costs

  • One-size-fits-all regulation destroys valuable customization
  • Compliance costs high
  • Innovation reduced
  • Government has poor information for designing rules

3. Private solutions emerging

  • Industry standards and certification
  • Reputation mechanisms
  • Third-party rating agencies
  • Private governance may be more efficient than regulation

Key factors determining whether regulation justified:

Factor Favors Regulation Favors Market
Customer information Poor (can't observe quality) Good (can distinguish banks)
Transaction costs High (individual contracts expensive) Low (standardized contracts work)
Externalities Large (systemic trust) Small (isolated pairs)
Customer sophistication Low (bounded rationality) High (rational evaluation)
Heterogeneity Low (similar customers) High (diverse needs)
Regulatory capacity High (good rule design) Low (poor information)

The economic logic: Markets work efficiently when Coase conditions hold - well-defined property rights, low transaction costs, no externalities, and informed rational actors. When these conditions fail, private contracts cannot achieve efficient outcomes, creating potential role for government regulation. However, regulation itself has costs (inflexibility, compliance burden, potential errors), so intervention is only justified when market failures are substantial enough to outweigh regulatory costs. The BankID fraud case exhibits multiple market failures (information asymmetry, transaction costs, externalities, bounded rationality), making it a strong candidate for regulatory intervention despite banks' arguments for freedom of contract.

Designing Good Contracts

Foundations used:

  • Cost–Benefit Analysis
  • Transaction Costs
  • Information, Evidence, and Verifiability
  • Risk Aversion

Learning goal: apply cost–benefit analysis to the design and interpretation of contractual clauses under real-world constraints.

Types of Questions You Should Be Able to Answer

  • Should clause X be included in the contract?
  • How should an incomplete contract be interpreted by courts?

🎯 Activation Question

Test your understanding before proceeding

In cost–benefit analysis, which costs and benefits should be included when evaluating whether an action increases total surplus?

Many of the most important economic transactions in modern economies are governed by complex contracts. Mergers and acquisitions, large construction and infrastructure projects, shipping and energy contracts, and long-term supply agreements often involve large investments, multiple parties, and significant uncertainty about future events. Small contractual details can have large economic consequences.

Many students reading this book will find themselves working on such contracts, whether as managers, advisors, consultants, lawyers, or regulators. Even when contracts are drafted by legal specialists, business decisions about risk allocation, incentives, and enforcement play a central role in shaping their content. Understanding contracts therefore requires more than knowing legal doctrine; it requires understanding how contractual terms affect behavior and outcomes.

These contracts often run to hundreds of pages and include detailed provisions on liability, payment, termination, delay, force majeure, warranties, and dispute resolution. Law and economics provides a way to cut through this complexity. By focusing on incentives, information, and risk, it offers a simple framework for thinking systematically about why contracts are written the way they are and how they can be improved.

This chapter uses economic reasoning to analyze how contractual clauses allocate risk, create incentives, and deal with uncertainty. The goal is to evaluate contract terms using economic efficiency as a benchmark, while accounting for incentive, information, and enforcement constraints. The aim is not to teach contract drafting, but to provide a general method for assessing whether contractual clauses are likely to work well in practice.

Once you understand the logic developed in this chapter, you should be able to analyze almost any contractual clause using economic theory and assess whether it is likely to work well in practice. This is also how contract problems are assessed in this course: on the exam, you are expected to analyze an unfamiliar contract clause using the analytical method developed here.

🎯 Activation Question

Test your understanding before proceeding

A construction contract for a road project includes a clause: "The contractor must pay €50,000 for each day of delay beyond the agreed completion date." Should this clause be included in the contract?

What Does It Mean to Design a "Good" Contract?

From a legal perspective, a contract is often understood as a document that specifies rights, obligations, and remedies in the event of breach. From a law and economics perspective, a contract is something more: it is a device for shaping behavior.

The central question in contract design is not only what happens if something goes wrong, but how the contract affects behavior before anything goes wrong. Well-designed contracts encourage the parties to take desirable actions ex ante, such as effort, quality, timing, and information revelation.

Efficiency as the Guiding Principle of Contract Design

To evaluate whether a contractual clause is good or bad, we need a clear normative benchmark. In this chapter, that benchmark is economic efficiency: does the contract maximize total surplus?

Total surplus is the sum of all benefits generated by the contractual relationship minus all real resource costs. A contractual clause is efficient if it increases total surplus, even if it makes one party worse off before prices, fees, or other transfers are adjusted.

A key implication of this perspective is that efficiency and distribution can be analyzed separately. Transfers between the parties affect who gets what, but they do not affect whether a clause is socially desirable. As long as a clause increases total surplus, the parties can in principle adjust prices or other terms to divide the gains from trade.

This separation allows us to focus on the content of contracts—what clauses should be included—without taking a position on bargaining power, fairness, or legal entitlements. Those considerations matter for distribution, not for efficiency.

Efficiency therefore provides the benchmark for evaluating contractual clauses. The next step is to develop a practical method for determining whether a specific clause increases total surplus.

Cost–Benefit Analysis of Contract Clauses

Once efficiency is established as the normative benchmark, the next question is how to evaluate whether a specific contractual clause is efficient. The central analytical tool is cost–benefit analysis (CBA).

In contract design, the purpose of cost–benefit analysis is not to decide how much one party should pay the other. Instead, it is to determine whether a clause should be included at all—namely, whether it increases total surplus.

Contractual clauses create value only by changing behavior. They do not generate surplus directly, but by inducing one or both parties to act differently. Cost–benefit analysis makes this logic explicit by tracing how a clause affects real resource costs and benefits through its incentive effects.

At a high level, a cost–benefit analysis of a contractual clause asks:

  1. How does the clause change behavior?
  2. What real resource costs does this behavioral change create?
  3. What real resource benefits does it create?

A clause is efficient if the benefits from the induced behavioral changes exceed the associated costs.

Pure transfers between the parties should be ignored. If a clause merely shifts money from one party to the other, this affects distribution but not efficiency.

Finally, cost–benefit analysis must be applied to how a clause works in practice. Incentive effects depend on information, observability, and enforcement. The relevant question is therefore not whether a clause would be efficient in an idealized setting with perfect information and enforcement, but whether it increases total surplus given the institutional constraints under which it operates.

Efficient Allocation of Tasks and Risks

A central problem in contract design is how to allocate tasks, risks, and responsibilities between contracting parties. Different allocations can lead to very different real resource costs, even when contracts are clear and enforceable.

From an economic perspective, efficient contract design assigns tasks and risks to the party that can carry them out or bear them at the lowest total cost. Throughout this book, we refer to this idea as the efficient allocation principle.

For practical purposes, the principle can be stated as a simple question:

Who can reduce the relevant cost—through lower direct expense, better control, or improved behavior—at the lowest total cost?

This principle applies both to productive tasks and to the management of risks. When risks are controllable, efficiency favors assigning responsibility to the party best placed to influence outcomes. When risks are uncontrollable, contracts may trade off incentives against risk bearing, leading to partial rather than full allocation.

The efficient allocation of tasks and risks provides a unifying lens for analyzing many contractual clauses. The specific mechanisms through which contracts implement this principle are examined in the examples and applications that follow.

Asymmetric Information

The analysis so far has assumed that relevant information about costs, risks, and behavior is effectively shared between the parties. In many contracts, however, one party knows more than the other—either before the contract is signed or after it is performed.

Asymmetric information does not prevent contracting, but it creates incentive problems that can distort behavior and reduce total surplus. The two canonical cases are adverse selection (private information before contracting) and moral hazard (hidden actions after contracting).

From a law and economics perspective, contractual clauses such as warranties, guarantees, disclosure obligations, and cost-sharing rules are best understood as responses to these problems. Their purpose is not to eliminate informational asymmetries, but to manage their consequences.

As elsewhere in this chapter, the relevant question is whether such clauses increase total surplus. The answer depends on a cost–benefit analysis of how they change behavior: improved incentives or information revelation must be weighed against costs such as weaker effort incentives, distorted choices, or reduced insurance.

Efficient contracts therefore often rely on partial rather than full insurance, or on imperfect screening and disclosure mechanisms. The specific tools used, and the trade-offs they involve, are examined in the examples that follow.

Efficient contracts do not eliminate asymmetric information; they manage its consequences by using clauses whose incentive benefits outweigh their incentive costs.

Verifiability and Enforcement

Efficiency provides a benchmark for what parties would like to achieve through a contract. Whether those goals can be achieved in practice, however, depends on limits of verifiability and enforcement.

A contractual clause can condition rights, obligations, or remedies only on facts that a court can verify using admissible evidence. Incentive effects therefore depend not only on what the parties observe, but on what can be proven ex post.

Many economically relevant variables—such as effort, care, diligence, or intent—are observable to the parties but difficult to verify in court. Anticipating this, contracts often avoid conditioning directly on such judgments.

Instead, contracts rely on verifiable proxies: delivery dates, quantities, documented procedures, certifications, test results, or other measurable milestones. These proxies are imperfect, but enforceable.

From an economic perspective, efficient contract design therefore trades off precision against enforceability. A coarse but verifiable clause may dominate a more precise clause that cannot be reliably enforced.

The role of verifiability is not to change the efficiency benchmark, but to constrain which incentive schemes can be implemented in practice. These constraints play a central role in evaluating contractual clauses and are examined further in the examples that follow.

Efficient contract design trades off precision against enforceability, using verifiable proxies when direct conditioning on behavior or intent is not feasible.

A General Recipe for Analyzing Contract Clauses

Most contract-law questions ask whether a particular clause should be included. A good answer follows a simple comparative logic.

  1. Clause and counterfactual: State the clause under consideration and the relevant counterfactual contractual arrangement it is compared to (the baseline).
  2. Key assumptions: State the key assumptions that are relevant for the analysis.
  3. Incentive and behavioral effects: Explain how behavior differs between the clause and the baseline (including whether a contract is made), under the stated assumptions.
  4. Welfare effects: Compare total surplus with and without the clause, focusing on real resource costs and benefits rather than transfers.
  5. Verifiability: Assess whether the clause can work in practice given what courts can verify.
  6. Conditional conclusion: Conclude whether the clause increases total surplus relative to the baseline, and under which assumptions this result holds. If the clause increases total surplus, it should be included, since the contract price or other transfers can in principle be adjusted to make both parties better off.

This structure is intentionally generic and reused verbatim in example solutions.

Breach, Remedies, and Efficient Performance

Contracts specify not only what must be done, but also what happens if it is not done. From a law and economics perspective, remedies matter because they shape incentives before performance decisions are made.

The central question is whether remedies induce efficient performance decisions.

Performance is efficient when the value created exceeds the cost. Breach is efficient when performance would destroy value. An efficient remedy structure should therefore induce parties to perform when performance is efficient and to breach when performance is inefficient.

The standard remedy in contract law is expectation damages, which require the breaching party to compensate the promisee for the loss from non-performance. Economically, this forces the promisor to compare the cost of performance with the cost of breach, leading to the logic of efficient breach.

From this perspective, remedies are part of contract design: they specify the cost of non-performance and determine whether parties internalize the consequences of breach.

Efficient remedies induce performance when it is efficient and breach when it avoids waste.

Interpreting Incomplete Contracts

Even carefully designed contracts are incomplete. It is often too costly or impossible for the parties to specify in advance how every possible contingency should be handled. As a result, courts are frequently asked to interpret contracts that contain gaps, ambiguities, or internal inconsistencies.

From a law and economics perspective, the central question is how contracts should be interpreted in order to promote efficient behavior ex ante.

Contracts may be incomplete for many reasons—uncertainty about future events, high drafting and negotiation costs, limited foresight, or strategic ambiguity. Importantly, incompleteness does not imply failure. Leaving issues unresolved can itself be an efficient choice.

The economic approach to contract interpretation starts from a simple premise: rational parties would like their contract to be efficient. This leads to the following interpretive principle:

When a contract is incomplete, courts should impute the term that the parties would have agreed to ex ante if they had bargained over the issue.

This principle focuses on efficiency rather than fairness or bargaining power. The court asks which allocation of risk or responsibility would minimize total expected costs, given the parties’ positions and available information.

A crucial insight is that judicial interpretation affects incentives before disputes arise. If parties can predict how courts will fill gaps or resolve ambiguities, they will adjust their behavior accordingly. In this sense, contract interpretation is itself a form of contract design.

Interpreting contracts in an efficiency-oriented way therefore serves two functions: it resolves the dispute at hand and shapes incentives in future contractual relationships.

Judicial interpretation that promotes efficiency today improves contractual behavior tomorrow.

Assumptions and Robustness

Risk neutrality. The baseline analysis in this chapter assumes parties are risk neutral (evaluate outcomes based on expected values). When parties are risk averse, efficient contracts trade off incentives against risk-bearing costs. This leads to partial risk allocation rather than full assignment: the party best able to control a risk bears some but not all of it, balancing improved incentives against insurance costs.

When parties are risk averse, insurance is a natural response to contractual risk. By reducing the downside borne by the insured party, insurance lowers risk-bearing costs. However, insurance also weakens incentives to reduce the probability or severity of harm.

For this reason, efficient arrangements rarely rely on full insurance. Instead, insurance contracts typically include:

  • Deductibles (insured bears first €X of loss)
  • Co-insurance (insured bears Y% of loss above deductible)
  • Exclusions (certain risks not covered)
  • Monitoring requirements (insurer can inspect or audit)

These features preserve some exposure to loss, maintaining incentives for care while reducing risk-bearing costs. This illustrates how the incentive–insurance tradeoff created by risk aversion is managed in practice.

Worked Examples

Consider the purchase of a desk sold by a furniture retailer. The seller's standard offer is a fully assembled desk delivered to the buyer at a price of 3000. Under this arrangement, the seller assembles the desk before delivery.

Suppose the seller's real resource cost of assembling the desk is 800, using tools and standardized processes. However, shipping and storing an assembled desk is costly: it takes up more space, requires careful handling, and increases transport costs. Assume that storage and delivery costs for an assembled desk are 700.

Now consider an alternative contractual clause: flat-pack delivery. Under this option, the buyer assembles the desk at home. Suppose the buyer values the time and effort required for assembly at 900. Flat-pack delivery is cheaper to store and transport, with logistics costs of 200.

Total real resource costs under the two arrangements are therefore:

  • Assembled delivery: 800 (seller assembly) + 700 (assembled logistics) = 1500
  • Flat-pack delivery: 900 (buyer assembly) + 200 (flat-pack logistics) = 1100

Flat-pack delivery reduces total real costs by 400 relative to the seller's standard assembled offer. From an economic perspective, this means that offering a flat-pack option can increase total surplus.

Whether both parties prefer the flat-pack clause depends on the price adjustment. Let \(P\) denote the price of the flat-pack option.

  • The buyer prefers flat-pack if: \[ P + 900 < 3000 \quad \Leftrightarrow \quad P < 2100. \]
  • The seller prefers flat-pack if: \[ P - 200 > 1500 \quad \Leftrightarrow \quad P > 1700. \]

There is therefore a range of prices—any \(P\) between 1700 and 2100—for which both parties prefer flat-pack delivery. This range exists because flat-pack delivery increases total surplus by 400, and the contract price can be adjusted to divide that surplus.

The key point is that whether assembly should be included in the contract depends on total real resource costs, not on any single margin in isolation.

In the furniture example, allocating a task affected costs but not behavior. In many contracts, however, allocating responsibility also affects how parties behave. Defect liability provides a simple and important illustration.

One of the most common problems in contract design is how to ensure adequate quality. In many contractual relationships, one party chooses the quality of performance while another party bears part of the consequences of low quality.

Consider a contractor who can choose between producing high quality and low quality. High quality requires higher upfront costs but reduces the expected costs of future repairs. Low quality is cheaper to produce but leads to higher expected repair costs.

If the contractor does not bear the cost of repairs, they have an incentive to choose low quality even when high quality is socially cheaper. Assigning responsibility for defects to the contractor changes this allocation of responsibility and, as a result, changes behavior.

Suppose:

  • High quality costs 100 to produce and leads to expected repair costs of 10.
  • Low quality costs 90 to produce but leads to expected repair costs of 30.

From a social perspective:

  • High quality: 100 + 10 = 110
  • Low quality: 90 + 30 = 120

High quality is therefore efficient.

Without defect liability, the contractor compares only production costs and chooses low quality. With defect liability, the contractor internalizes expected repair costs and chooses high quality. Production costs rise by 10, but expected repair costs fall by 20, increasing total surplus.

Any increase in the contract price to compensate the contractor is a transfer and does not affect efficiency.

This example also illustrates an important limitation. Some defects arise from factors outside the contractor's control, such as poor maintenance after delivery. Efficient contracts therefore limit defect liability to construction defects and leave responsibility for maintenance-related defects with the party who controls maintenance.

Consider a public authority that tenders the construction of a road tunnel. The cost of construction depends critically on underground geological conditions. Before tendering the project, the authority conducts geological surveys and has better information about the likelihood of difficult ground conditions than potential contractors.

If contractors suspect that the authority's information is incomplete or overly optimistic, they may respond by inflating their bids to protect themselves against the risk of unexpected cost overruns. This can lead to inefficiently high prices or, in extreme cases, deter participation altogether. The underlying problem is asymmetric information: the party with better information does not fully bear the consequences of inaccurate or misleading disclosure.

One contractual response is a clause under which the authority bears the cost of additional excavation or reinforcement if actual geological conditions turn out to be worse than indicated by the survey. By shifting this risk to the informed party, the clause changes incentives before the contract is signed. The authority now internalizes the cost of providing inaccurate information and has stronger incentives to conduct careful surveys and disclose information truthfully. Contractors, in turn, can bid more aggressively when they trust the information provided.

From a cost–benefit perspective, the benefit of the clause is improved information revelation and reduced bid padding, which can lower expected procurement costs and increase the likelihood that efficient projects are undertaken.

The clause also imposes costs. By insulating the contractor from geological risk, it weakens incentives for cost control and efficient adaptation once construction begins. Contractors may exert less effort to minimize overruns when additional costs are borne by the authority. As a result, some inefficiencies may shift from the bidding stage to the performance stage.

Whether the clause increases total surplus therefore depends on a trade-off. It is more likely to be efficient when information asymmetries are severe and inaccurate disclosure would otherwise lead to large bid distortions, and less likely to be efficient when cost control during construction is crucial and largely under the contractor's control. This explains why such clauses are often limited in scope, combined with monitoring requirements, or paired with cost-sharing rules rather than providing full insurance against geological risk.

Consider a large infrastructure project that must be completed by a target date. Delays impose real social costs, such as traffic congestion and coordination problems for other projects. The contractor controls the pace of work, but the buyer and third parties cannot observe the contractor's effort directly. The completion date, however, is observable and verifiable.

Suppose that delaying completion by one day imposes a social cost of 2 million. Avoiding delay requires additional effort by the contractor.

Assume the following for the purpose of analysis:

  • If the contractor finishes on time, the cost of performance is 1,500 million.
  • If the contractor finishes 100 days late, the cost of performance is 1,450 million.
  • Each day of delay imposes a congestion cost of 2 million.

From a social perspective:

  • Finishing on time costs 1,500 million.
  • Finishing 100 days late costs 1,450 + (100 × 2) = 1,650 million.

Timely completion therefore minimizes total social cost.

Without a delay penalty, the contractor compares only its private performance costs and chooses to finish late, saving 50 million. The costs imposed by delay are borne by others and do not affect the contractor's decision.

Now suppose the contract includes a delay penalty of 2 million per day of delay. The contract price is adjusted so that the expected surplus is shared between the parties.

Under this clause, delaying completion reduces the contractor's performance costs but triggers a penalty equal to the cost imposed by delay. The contractor therefore compares:

  • the cost of accelerating completion, and
  • the penalty avoided by doing so.

With this clause in place, the contractor chooses to finish on time, because delaying would increase total costs. The delay penalty aligns the contractor's incentives with the effects of delay on total surplus.\medskip

Force Majeure and Uncontrollable Delay

The analysis above assumes that delay is fully under the contractor's control. In many real-world projects, some delays are caused by uncontrollable events such as extreme weather, regulatory decisions, or supply-chain disruptions.

When contractors are risk averse, penalizing delays caused by such events imposes risk-bearing costs without improving incentives. Even if the expected penalty can be priced into the contract, exposing the contractor to penalties for bad luck reduces total surplus by shifting risk to the party least able to insure against it.

Force majeure clauses respond to this problem by excusing delays caused by specified uncontrollable events. By removing penalties for outcomes that effort cannot influence, these clauses preserve the incentive effects of delay penalties on controllable behavior while avoiding inefficient risk shifting.

In this sense, force majeure clauses reflect the same economic logic as other contractual responses to moral hazard: incentives should be tied to outcomes that the relevant party can affect, while uncontrollable risk is efficiently allocated or excused.\medskip

Multidimensional Effort

The analysis above assumes that the contractor's effort affects only the timing of completion. In many real-world projects, effort affects several dimensions at once, such as speed, quality, safety, or future maintenance costs. A delay penalty therefore targets only one margin of behavior: strong incentives to finish quickly may reduce effort along other, unpriced dimensions, increasing costs elsewhere. From a cost–benefit perspective, higher delay penalties strengthen timing incentives but may distort behavior on other margins, which explains why efficient contracts often combine delay penalties with additional clauses—such as defect liability, quality standards, or monitoring—rather than relying on a single high-powered incentive.

Takeaways

  • Include a contractual clause if the behavioral changes it induces create real resource benefits that exceed real resource costs (ignore pure transfers between parties)
  • Allocate tasks, risks, and responsibilities to the party that can handle them at lowest total cost (efficient allocation principle / least-cost avoider)
  • Contractual responses to asymmetric information (warranties, guarantees, cost-sharing) trade off improved incentives or information revelation against incentive distortions or reduced insurance
  • Use verifiable proxies when direct conditioning on behavior or intent is not feasible (trade-off between precision and enforceability)
  • Expectation damages induce efficient breach decisions when the breaching party internalizes the harm caused to the other party
  • When interpreting incomplete contracts, impute the term that minimizes total expected costs (typically assigns responsibility to the least-cost avoider)

Exam Questions

▶ Question 15.1: Carbon Capture System: Liability Clause for Performance Risk

Case Description:

The cement company NordCem AS plans to install a carbon capture system at its plant in southern Norway to meet increasingly strict emission targets. NordCem has signed a letter of intent with CleanCarbon Technologies AS, a Norwegian technology company specializing in CO2 capture solutions for industrial applications.

The system promises to capture around 400,000 tonnes of CO2 per year—roughly 50% of NordCem's current emissions—using an amine-based chemical process. However, the actual capture rate depends on local operating conditions, such as kiln gas composition, temperature stability, and maintenance routines. If capture rates fall below expectations, NordCem risks exceeding its permitted emissions limits and will be required to purchase additional CO2 quotas under the EU Emissions Trading System (EU ETS), potentially at a high market price.

Question:
