NordCem AS is considering including a clause in the contract stating that CleanCarbon Technologies AS must cover any additional CO2 quota costs NordCem incurs if the carbon capture system underperforms.

Which factors determine whether including such a liability clause is a good idea for NordCem AS? Discuss using economic theory.

▶ What a Good Answer Should Contain
  1. Apply cost-benefit analysis to evaluate whether the clause increases total welfare
  2. Identify the real resource costs and benefits for each party
  3. Determine which party can most efficiently control the performance risk (least-cost avoider principle)
  4. Consider how the clause affects each party's incentives (moral hazard)
  5. Assess whether asymmetric information about system quality makes the clause valuable as a signal
  6. Use a numerical example to illustrate the trade-offs
  7. State key assumptions and how conclusions would change if assumptions are relaxed
▶ Example solution

Framework: Cost-Benefit Analysis and Contract Design

This question asks whether a liability clause should be included in the contract. Following the analytical framework from the Contracts chapter, we need to:

  1. Conduct cost-benefit analysis of the clause
  2. Consider information asymmetries
  3. Consider enforcement and verifiability constraints

Step 1: Cost-Benefit Analysis

The clause should be included if it increases total welfare. If it does, both parties can be made better off by adjusting the contract price.

To assess this, we need to identify the real resource costs and benefits:

Benefits of including the clause Costs of including the clause
B1. Lower quota costs (if CleanCarbon C1. CleanCarbon invests more resources in
has stronger incentives to deliver system development/installation
a high-performance system)  
B2. NordCem spends less on maintenance C2. Risk of higher quota costs (if NordCem
and operation reduces effort on maintenance/operation)

Effect on incentives:

  • CleanCarbon's incentives: If made responsible for covering additional CO2 quota costs, CleanCarbon will likely invest more effort in developing, installing, and configuring the system to avoid underperformance. This creates real resource cost C1.
  • NordCem's incentives: The clause weakens NordCem's incentives to maintain and operate the system carefully. Since they no longer bear the financial consequences of underperformance, they may reduce maintenance effort (benefit B2 from their perspective, but potential cost C2 for total welfare).

Step 2: Numerical Example

Whether the clause improves total welfare depends on which party can most efficiently control performance risk—the "least-cost avoider" principle.

Assume:

  • CleanCarbon can invest 10 million NOK to improve the system
  • NordCem can spend 10 million NOK on effective operation and maintenance
  • Additional CO2 quota costs depend on their actions:
    • 0 million NOK if both parties invest/operate carefully
    • 10 million NOK if NordCem operates carefully but CleanCarbon doesn't invest
    • 20 million NOK if CleanCarbon invests but NordCem doesn't operate carefully
    • 40 million NOK if neither party invests/operates carefully

Without the liability clause:

  • CleanCarbon has no incentive to invest (doesn't bear quota costs)
  • NordCem has strong incentive to operate carefully: benefit = 40M - 10M = 30M NOK > 10M cost
  • Result: NordCem operates carefully, CleanCarbon doesn't invest
  • Total costs: 10M (operation) + 10M (quota costs) = 20M NOK

With the liability clause:

  • CleanCarbon has strong incentive to invest (bears quota costs)
  • NordCem has no incentive to operate carefully (doesn't bear quota costs)
  • Result: CleanCarbon invests, NordCem doesn't operate carefully
  • Total costs: 10M (investment) + 20M (quota costs) = 30M NOK

Conclusion for these numbers: The clause increases total costs and should NOT be included. The reason is that NordCem is the least-cost avoider—the system's effectiveness depends primarily on careful operation and maintenance, not just initial investment.

Alternative scenario: If the quota costs were instead:

  • 20 million NOK if NordCem operates carefully but CleanCarbon doesn't invest
  • 10 million NOK if CleanCarbon invests but NordCem doesn't operate carefully

Then the clause would reduce total costs because CleanCarbon becomes the least-cost avoider.

Step 3: Information Asymmetries

The clause may also help address asymmetric information. If CleanCarbon has private information about the system's likely performance, their willingness to accept the liability clause serves as a credible signal. A supplier with an unreliable system would be unwilling to accept such terms.

This signaling value could justify including the clause even if it doesn't improve operational incentives, because it helps NordCem avoid purchasing an unreliable system.

Step 4: Key Factors

The factors determining whether the liability clause is a good idea are:

  1. Which party can most efficiently control performance risk:
    • If CleanCarbon's design, installation, and system quality are decisive → include the clause
    • If NordCem's maintenance and operation are decisive → don't include the clause
  2. Asymmetric information:
    • If CleanCarbon has significant private information about system reliability, the clause has value as a quality signal
  3. Verifiability:
    • The clause requires ability to verify whether underperformance occurred and attribute causes. If this is difficult or expensive, the clause may not be enforceable in practice.

Key Assumptions:

  • Risk neutrality (parties evaluate outcomes by expected value)
  • Parties respond to incentives (moral hazard exists)
  • Contract terms are enforceable

How conclusions change:

  • If both parties' efforts are important, a split liability clause (allocating responsibility based on the cause of underperformance) might be superior
  • If verification is difficult, performance-based payment or warranties might be more practical than ex-post liability
▶ Question 15.2: Carbon Capture System: Alternative Performance Assurance Clauses

Case Description:

The cement company NordCem AS plans to install a carbon capture system at its plant in southern Norway to meet increasingly strict emission targets. NordCem has signed a letter of intent with CleanCarbon Technologies AS, a Norwegian technology company specializing in CO2 capture solutions for industrial applications.

The system promises to capture around 400,000 tonnes of CO2 per year—roughly 50% of NordCem's current emissions—using an amine-based chemical process. However, the actual capture rate depends on local operating conditions, such as kiln gas composition, temperature stability, and maintenance routines. If capture rates fall below expectations, NordCem risks exceeding its permitted emissions limits and will be required to purchase additional CO2 quotas under the EU Emissions Trading System (EU ETS), potentially at a high market price.

Question:

What other types of clauses could NordCem AS include in the contract to ensure that the carbon capture system performs as promised? Explain your suggestions using economic theory.

▶ What a Good Answer Should Contain
  1. Identify the underlying economic problems the clauses need to address (asymmetric information, moral hazard, verifiability)
  2. For each proposed clause, explain:
    • What economic problem it solves
    • How it changes incentives
    • Whether it is verifiable/enforceable
  3. Propose at least 2-3 different types of clauses
  4. Use economic theory explicitly (e.g., signaling, incentive alignment, information revelation)
  5. Consider which clauses work best under different assumptions
▶ Example solution

Framework: Contract Design Under Asymmetric Information and Moral Hazard

The question asks what contract clauses can ensure system performance. The economic problems to address are:

  1. Asymmetric information: CleanCarbon knows more about system quality than NordCem
  2. Moral hazard: Both parties' hidden actions affect performance
  3. Verifiability: Some performance metrics are easier to verify than others

I will propose several clauses and explain how each addresses these problems.

Clause 1: Performance Guarantee

Description: CleanCarbon guarantees a minimum capture rate (e.g., 90% CO2 capture efficiency over a specified period).

Economic theory: This is a warranty that serves as a quality signal. A supplier with a low-quality system would be unwilling to offer such a guarantee because they expect to pay compensation when the guarantee is breached.

Benefits:

  • Reduces asymmetric information about system quality
  • Credibly separates high-quality from low-quality suppliers
  • Shifts performance risk to the party with better information

Limitations:

  • Requires verifiable measurement of capture rate
  • May be ineffective if NordCem's operation also affects performance (moral hazard on NordCem's side)

Clause 2: Performance-Based Payment

Description: CleanCarbon's payment is tied to actual system performance (e.g., payment per tonne of CO2 captured, or bonus payments for exceeding capture targets).

Economic theory: This aligns CleanCarbon's incentives with the desired outcome and addresses moral hazard. Payment-for-performance reduces the need to monitor CleanCarbon's hidden effort.

Benefits:

  • Creates direct incentive for CleanCarbon to deliver high performance
  • Automatically adjusts compensation based on results
  • Reduces need for detailed specification of required actions

Limitations:

  • Requires accurate, verifiable measurement of performance
  • May transfer too much risk to CleanCarbon if performance depends heavily on factors outside their control
  • Could weaken CleanCarbon's incentives to invest if NordCem's maintenance effort is also important

Clause 3: Independent Third-Party Testing

Description: Require independent testing and certification of the system's performance before final payment or acceptance.

Economic theory: This is an information revelation mechanism that reduces asymmetric information. Third-party verification makes performance observable to both parties.

Benefits:

  • Provides credible information about system quality before NordCem commits fully
  • Reduces risk of accepting an underperforming system
  • May be less adversarial than ex-post litigation

Limitations:

  • Testing has real resource costs
  • May not detect problems that emerge only during long-term operation
  • Effectiveness depends on test quality and independence

Clause 4: Split Liability Based on Cause of Underperformance

Description: Allocate liability for additional CO2 quota costs based on the verified cause:

  • CleanCarbon pays if underperformance is due to system design, installation, or technological failure
  • NordCem pays if underperformance is due to improper operation or maintenance

Economic theory: This is an application of the least-cost avoider principle. Each party bears the cost of outcomes they can control, providing efficient incentives for both.

Benefits:

  • Aligns incentives for both parties (addresses bilateral moral hazard)
  • More efficient than making one party fully liable when both affect outcomes
  • Preserves incentives for cost-effective risk reduction by both sides

Limitations:

  • Requires ability to verify the cause of underperformance (may be difficult in practice)
  • May lead to disputes about causation
  • High transaction costs if verification is expensive

Clause 5: CleanCarbon Responsible for Operation and Maintenance

Description: CleanCarbon not only delivers the system but also operates and maintains it for a specified period (e.g., 5 years).

Economic theory: This is a vertical integration solution that gives CleanCarbon full control over all factors affecting performance. Combined with a performance guarantee or performance-based payment, this eliminates the moral hazard problem on NordCem's side.

Benefits:

  • Removes the bilateral moral hazard problem
  • CleanCarbon can optimize both system design and operation
  • Simpler to enforce than split liability

Limitations:

  • May be more expensive if CleanCarbon is less efficient at operation than NordCem
  • Requires long-term contracting
  • May create dependency on CleanCarbon

Comparison and Recommendations

The best clause depends on:

  1. Information asymmetry: If large → performance guarantees, testing
  2. Which party controls performance: If both → split liability or integrated operation
  3. Verifiability constraints: If cause of failure is hard to verify → performance-based payment or integrated operation
  4. Transaction costs: If high → simpler clauses like performance guarantees

A combination of clauses is often optimal. For example:

  • Performance guarantee (addresses information asymmetry)
  • Plus independent testing (verifies quality before acceptance)
  • Plus performance-based payment component (maintains incentives during operation)

Key Assumptions:

  • Parties are rational and respond to incentives
  • Some performance metrics are verifiable by courts
  • Contract terms are legally enforceable

How conclusions change:

  • If verification is impossible → only reputation-based incentives or vertical integration work
  • If both parties' efforts are important and verifiable → split liability is efficient
  • If CleanCarbon has much better information → warranties and testing are most valuable
▶ Question 15.3: Pharmaceutical Supply Agreement: Optimal Damage Clauses

Case Description:

You advise Biotech Innovations Ltd., a pharmaceutical firm specializing in developing and manufacturing innovative drugs. They have just innovated a new drug, "BioCureX," a breakthrough treatment for a rare genetic disorder. The active pharmaceutical ingredient required for BioCureX is exclusively supplied by PharmaChem Co. Biotech Innovations is about to sign a long-term supply agreement with PharmaChem. But they are worried that PharmaChem might fail to consistently supply the amount of the ingredient necessary for large-scale production.

Question:

The contract will include a clause stipulating the damages PharmaChem should pay Biotech Innovations if it fails to supply the ingredient. How large will you recommend these damages to be? Which factors determine your advice? Explain using economic theory. Pay specific attention to the fact that, after signing the contract, Biotech Innovations will enter into significant investments to expand its capacity to produce the drug and get it approved for commercial sale.

▶ What a Good Answer Should Contain
  1. Identify the goal: maximize total welfare (efficient contract)
  2. Explain why expectation damages are efficient using the efficient breach principle
  3. Define what expectation damages mean in this specific context
  4. Use numerical examples to show why damages that are too high or too low are inefficient
  5. Address the reliance investment issue: when should investments be compensated?
  6. Discuss the concept of hypothetical expectation damages
  7. State key assumptions and how conclusions change if relaxed
▶ Example solution

Framework: Efficient Breach and Expectation Damages

The question asks how large damages should be in a supply contract. The economic principle is that damages should induce efficient breach: PharmaChem should breach if and only if the cost of performance exceeds the benefit to Biotech Innovations.

Step 1: Why Both Parties Want an Efficient Contract

Both parties benefit from maximizing the total surplus (the "total pie") from their contract. If the contract creates more total value, they can adjust the price to make both better off. Therefore, we should recommend the damage level that maximizes efficiency, not the level that transfers the most value to Biotech Innovations.

Step 2: The Efficient Breach Principle

PharmaChem should supply the ingredient if and only if:

  • Benefit to Biotech Innovations ≥ Cost to PharmaChem

If damages equal Biotech's benefit from performance (expectation damages), PharmaChem's decision rule becomes:

  • Supply if: Cost to PharmaChem < Damages = Biotech's benefit
  • Don't supply if: Cost to PharmaChem > Damages = Biotech's benefit

This aligns PharmaChem's incentives with efficiency.

Step 3: Numerical Example - Why Damages Should Equal Expectation Damages

Assume:

  • Biotech's benefit from receiving the ingredient: 100 million NOK (from drug sales, reputation, etc.)
  • PharmaChem's normal cost of supply: 40 million NOK
  • PharmaChem faces unexpected production problem; new cost: 120 million NOK

Case A: Damages too low (e.g., 50 million NOK)

  • If PharmaChem supplies: Cost = 120M, receives payment
  • If PharmaChem breaches: Pays 50M in damages
  • PharmaChem will breach (120M > 50M)
  • But this is inefficient! Biotech loses 100M in value while PharmaChem saves only 120M in cost. Net loss to society: 100M (benefit lost) though breach saves net resources.

Wait, let me recalculate:

  • If supply: PharmaChem pays 120M cost, Biotech gets 100M benefit, net social value = 100M - 120M = -20M
  • If breach: No supply cost, no benefit, damages are just a transfer
  • Breach is actually efficient here (saves 20M in net resources)

Let me redo with the right scenario:

Corrected Case A: Damages too low (e.g., 50 million NOK), breach is inefficient Assume unexpected cost is 80M instead of 120M:

  • If PharmaChem supplies: Cost 80M, Biotech gets 100M benefit → Net social value = 20M
  • If PharmaChem breaches: Pays only 50M damages → PharmaChem prefers to breach even though supply is efficient
  • Result: Inefficient breach occurs (foregoes 20M in social value)

Case B: Damages too high (e.g., 150 million NOK), performance is inefficient PharmaChem's cost rises to 120M:

  • If PharmaChem supplies: Cost 120M, Biotech gets 100M benefit → Net social value = -20M (inefficient)
  • If PharmaChem breaches: Pays 150M damages → PharmaChem will supply to avoid damages
  • Result: Inefficient performance occurs (wastes 20M in social resources)

Case C: Damages equal expectation damages (100 million NOK)

  • When cost = 80M: PharmaChem supplies (80M < 100M) → Efficient
  • When cost = 120M: PharmaChem breaches (120M > 100M) → Efficient
  • Result: PharmaChem's incentives align with efficiency

Step 4: What Are Expectation Damages in This Case?

Expectation damages put Biotech Innovations in the position they would have been in if PharmaChem had performed. This includes:

  1. Lost profits from drug sales they cannot make without the ingredient
  2. Reputational harm from being unable to supply patients with the promised treatment
  3. Wasted investments that have no value without the ingredient

The question specifically mentions "significant investments to expand capacity and get approval for commercial sale." Since the ingredient is exclusively supplied by PharmaChem, these investments have no alternative use if PharmaChem breaches.

Step 5: The Reliance Investment Problem

A critical issue: Should Biotech Innovations be compensated for ALL their investments?

If Biotech is fully compensated for all investments, they might make inefficient investments.

Numerical example:

  • Probability PharmaChem can supply: 70%
  • Biotech considers investment in production capacity: 50M NOK
  • Expected value of investment: 0.7 × 150M - 50M = 55M (efficient if PharmaChem supplies)
  • If breach, investment is worthless

Without full compensation: Biotech's decision:

  • Expected return: 0.7 × 150M = 105M
  • Expected cost: 50M
  • Expected net: 55M → Invest (efficient)

With full compensation for all investments: Biotech's decision:

  • Expected return if supply: 0.7 × 150M = 105M
  • Expected return if breach: 0.3 × 50M (get compensation) = 15M
  • Total expected return: 105M + 15M = 120M
  • Expected cost: 50M
  • Expected net: 70M → Still invest

This seems fine, but now consider an inefficient investment worth only 20M if PharmaChem supplies:

  • Without compensation: 0.7 × 20M - 50M = -36M → Don't invest (correct)
  • With full compensation: 0.7 × 20M + 0.3 × 50M - 50M = -21M → Still don't invest (correct)

Actually, let me reconsider. The issue is when Biotech makes investments that are only valuable if PharmaChem's cost remains low.

Better example:

  • Investment cost: 50M
  • Value if PharmaChem's cost stays low (supplies): 60M
  • Value if PharmaChem's cost is high (should breach): 0M
  • Probability cost stays low: 40%

Efficient decision: Expected value = 0.4 × 60M - 50M = -26M → Don't invest

With full compensation: Biotech gets 50M back if breach occurs

  • Biotech's expected return: 0.4 × 60M + 0.6 × 50M = 54M
  • Cost: 50M
  • Net: 4M → Invest (inefficient!)

Solution: Hypothetical Expectation Damages

Compensate only for investments that would have been efficient ex ante, given the information available. This prevents over-investment while still protecting legitimate reliance.

Step 6: Factors Determining the Damage Level

  1. Biotech's lost profits from being unable to produce and sell BioCureX
  2. Reputational damage from failing to supply a promised treatment
  3. Efficient reliance investments (not all investments, only those that were efficient ex ante)
  4. Verifiability: Damages must be verifiable by courts to be enforceable

Recommendation:

Set damages at expectation damages level, defined as:

  • Lost profits from drug sales
  • Reputational harm (if measurable)
  • Reliance investments that were efficient given the probability of performance

This gives PharmaChem efficient breach incentives while protecting Biotech's legitimate reliance.

Key Assumptions:

  • Parties are risk-neutral
  • Courts can verify the value of performance
  • No additional externalities beyond the parties' benefits

How conclusions change:

  • If Biotech is risk-averse → might want damages slightly above expectation to compensate for risk-bearing
  • If courts cannot verify lost profits → might need to rely on proxies or standardized damages
  • If reliance investments create positive externalities → might want to encourage them with super-expectation damages
▶ Question 15.4: Startup Damage Clause: Uncertain Development Costs

Case Description:

A start-up tech company develops AI-powered apps for its customers. A customer requests an app that the company fears could be difficult to develop. To reduce risks, the company wants to have the option to breach the contract and pay damages if the app turns out to be too costly to develop. The company wants to include a term in the contract that explicitly states the amount to be paid in damages in case of breach of contract.

Question:

How large would you recommend this amount to be? Explain using economic theory.

▶ What a Good Answer Should Contain
  1. Identify the goal: efficient breach (breach if and only if cost exceeds value)
  2. Define expectation damages as the benchmark
  3. Use numerical example to show why expectation damages create efficient breach
  4. Show why damages that are too high or too low are inefficient
  5. Address the reliance/investment problem (hypothetical expectation damages)
  6. State key assumptions and when conclusions change
▶ Example solution

Framework: Efficient Breach and Expectation Damages

The question asks how large damages should be when development costs are uncertain. The economic principle is to set damages at the level that induces efficient breach: the company should breach if and only if the cost of development exceeds the customer's value.

Step 1: Setup with Uncertainty

Assume:

  • Customer's value of the app: V = 120
  • Development cost is uncertain:
    • Low cost: C_L = 80 (probability 50%)
    • High cost: C_H = 150 (probability 50%)
  • Contracted damages for breach: D (to be determined)

Step 2: When Should the Company Breach?

Efficiency criterion (Kaldor-Hicks):

  • Develop if cost < customer's value
  • Breach if cost > customer's value

Apply to our example:

  • If cost = 80: Develop (80 < 120) → Net social value = 120 - 80 = 40
  • If cost = 150: Breach (150 > 120) → Net social value = 0 (no resources wasted)

The efficient outcome is to develop only in the low-cost state.

Step 3: How Large Should Damages Be?

The company breaches if: Cost of development > Cost of breach C > D

To achieve efficient breach, we want:

  • Breach when C = 150 (efficient)
  • Don't breach when C = 80 (efficient)

This requires: 80 < D < 150

But more precisely, we want breach to occur if and only if C > 120 (customer's value).

Optimal damage level: D = 120 (customer's value)

This is expectation damages: the amount that makes the customer indifferent between receiving the app and receiving damages.

Step 4: Numerical Verification

With D = 120:

When cost = 80:

  • Develop: Cost 80, customer gets value 120, company gets payment (say 130)
  • Breach: Pay damages 120
  • Company prefers to develop if: Payment - 80 > -120
    • Or: Payment > -40 (always true)
  • Company develops ✓

When cost = 150:

  • Develop: Cost 150, company gets payment 130
  • Breach: Pay damages 120, save development cost
  • Company prefers breach if: -120 > 130 - 150 = -20
  • This is false! Company would still develop.

Wait, let me recalculate. The company's decision:

When cost = 150:

  • Develop: Pay 150 cost, receive P (price), net = P - 150
  • Breach: Pay 120 damages, net = -120

Company breaches if: -120 > P - 150 Or: P < 30

This doesn't make sense with the setup. Let me reconsider.

The company receives payments (total of 100 as set up earlier). Let me clarify:

Actually, thinking about it more carefully:

  • Customer pays total of 100 for the app
  • If company breaches, company must pay damages D back to customer
  • Customer has already paid 50 upfront

Company's payoff:

  • Develop: Revenue 100, Cost C, Net = 100 - C
  • Breach: Keep upfront payment 50, pay damages D, Net = 50 - D

Company breaches if: 50 - D > 100 - C C > 100 + 50 - D C > 150 - D

To get efficient breach (breach when C > 120): 120 = 150 - D D = 30?

No wait, this still doesn't align. Let me reconsider the damage structure.

Standard approach: Damages compensate customer for not getting the app.

If customer pays total price P = 100 and receives damages D:

  • Customer's net position with damages: D - P (since they already paid P)
  • Customer's net position if app delivered: V - P (value V, paid P)

For expectation damages (customer indifferent): D - P = V - P D = V = 120

So damages should be D = 120.

Now company's decision:

  • Develop: Receive P = 100, pay cost C, net = 100 - C
  • Breach: Pay damages D = 120 (customer gets refunded plus compensation), net = -120

Actually, I need to clarify whether: (a) Customer pays P, if breach customer gets D back (so D is total amount returned), or (b) Customer pays P, if breach customer gets D in addition to keeping their payment

Let's use interpretation (a): D is the total amount customer receives if there's a breach.

Customer's position:

  • If performance: Pays P, gets value V, net = V - P = 120 - 100 = 20
  • If breach: Pays P, receives D back, net = D - P

Expectation damages: D - P = V - P → D = V = 120

Company's position:

  • If performance: Receives P, pays cost C, net = P - C = 100 - C
  • If breach: Receives P, pays back D, net = P - D = 100 - 120 = -20

Company breaches if: -20 > 100 - C Or: C > 120

Perfect! Company breaches if and only if cost exceeds customer's value.

Step 5: Why This Is Efficient

With D = V = 120:

Low cost scenario (C = 80):

  • Develop: 100 - 80 = 20
  • Breach: -20
  • Company develops (20 > -20) ✓ Efficient

High cost scenario (C = 150):

  • Develop: 100 - 150 = -50
  • Breach: -20
  • Company breaches (-20 > -50) ✓ Efficient

Step 6: Why Other Damage Levels Are Inefficient

If D = 150 (too high): High cost scenario:

  • Develop: -50
  • Breach: 100 - 150 = -50
  • Indifferent, might develop → Inefficient! (wastes 150 - 120 = 30 in resources)

If D = 80 (too low): High cost scenario:

  • Develop: -50
  • Breach: 100 - 80 = 20
  • Company breaches ✓ Correct

Low cost scenario:

  • Develop: 20
  • Breach: 20
  • Indifferent, might breach → Inefficient! (foregoes value 120 - 80 = 40)

Conclusion: D = 120 (customer's value) = expectation damages creates efficient breach incentives.

Step 7: The Reliance Investment Problem

A complication arises if the customer can make investments that increase the app's value relying on the promise of delivery.

Example:

  • Base value of app to customer: 120
  • Customer can invest 20 in complementary technology
  • If invest, app value increases to 140
  • Investment only valuable if app is delivered

With standard expectation damages D = customer's value:

If customer invests:

  • New expectation damages: D = 140
  • Company breaches when: C > 140
  • With 50% chance of C = 150, expected probability of breach = 50%

Customer's decision to invest:

  • Benefit if app delivered (50% chance): 140 - 120 = 20
  • Cost: 20
  • Expected benefit: 0.5 × 20 = 10
  • Expected cost: 20
  • Don't invest? Wait, if breach, customer gets damages = 140, so they're fully compensated!

Customer's expected value if invest:

  • 50% get app worth 140: value 140 - 100 (price) - 20 (investment) = 20
  • 50% breach, get damages 140: 140 - 100 (price) - 20 (investment) = 20
  • Expected value: 20 (certain)

Without investment:

  • Value = 120 - 100 = 20

So customer is indifferent. But socially, the investment might be inefficient!

Social value of investment:

  • Cost: 20
  • Benefit: Only if app delivered AND cost is low
  • Prob(deliver) = Prob(C = 80) = 50%
  • Social benefit: 0.5 × (140-120) = 10
  • Social cost: 20
  • Net: -10 (inefficient!)

Problem: Customer invests because they're fully compensated even if there's breach, but the investment is socially inefficient.

Solution: Hypothetical Expectation Damages

Compensate the customer only for the value they would have had if they hadn't over-relied.

Hypothetical expectation damages: D = 120 (the value without the inefficient investment)

This gives customer correct incentives:

  • If invest and app delivered: 140 - 100 - 20 = 20
  • If invest and breach: 120 - 100 - 20 = 0
  • Expected value if invest: 0.5 × 20 + 0.5 × 0 = 10
  • Cost: 20
  • Don't invest ✓ (correct decision)

Recommendation

Set damages D = 120 (customer's value of the app without excessive reliance investments).

If customer makes efficient investments that increase value, those can be included in damages. But customer should not be compensated for investments that were inefficient given the probability of breach.

In the U.S., the "foreseeability" doctrine implements this: customer is compensated only for losses the company could reasonably foresee, which approximates hypothetical expectation damages.

Key Assumptions:

  • Cost uncertainty is exogenous (not influenced by company's effort)
  • Customer's value is verifiable by courts
  • Parties are risk-neutral
  • No renegotiation after cost is realized

How conclusions change:

  • If company can influence costs through effort → might need higher damages to prevent moral hazard
  • If customer is risk-averse → might want damages above expectation to provide insurance
  • If parties can renegotiate costlessly → initial damage level matters less (Coase theorem)
▶ Question 15.5: Bareboat Charter: Maintenance Responsibility Allocation

Case Background:

You are an advisor for an international shipping company considering entering into a long-term bareboat charter (lease) of a tanker from a shipowner. "Bareboat" charter means the vessel is leased without crew or equipment, so the charterer is responsible for operations, including crewing, fuel, and all operating costs.

Question:

Your boss thinks it might be advantageous to include a clause making the shipowner responsible for maintenance of the tanker throughout the contract period. Conduct an economic analysis of the proposal. What would you answer your boss?

▶ What a Good Answer Should Contain

A good answer should compare the proposed clause to the baseline allocation of maintenance responsibility under clearly stated assumptions. It should explain how assigning maintenance responsibility affects the charterer’s operational behavior, given that the charterer controls vessel use, and how this influences maintenance costs. The answer should also account for other sources of cost differences between the parties, such as differences in expertise, information, or coordination costs. It should then compare total surplus with and without the clause and conclude whether the clause should be included based on whether it increases total surplus.

▶ Example solution

1. Clause and counterfactual

Counterfactual (baseline): The charterer is responsible for maintenance.

Clause under consideration: The owner assumes maintenance responsibility.

—

2. Key assumptions

  • The charterer controls the vessel’s day-to-day operation (usage intensity, handling, routine checks).
  • The charterer’s operational care affects maintenance costs.
  • Operational care is not verifiable by courts.

—

3. Incentive and behavioral effects

The key behavioral margin is how carefully the charterer operates the vessel.

  • Baseline (charterer responsible): The charterer internalizes the maintenance consequences of their behavior. Rough operation increases their own costs, so they choose careful operation.
  • Clause (owner responsible): The charterer no longer bears the maintenance consequences of rough operation. They capture the benefits of harder use (faster turnaround, higher output) without bearing the associated wear-and-tear costs.

As a result, shifting maintenance responsibility to the owner weakens the charterer’s incentives to operate the vessel carefully. This creates a moral-hazard problem: the party controlling usage no longer bears the cost consequences of that usage.

—

4. Welfare effects

Total surplus depends on direct maintenance costs, costs induced by distorted operational behavior, and transaction costs of performing maintenance.

Under charterer responsibility:

  • The charterer operates the vessel carefully.
  • Maintenance costs are lower.
  • Routine maintenance is performed quickly and cheaply by the onboard crew.
  • The charterer’s proximity to the vessel allows timely responses and reduces coordination costs.

Illustration:

  • Cost of care: 10
  • Maintenance cost with care: 100
  • Total real cost: 110

Under owner responsibility:

  • The charterer operates the vessel more roughly.
  • Maintenance costs rise due to increased wear and delayed response.
  • Maintenance is performed less efficiently due to coordination and contracting frictions.
  • Even apart from incentives, relying on the owner may involve higher costs if maintenance requires external contractors or delayed intervention.

Illustration:

  • Maintenance cost: 300
  • Total real cost: 300

Total surplus is therefore lower under owner responsibility, because the clause both weakens operational incentives and increases real maintenance and coordination costs.

—

5. Verifiability

The clause allocating maintenance responsibility is verifiable by courts. If operational care were also verifiable, the owner could retain maintenance responsibility while including a contractual obligation that the charterer operate the vessel carefully.

Under the stated assumptions, however, operational care is not verifiable. The contract therefore cannot condition liability directly on care, and allocating maintenance responsibility is the available instrument for providing operational incentives.

—

6. Conditional conclusion

Under the stated assumptions, shifting maintenance responsibility from the charterer to the owner reduces total surplus by weakening operational incentives and increasing real maintenance and coordination costs.

Because the clause does not increase total surplus, it should not be included. Any change in the allocation of maintenance responsibility would be reflected in the lease price, so the higher real costs would ultimately be borne by the parties.

Answer to the boss: "We should not include this clause. Making the owner responsible for maintenance would reduce our incentives to operate the vessel carefully and increase total maintenance costs. Although the owner would formally bear these costs, they would be passed back to us through a higher lease price, leaving us worse off overall."

This conclusion holds as long as charterer behavior materially affects maintenance costs and operational care is not verifiable. An exception may arise for major structural repairs or dry-docking requiring specialized equipment, where owner responsibility may be efficient.

▶ Question 15.6: Bareboat Charter: Accident Liability Allocation

Case Background:

You are an advisor for an international shipping company considering entering into a long-term bareboat charter (lease) of a tanker from a shipowner. "Bareboat" charter means the vessel is leased without crew or equipment, so the charterer is responsible for operations, including crewing, fuel, and all operating costs.

Question:

During the contract period, accidents such as collisions, sinking, and grounding can occur. What factors affect who should bear the costs of such accidents? Explain using economic theory.

▶ What a Good Answer Should Contain
  1. Use cost-benefit analysis framework
  2. Apply least-cost avoider principle—allocate risk to party best able to reduce it
  3. Analyze moral hazard for different types of accidents
  4. Consider who has control over factors causing each type of accident
  5. Discuss efficient risk allocation and incentive effects
  6. Use numerical examples to illustrate cost minimization
  7. Consider role of insurance and risk preferences
▶ Example solution

Framework: Cost-Benefit Analysis and Efficient Risk Allocation

The question asks who should bear costs of accidents. The economic answer is that the charterer should be responsible for accidents if this reduces total costs of accidents plus accident prevention measures.

Step 1: General Principle—Least-Cost Avoider

Economic theory suggests that liability for accidents should be allocated to the party best able to control or reduce the risk. This gives strong incentives to minimize accident probability and handle consequences cost-effectively.

The key principle is that allocating liability to the party that can most cheaply prevent accidents minimizes total social costs (prevention costs plus accident costs).

Step 2: Analysis of Operational Accidents—Charterer Liability

For accidents arising from vessel operation, the charterer should bear liability because they control operations and can take cost-effective prevention measures.

Collision liability example:

If the owner has liability for collisions, the charterer will be less careful and collision frequency will increase. Cost-benefit analysis of charterer bearing these costs:

Costs Benefits
More caution required Fewer accidents

The cost is that the charterer will take more precautions. The benefit is fewer accidents. How do we know benefits exceed costs? Since the charterer pays both costs of caution and costs of accidents, a rational charterer will ensure total costs are minimized. Total costs of caution plus accidents will therefore be lower than if the charterer only considers costs of caution (and doesn't have liability for accidents).

Numerical example:

Assume the charterer can be careful or not. Caution costs 10 and reduces collision probability from 1% to 0.1%. A collision costs 10,000.

Total cost with caution: 10 + 0.001 × 10,000 = 20 Total cost without caution: 0.01 × 10,000 = 100

It is Kaldor-Hicks efficient for the charterer to be careful (20 < 100). But the charterer won't be willing to pay 10 for caution if the owner has liability. If the charterer has liability, they have incentives to be careful.

Contract price adjustment:

Assume lease price would be 1,000 if owner has collision liability. By transferring collision liability to charterer and reducing lease price to 950, both parties benefit:

  • Charterer pays 950 + 10 (caution) + 0.001 × 10,000 (expected collision) = 970 instead of 1,000
  • Owner receives 950 instead of 1,000 - 0.01 × 10,000 (expected collision) = 900

Both parties are better off (Pareto improvement).

Step 3: Types of Accidents Charterer Should Cover

The charterer should bear liability for accidents arising from their operations:

1. Collisions and groundings:

  • Charterer controls crew and navigation
  • Has best incentives to avoid navigation errors
  • Allocating risk encourages investment in experienced crew, safety procedures, modern navigation systems

Cost analysis:

  • Cost of experienced crew: 50,000/year
  • Reduction in accident probability: from 2% to 0.5%
  • Expected accident cost: 1,000,000
  • Without liability: Charterer pays 0 → no incentive to hire experienced crew
  • With liability: Charterer pays 50,000 to save 0.015 × 1,000,000 = 15,000 → rational to invest

2. Cargo damage:

  • Charterer controls loading, unloading, and securing cargo
  • Responsible handling minimizes damage
  • Encourages investment in proper procedures and technology

3. Pollution and environmental damage:

  • Charterer controls operations that could cause pollution
  • Liability encourages careful operation and spill prevention measures

Step 4: Types of Accidents Owner Should Cover

The owner should bear liability for accidents due to structural defects or conditions existing before charter:

1. Latent defects:

  • Defects present at delivery but not discovered during inspection
  • Owner is better positioned to ensure vessel quality before lease
  • Creates incentives for owner to deliver vessel in optimal condition

2. Structural failures:

  • Issues from design, construction, or long-term wear not attributable to charterer's operations
  • Motivates owner to ensure vessel is built and maintained for long life

Numerical example:

Assume structural defect causes accident:

  • Probability if owner doesn't inspect thoroughly: 5%
  • Probability if owner inspects: 0.5%
  • Inspection cost: 20,000
  • Accident cost: 500,000

Without owner liability:

  • Owner has no incentive to inspect
  • Expected cost: 0.05 × 500,000 = 25,000 (borne by charterer)

With owner liability:

  • Owner inspects to reduce risk
  • Owner's cost: 20,000 + 0.005 × 500,000 = 22,500 < 25,000
  • Social savings: 2,500

Step 5: Moral Hazard and Efficient Allocation

If the charterer doesn't bear costs of accidents they can influence, moral hazard arises—the charterer has less incentive to operate safely. This can lead to increased accidents, higher maintenance costs, and ultimately lower vessel value.

Avoiding moral hazard:

By making the charterer liable for operational accidents, incentives for safe operation are maintained. This reduces risk that the charterer takes unnecessary chances or fails to maintain the vessel properly.

Step 6: Insurance Considerations

Both parties should have appropriate insurance coverage:

Charterer's insurance:

  • Hull and Machinery Insurance: Covers damage to vessel hull and machinery from accidents during operation
  • Protection and Indemnity (P&I) Insurance: Covers third-party liabilities including cargo damage, pollution, personal injury
  • War Risk Insurance: If operating in conflict areas

Owner's insurance:

  • Construction and Latent Defect Insurance: Covers accidents caused by structural defects
  • Return liability: Coverage for damage discovered after vessel return if due to underlying structural issues

Insurance cost analysis:

  • Charterer with operational liability: Must buy P&I insurance, but this aligns with their ability to control risk
  • Owner insuring charterer's operational risks: Higher premiums due to moral hazard, ultimately paid by charterer through higher lease price

Step 7: Force Majeure and Uncontrollable Risks

For accidents neither party can prevent ("force majeure" such as natural disasters or war), allocation may depend on:

  1. Risk tolerance: The more risk-tolerant party should bear uncontrollable risks
  2. Insurance access: Party with better access to affordable insurance
  3. Diversification: Party better able to spread risk across multiple operations

Note: Since risk preferences and insurance aren't covered in this course, detailed discussion isn't expected, but awareness of these factors is valuable.

Step 8: Summary of Allocation Principles

The key factor determining who should have accident liability is who can most easily reduce the risk of the given type of accident through due care.

Allocation framework:

Accident Type Liable Party Reason
Operational (collision, grounding) Charterer Controls navigation and crew
Cargo damage Charterer Controls loading/handling
Structural defects Owner Controls vessel condition at delivery
Latent defects Owner Better positioned to detect before lease
Force majeure Either* Based on risk tolerance and insurance access

*For force majeure, economic theory is less clear, and allocation may depend on risk preferences.

Step 9: Verification with Numerical Example

Let me verify the logic with a comprehensive example:

Accident: Collision due to navigation error

If owner has liability:

  • Charterer training investment: 0 (no incentive)
  • Collision probability: 2%
  • Expected cost: 0.02 × 1,000,000 = 20,000
  • Owner charges lease premium: +20,000
  • Total charterer cost: 1,000,000 + 20,000 = 1,020,000

If charterer has liability:

  • Charterer training investment: 5,000 (reduces probability to 0.5%)
  • Collision probability: 0.5%
  • Expected cost: 0.005 × 1,000,000 = 5,000
  • Lease price reduction: -10,000 (owner's risk reduced)
  • Total charterer cost: 990,000 + 5,000 + 5,000 = 1,000,000

Charterer liability saves 20,000 through better incentives.

Step 10: Practical Implementation

Typical bareboat charter agreements allocate:

To charterer:

  • All operational accidents
  • Damage during charter period
  • Third-party liabilities from operations
  • Requirement to maintain specified insurance

To owner:

  • Pre-existing defects
  • Structural failures not due to charterer's operations
  • Vessel unseaworthiness at delivery

This allocation maximizes incentives for both parties to minimize risks they can control.

Key Assumptions:

  • Parties are rational and respond to liability incentives
  • Contract price adjusts to reflect risk allocation
  • Goal is to minimize total social costs (Kaldor-Hicks efficiency)
  • Moral hazard is significant (behavior affects accident probability)
  • Insurance is available but subject to moral hazard problems

How conclusions change:

  • If monitoring can eliminate moral hazard → liability allocation less important for incentives
  • If one party is much more risk-averse → may justify that party bearing less risk even if they could prevent it
  • If insurance markets are perfect and costless → pure risk-spreading considerations dominate
  • If parties have different access to insurance → party with cheaper insurance access might efficiently bear more risk
▶ Question 15.7: M&A Transaction: Warranties and Indemnities Risk Allocation

Case Background:

You are part of an advisory group assisting the owners of Norsk Teknologi AS in connection with an upcoming acquisition of the company by a larger foreign company, GlobalTech Inc. The acquisition is planned as a Mergers and Acquisitions (M&A) transaction.

Norsk Teknologi AS operates in the development of technological solutions for industrial production and has experienced steady growth over the past ten years. Although the company has good future prospects, the buyers are concerned about several risk factors:

  1. Possible future litigation related to patent claims from competitors about the technology
  2. Possible future decline in demand for the technology the company offers
  3. The cultures of the two companies may prove difficult to integrate
  4. Possible future tax claims because the company's tax planning may have been borderline legal
  5. Possible future EU regulations that could make the technology illegal

Question:

As part of the acquisition process, the parties have begun discussing contract terms. GlobalTech Inc. wants to include a warranties and indemnities clause where the sellers would be liable for indemnification if the above risks materialize. However, the owners of Norsk Teknologi AS are skeptical about assuming significant liability for whether such risks materialize after the acquisition and ask you for advice. What advice would you give? Which risks (if any) should the sellers assume indemnification liability for? Explain using economic theory.

▶ What a Good Answer Should Contain
  1. Use cost-benefit analysis and asymmetric information framework
  2. Distinguish between pre-acquisition risks (seller has information) and post-acquisition risks (buyer has control)
  3. Apply principle that risk should be allocated to party with best information or control
  4. Analyze how warranties solve asymmetric information problem
  5. Show how efficient risk allocation creates Pareto improvements through price adjustments
  6. Use numerical examples to illustrate gains from proper risk allocation
  7. Identify moral hazard problems when risk is misallocated
  8. State assumptions about information and control
▶ Example solution

Framework: Asymmetric Information and Efficient Risk Allocation

The question asks which risks sellers should bear indemnification liability for in an M&A transaction. The economic answer is that sellers should bear liability for risks where they have superior information, while buyers should bear liability for risks they can control post-acquisition.

Step 1: Theory of Asymmetric Information

Sellers know more about the company and its history than buyers. For example, sellers know better whether the company's tax planning has been illegal and how similar the company's technology is to patented technologies.

Without sufficient warranties, sellers have incentives to say that the probability of tax claims and patent litigation is very low. Since buyers cannot trust the information sellers provide, they will likely discount the company's value heavily to compensate for unknown risks.

When warranties and indemnification are included in the contract, this incentive is reduced because sellers are held accountable for the information provided. Buyers can therefore trust the information and will be willing to pay a higher price.

Recommendation for risks with asymmetric information: Sellers should accept indemnification liability for future tax claims and patent litigation stemming from the pre-acquisition period.

Step 2: Numerical Example of Asymmetric Information

Assume the buyer believes there is a 50% probability the company will face a tax claim of 10 million. The seller knows with high certainty whether they will face a tax claim.

If sellers know probability is only 1%:

Expected cost of indemnification to seller: = 0.01 × 10 million = 100,000

Buyer's willingness to pay more with warranty (eliminates their risk): = 0.50 × 10 million = 5 million

Seller gains: 5 million - 100,000 = 4.9 million by providing warranty

Conclusion: Sellers profit from taking on indemnification liability when they have better information showing risk is lower than buyer believes.

Step 3: Efficient Risk Allocation Through Control

It is optimal for buyers to have liability for risks they can control. This applies to:

  • Risk 2: Future decline in demand for the technology the company offers
  • Risk 3: Difficulty integrating cultures of the two companies
  • Risk 5: Possible future EU regulations making the technology illegal

The argument is similar to the bareboat charter maintenance question: allocate responsibility to the party that can most cost-effectively manage the risk.

Step 4: Cost-Benefit Analysis for Risk 2 (Demand Decline)

Costs of buyer liability Benefits of buyer liability
Buyer spends more resources adapting technology to market demand Decline in demand is smaller

Since the buyer pays both the cost of adaptation and receives the benefit of reduced demand decline, they will choose the adaptation that maximizes total surplus.

If the seller has liability, the buyer only pays the cost of adaptation but does not benefit from reduced demand decline (since the seller will indemnify them regardless). The buyer will therefore not adapt the technology, and total costs will increase.

Step 5: Numerical Example for Demand Risk

Assume decline in future demand reduces profit by 100 if buyer does not adapt the technology.

If buyer adapts technology:

  • Decline in profit: only 50
  • Adaptation cost: 10
  • Net benefit of adaptation: 100 - 50 - 10 = 40

Adaptation is Kaldor-Hicks efficient (saves 40).

If seller has liability:

  • Buyer pays 10 for adaptation but receives nothing in return (seller indemnifies full 100)
  • Buyer will not adapt
  • Total cost: 100

If buyer has liability:

  • Buyer pays 10 to save 50
  • Buyer adapts
  • Total cost: 50 + 10 = 60
  • Social saving: 40

Step 6: Price Adjustment Creates Pareto Improvement

Assume company price would be 1,000 if seller has liability for demand decline.

By transferring liability to buyer and reducing price to 920, both parties benefit:

Buyer:

  • Pays: 920 + 50 (reduced demand) + 10 (adaptation) = 980
  • Saves: 1,000 - 980 = 20 (better off)

Seller:

  • Receives: 920
  • Previously: 1,000 - 100 (indemnification) = 900
  • Gains: 920 - 900 = 20 (better off)

Both parties are better off by 20—a Pareto improvement.

Step 7: Analysis of Each Risk

Risks sellers should bear indemnification liability for:

Risk 1: Patent litigation from competitors

  • Reasoning: Sellers have superior information about whether their technology infringes on competitors' patents
  • Mechanism: Warranty makes seller's information credible, increases buyer's willingness to pay
  • Type: Asymmetric information problem

Risk 4: Tax claims from borderline legal tax planning

  • Reasoning: Sellers know with high certainty whether tax planning was legal
  • Mechanism: Indemnification eliminates buyer's uncertainty about historical tax compliance
  • Type: Asymmetric information problem

Risks buyers should bear liability for:

Risk 2: Future decline in demand

  • Reasoning: Buyer can modify technology to match market demand
  • Mechanism: Buyer internalizes costs and benefits of adaptation, chooses efficient level
  • Type: Moral hazard if seller has liability (buyer won't adapt)

Risk 3: Cultural integration difficulties

  • Reasoning: Buyer controls how integration is conducted
  • Mechanism: Buyer can choose integration methods cost-effectively
  • Type: Moral hazard if seller has liability (buyer won't invest in integration)

Risk 5: Future EU regulations

  • Reasoning: Buyer can modify technology to comply with new regulations
  • Mechanism: These are post-acquisition regulatory changes affecting buyer's future operations
  • Type: Buyer has control over adaptation and compliance

Step 8: Summary Table

Risk Liable Party Economic Rationale
1. Patent litigation Seller Superior information about pre-acquisition IP issues
2. Demand decline Buyer Can adapt technology to market
3. Cultural integration Buyer Controls integration process
4. Tax claims Seller Knows historical tax compliance
5. EU regulations Buyer Can adapt to new regulations

Step 9: Key Insight About Contract Efficiency

Both parties should agree on all contract clauses except price. A clause that leads to seller taking on more costs (indemnification liability) should not make sellers necessarily skeptical—as long as they are compensated through higher price.

The optimal allocation maximizes total surplus, which benefits both parties through appropriate price adjustment. Efficient contracts:

  1. Allocate risk to party with best information (asymmetric information risks)
  2. Allocate risk to party with best control (post-acquisition operational risks)

Step 10: Assumptions and Robustness

Key assumptions:

  • Sellers have significantly better information about pre-acquisition tax and IP issues than buyers
  • Buyers have control over post-acquisition technology adaptation and integration
  • Contract price can adjust to reflect risk allocation
  • Warranties are enforceable (sellers will actually pay if risks materialize)
  • Buyers will invest in due diligence even with warranties (not complete moral hazard)

How conclusions change:

If sellers don't have superior information about tax/patent risks:

  • No asymmetric information benefit from warranties
  • Might be more efficient to share these risks or use insurance

If buyers cannot control technology adaptation:

  • Less clear that buyer should bear demand/regulatory risks
  • Might justify some seller involvement or shared responsibility

If warranty enforcement is costly or uncertain:

  • Benefits of warranties reduced
  • Might prefer alternative mechanisms (escrow, earn-outs)

If information asymmetry is severe and buyers can't verify seller claims:

  • May need additional mechanisms beyond warranties
  • Representation and warranty insurance
  • Delayed payments contingent on outcomes

Conclusion

Sellers should accept indemnification liability for Risks 1 and 4 (patent litigation and tax claims) because they have superior information that warranties make credible. Buyers should bear liability for Risks 2, 3, and 5 (demand, integration, regulations) because they control the post-acquisition actions that affect these outcomes. This allocation maximizes total surplus and allows both parties to benefit through appropriate price adjustment.

TryggBolig AS discovers that some customers systematically exaggerate losses to obtain higher compensation. Discuss how the insurance company should design its responses and control procedures to reduce the extent of such behavior.

▶ Question 15.9: Insurance Contract: Mandatory Security Lock Requirement

The company TryggBolig AS offers home contents insurance to private individuals. In recent years, more customers have chosen to go without insurance, while the number of large damage payouts has increased. The industry also reports increasing insurance fraud, particularly in claimed thefts and fire damage.

Question:

TryggBolig AS is considering introducing a clause in the insurance contract requiring all customers to install an FG-approved security lock at a cost of at least 7,000 kroner. Installation will in many cases require replacement of the door. What factors determine whether TryggBolig should include such a clause? Discuss using economic theory.

▶ What a Good Answer Should Contain

[TO BE COMPLETED]

▶ Example solution

[SOLUTION TO BE COMPLETED]

▶ Question 15.10: Electricity Contract: Pricing Structure for Industrial Customer

Case Background:

NordEnergi AS is a medium-sized electricity supplier selling power to both households and businesses. In 2024, the company experienced large fluctuations in electricity prices due to increased exports, varying reservoir inflows, and unpredictable weather conditions. Several customers and industrial companies believe prices have been unreasonably high, and there has been debate about whether today's market model for electricity functions as intended.

Question:

A large industrial conglomerate, NordMetall, wants to negotiate a separate electricity agreement with NordEnergi. The customer demands predictable prices but also wants to benefit from low spot prices when the market falls. You represent NordEnergi and must propose a price structure for the agreement — for example, a fixed-price agreement, a spot-price agreement, or a combination. Which price structure would you propose, and why? Discuss using economic theory.

▶ What a Good Answer Should Contain

[TO BE COMPLETED]

▶ Example solution

[SOLUTION TO BE COMPLETED]

The solution should discuss:

  • Risk allocation between supplier and customer
  • Trade-off between price certainty and flexibility
  • Customer characteristics: Can NordMetall adjust consumption in response to price signals?
  • Moral hazard: Does spot pricing give better consumption incentives?
  • Supplier's hedging costs and risk premium
  • Hybrid contract design: Partial fixed price + partial spot price
  • Numerical example showing payoffs under different price scenarios

Partial notes from source:

  • Assume electricity price is 0.40 on cheap days and 1.20 kr/kWh on expensive days
  • Company can choose to use 100,000 kWh or 50,000 kWh per day
  • First 50 MWh generates 1.50 kr/kWh in revenue, next generates 0.60 kr/kWh
