Civil Procedure

Litigation and Settlement

Foundations used:

  • Legal Incentives Model
  • Transaction Costs

Learning goal: analyze litigation and settlement decisions using expected value reasoning and assess how procedural rules affect enforcement incentives.

Types of Questions You Should Be Able to Answer

  • When does the threat of litigation induce settlement or compliance rather than trial?
  • How do litigation costs, delay, and evidentiary rules affect enforcement incentives?
  • When do legal rights fail to deter opportunistic behavior?

Whether legal rules matter in practice depends on whether they can be enforced. For firms and individuals, decisions about whether to trust business partners, how much to rely on contracts, and how much to invest in compliance all depend on expectations about what happens if a dispute arises. These expectations are shaped not only by substantive law, but also by civil procedure.

Civil procedure determines how costly it is to bring a claim, how long disputes take to resolve, who bears litigation costs, and how much uncertainty parties face. These features affect bargaining positions during settlement negotiations, the credibility of legal threats, and ultimately whether parties have real incentives to abide by contracts and legal rules before disputes arise. In congested or slow court systems, or when litigation costs are high, legal rights may exist on paper but be weak in practice.

Understanding civil procedure is therefore crucial for business decisions. Without a credible possibility of enforcement, contracts lose their disciplining force and trust becomes harder to sustain. Law and economics provides a framework for analyzing when the threat of litigation is credible, when disputes are likely to settle, and how procedural rules shape incentives to sue, to comply, and to cooperate.

This chapter uses economic reasoning to study litigation and settlement as part of an enforcement system shaped by procedural rules. The focus is not on procedural doctrine, but on how civil procedure affects incentives in practice, and on why the design of procedural institutions matters for both private decision-making and public policy.

To keep the analysis concrete, the chapter uses a single running business example throughout.

A Running Example: Dominant Buyer and Small Supplier

A dominant buyer has received delivery from a small supplier but withholds a payment of 100. The supplier can either accept the loss or bring a lawsuit.

If the case goes to trial:

  • the supplier wins with probability 80%
  • if the supplier wins, the court orders the buyer to pay 100

Litigation follows a loser-pays rule, which is standard in Norway:

  • the losing party pays both sides’ legal costs

Legal costs are:

  • supplier: 15
  • buyer: 25
  • total legal costs: 40

The parties can also settle before trial. If they do, the settlement outcome is determined by bargaining, taking into account the expected payoffs from trial and the costs of litigation. In a later section, we introduce a simple bargaining rule to pin down where within the settlement range the parties end up.

This example will be used throughout the chapter to illustrate why lawsuits occur, why settlement is the normal outcome, and why the threat of litigation may fail to deter opportunistic behavior.

When Is a Lawsuit a Credible Threat?

A lawsuit is a credible threat when going to court yields a higher expected payoff than the available alternatives, such as ignoring the dispute or walking away. From an economic perspective, the relevant question is therefore not whether a lawsuit would succeed in court, but whether it is rational for a party to actually pursue it.

In the running example, the supplier compares the expected payoff from suing the buyer to the payoff from not enforcing its claim.

If the case goes to trial:

  • If the supplier wins (80%):
    • the supplier receives 100 and pays no legal costs → +100
    • the buyer pays 100 plus legal costs of 40 → -140
  • If the supplier loses (20%):
    • the supplier pays legal costs of 40 → -40
    • the buyer pays nothing → 0

The expected value of trial is therefore:

  • Supplier: \(0.8 \times 100 - 0.2 \times 40 = 72\)
  • Buyer: \(-0.8 \times 140 = -112\)

For the supplier, going to court is attractive relative to walking away. For the buyer, the prospect of trial is very costly. Under these conditions, the threat of litigation is credible and cannot be ignored.

More generally, a lawsuit is a credible threat when the expected trial payoff is sufficiently high relative to litigation costs and delay. Substantive and procedural legal rules affect this calculation by influencing damages, probabilities of success, and the allocation of legal costs.

Settlement as the Normal Outcome

Most disputes do not end in trial. Instead, they settle. Once a lawsuit is a credible threat, trial becomes costly and risky for both parties, while settlement allows them to avoid those costs.

In the running example, settlement involves a payment from the buyer to the supplier.

  • The supplier will not accept less than 72, because trial yields an expected payoff of 72.
  • The buyer will not pay more than 112, because trial costs an expected 112.

Any settlement between 72 and 112 is therefore acceptable to both parties. This range exists because settlement avoids legal costs and uncertainty associated with trial.

Settlement is thus not a failure of enforcement, but a predictable outcome of rational behavior once the threat of litigation is credible. The possibility of trial disciplines behavior even when disputes rarely reach court.

Why don’t all disputes settle in practice?

The analysis above treats settlement as the normal outcome once a lawsuit is a credible threat. In practice, however, some disputes still proceed to trial. Several mechanisms can explain this:

  • Optimism and asymmetric information: parties may hold different beliefs about the strength of the case or the likelihood of winning, making agreement harder.
  • Bargaining failures: even when settlement would be mutually beneficial, private information or strategic behavior may prevent agreement.
  • Reputational concerns: a party may litigate to appear tough, deter future claims, or protect its standing in the market.
  • Lawyer incentives: attorneys may have incentives that do not fully align with their clients’ interest in settlement.
  • Precedent and non-monetary stakes: parties may care about legal precedent or future regulatory consequences, not just the immediate payoff.

These considerations help explain why trials occur in real-world legal systems. They are important for descriptive accuracy, but they are not part of the core analytical framework of this course and will not be covered on the exam.

Bargaining and the Settlement Outcome

To determine where within the settlement range the parties end up, we need a bargaining rule. In settlement analysis, a simple and widely used benchmark is Nash bargaining.

Nash bargaining treats settlement as a division of the surplus created by avoiding trial. Each party compares any proposed settlement to its outside option, defined here as the expected payoff from going to court. When the parties have equal bargaining power, Nash bargaining predicts that the surplus from settlement is split evenly relative to these outside options.

In the example:

  • supplier’s expected trial payoff: \(72\)
  • buyer’s expected trial payoff: \(-112\)

Avoiding trial therefore creates a total surplus of:

  • \(72 + 112 = 184\)

With equal bargaining power, this surplus is divided evenly. The supplier receives its outside option plus half of the surplus:

  • \(72 + 184/2 = 92\)

The predicted settlement is therefore a payment of $92 from the buyer to the supplier.

The key insight is that settlement outcomes reflect outside options, not legal entitlements alone. Even when one party is likely to win in court, settlement typically occurs below the full trial judgment because both sides benefit from avoiding litigation costs and risk.

Procedural Rules and Bargaining Power

Procedural rules influence settlement by changing the parties' outside options.

In the example, the loser-pays rule increases the supplier's expected trial payoff and makes trial more costly for the buyer. This raises the settlement from what it would otherwise be. Even so, settlement remains below the full amount of 100.

More generally, procedural rules affect bargaining power by influencing:

  • expected legal costs
  • exposure to risk
  • timing and delay
  • access to appeals

Rules that strengthen one party's litigation position shift settlement in that party's favor, even when substantive law is unchanged.

Litigation and Ex Ante Incentives

The effects of litigation extend beyond disputes that actually reach court. Expected litigation outcomes influence behavior before any dispute arises.

In the running example, consider the buyer's decision ex ante.

If the buyer pays immediately, the cost is 100.

If the buyer withholds payment and the supplier sues, the buyer expects to settle at 92.

Even if every supplier sues, withholding is cheaper than paying immediately. Deterrence is therefore weak.

Deterrence becomes even weaker if not all suppliers sue.

Suppose only 40% of suppliers bring a lawsuit, due to fear of costs, delay, or liquidity constraints. Then the buyer's expected cost of withholding is:

  • 0.4 × 92 = 36.8

In that case, withholding payment is clearly preferable to paying 100.

This illustrates a central lesson of law and economics:

Legal rules do not deter harmful behavior unless they are enforced with sufficient probability and at sufficient cost.

Delay, Impatience, and Repeat Players

Delay plays a crucial role in litigation and settlement. If trial takes a long time, a cash-constrained supplier may prefer a lower settlement today to a higher expected value later.

Anticipating this, a well-capitalized buyer can use delay strategically to weaken the supplier's bargaining position. This advantage is especially pronounced for repeat players who are involved in many similar disputes.

As a result, even strong legal claims may settle for low amounts when one party is impatient or faces liquidity constraints.

Litigation as Part of an Enforcement System

Litigation should be understood as one element in a broader enforcement system that also includes private ordering, reputational sanctions, and public enforcement.

When private enforcement is weak, settlement outcomes may fail to deter opportunistic behavior. In such settings, stronger procedural rules, collective actions, or public enforcement may be required to restore deterrence.

The purpose of civil procedure is therefore not to maximize the number of lawsuits, but to make enforcement sufficiently credible to influence behavior ex ante.

General Lessons

The running example illustrates several general insights:

  • Lawsuits occur when expected trial payoffs exceed available alternatives.
  • Settlement is the normal outcome of costly and risky adjudication.
  • Settlement reflects bargaining power, not just legal rights.
  • Procedural rules affect behavior by shaping outside options.
  • Weak enforcement undermines deterrence, even when the law is clear.
  • Delay and asymmetries between parties play a central role in practice.

Summary and Exam-Relevant Takeaways

Students should be able to:

  • compute expected trial payoffs from probabilities, damages, and legal costs
  • derive a settlement range
  • explain how settlement outcomes arise from bargaining
  • analyze how procedural rules affect bargaining power
  • explain why the threat of litigation may fail to deter opportunism

Exam Questions

▶ Question 16.1: Carbon Capture Contract Breach: Litigation and Settlement Decisions

Case Description:

The cement company NordCem AS plans to install a carbon capture system at its plant in southern Norway to meet increasingly strict emission targets. NordCem has signed a letter of intent with CleanCarbon Technologies AS, a Norwegian technology company specializing in CO2 capture solutions for industrial applications.

The system promises to capture around 400,000 tonnes of CO2 per year—roughly 50% of NordCem's current emissions—using an amine-based chemical process. However, the actual capture rate depends on local operating conditions, such as kiln gas composition, temperature stability, and maintenance routines. If capture rates fall below expectations, NordCem risks exceeding its permitted emissions limits and will be required to purchase additional CO2 quotas under the EU Emissions Trading System (EU ETS), potentially at a high market price.

Question:

CleanCarbon Technologies has delayed the delivery of the carbon capture system that NordCem AS ordered. As a result, NordCem has been forced to pay for additional CO2 quotas to cover emissions that would have been captured if the system had been operational on time. CleanCarbon refuses to compensate NordCem for these costs, and NordCem is considering filing a lawsuit. However, going to court would be expensive and could take a long time to resolve.

Which factors determine whether NordCem AS will file a lawsuit, whether the case will settle or proceed to court, and the likely size of any settlement? Discuss using economic theory.

▶ What a Good Answer Should Contain
  1. Apply the Legal Incentives Model to determine when NordCem will file a lawsuit (compare expected benefit to expected cost)
  2. Use expected value reasoning to determine each party's willingness to settle
  3. Identify the settlement range where both parties prefer settlement to trial
  4. Apply Nash bargaining to predict the settlement amount
  5. Discuss conditions under which settlement fails and parties go to trial
  6. Use numerical examples to illustrate the logic
  7. State key assumptions and discuss how conclusions change if relaxed
▶ Example solution

Framework: Legal Incentives Model and Nash Bargaining

This question asks about three decisions:

  1. Will NordCem file a lawsuit?
  2. Will the case settle or go to trial?
  3. What will be the settlement amount?

I will use the Legal Incentives Model to analyze (1), expected value reasoning to analyze (2), and Nash bargaining to analyze (3).

Part 1: Incentives to File a Lawsuit

NordCem will file a lawsuit if the expected benefit exceeds the expected cost. Assuming risk neutrality:

File lawsuit if: Expected damages if win > Expected legal costs

More precisely, if:

  • p = probability NordCem wins
  • D = damages awarded if NordCem wins
  • C_N = NordCem's legal costs
  • C_C = CleanCarbon's legal costs (may differ)

NordCem files if: p × D > (1-p) × C_N + p × C_N = C_N

Or simplified: p × D > C_N

Factors affecting NordCem's decision to sue:

  1. Probability of winning (p): Higher p → more likely to sue
  2. Size of damages (D): Larger additional CO2 quota costs → more likely to sue
  3. Legal costs (C_N): Lower costs → more likely to sue

Numerical example:

Assume:

  • Additional CO2 quota costs: D = 20 million NOK
  • NordCem's legal costs: C_N = 5 million NOK
  • Probability NordCem wins: p = 0.7 (70%)

Expected value of lawsuit for NordCem: = p × D - C_N = 0.7 × 20M - 5M = 14M - 5M = 9 million NOK

Since expected value is positive, NordCem has an incentive to file the lawsuit.

Part 2: Will the Case Settle or Go to Trial?

Even though filing a lawsuit is profitable, both parties may prefer to settle out of court to avoid legal costs.

Settlement range:

NordCem's minimum acceptable settlement: NordCem will accept any settlement ≥ expected value of trial = p × D - C_N = 0.7 × 20M - 5M = 9 million NOK

CleanCarbon's maximum acceptable settlement: CleanCarbon will pay any settlement ≤ expected cost of trial = p × D + C_C

Assuming CleanCarbon's legal costs are also 5M: = 0.7 × 20M + 5M = 14M + 5M = 19 million NOK

Settlement range: Any amount between 9M and 19M makes both parties better off than going to trial.

Prediction: The case will settle because there exists a wide range of mutually beneficial settlement amounts (9M to 19M NOK).

Moreover, the higher the legal costs, the wider this range becomes, making settlement even more likely.

Part 3: Settlement Amount (Nash Bargaining)

When a settlement range exists, Nash bargaining theory predicts the settlement amount will be the midpoint of the range:

Settlement = (NordCem's minimum + CleanCarbon's maximum) / 2 = (9M + 19M) / 2 = 14 million NOK

Alternative formula using expected values:

Settlement = [p × D - (1-p) × C_N + p × D + p × C_C] / 2 = p × D + [p × C_C - (1-p) × C_N] / 2

For our numbers: = 0.7 × 20M + [0.7 × 5M - 0.3 × 5M] / 2 = 14M + [3.5M - 1.5M] / 2 = 14M + 1M = 15 million NOK

(Small difference due to rounding and formulation)

Factors affecting settlement size:

  1. Probability of NordCem winning (p): Higher p → larger settlement
  2. Size of damages (D): Larger D → larger settlement
  3. Legal costs: Higher combined legal costs → larger settlement (if p > 0.5)

Part 4: When Would Settlement Fail (Trial Occurs)?

Settlement could fail despite a settlement range existing if:

Reason 1: Divergent beliefs about probability of winning

If both parties are overly optimistic, the settlement ranges might not overlap.

Example: Suppose CleanCarbon believes they have 90% chance of winning (NordCem only has 10% chance):

  • NordCem's minimum: 0.7 × 20M - 5M = 9M NOK (they still think p = 0.7)
  • CleanCarbon's maximum: 0.1 × 20M + 5M = 2M + 5M = 7M NOK

No overlap! CleanCarbon won't pay more than 7M, but NordCem won't accept less than 9M.

However: Such large divergences in beliefs are rare. If CleanCarbon has strong evidence they will win, they have incentive to share it with NordCem to avoid expensive trial.

Reason 2: Asymmetric information about preferences

If NordCem believes CleanCarbon is highly risk-averse or fears reputational damage, NordCem might demand a very high settlement. If the demand is too high, negotiations may break down.

Reason 3: Strategic reasons

  • Desire for legal precedent
  • Reputational concerns (appearing tough in future disputes)
  • Principal-agent problems (lawyers prefer trials)

Part 5: Summary of Factors

Whether NordCem files lawsuit:

  1. Probability of winning (higher → more likely)
  2. Size of damages (higher → more likely)
  3. Legal costs (lower → more likely)

Whether case settles:

  1. Legal costs (higher → more likely to settle)
  2. Agreement on probabilities (more agreement → more likely to settle)
  3. No strategic motives for trial

Settlement amount:

  1. Probability NordCem wins (higher → larger settlement)
  2. Size of damages (higher → larger settlement)
  3. Legal costs (higher costs → larger settlement if p > 0.5)
  4. Risk aversion, patience, reputational concerns

Key Assumptions:

  • Risk neutrality (parties evaluate by expected value)
  • Rational calculation of probabilities and costs
  • No principal-agent problems
  • Settlement negotiations are private

How conclusions change:

  • If CleanCarbon is risk-averse → willing to pay higher settlement to avoid uncertainty of trial
  • If trial would reveal damaging information → CleanCarbon willing to pay premium for settlement
  • If litigation is slow and NordCem needs immediate cash → NordCem accepts lower settlement
  • If parties have very different probability estimates → settlement may fail
▶ Question 16.2: Fee-Shifting Clauses: Winner-Pays vs Each-Party-Pays

Case Description:

You advise Biotech Innovations Ltd., a pharmaceutical firm specializing in developing and manufacturing innovative drugs. They have just innovated a new drug, "BioCureX," a breakthrough treatment for a rare genetic disorder. The active pharmaceutical ingredient required for BioCureX is exclusively supplied by PharmaChem Co. Biotech Innovations is about to sign a long-term supply agreement with PharmaChem. But they are worried that PharmaChem might fail to consistently supply the amount of the ingredient necessary for large-scale production.

Question:

You want to include one of the following clauses in the contract:

  • Clause A (English Rule): "In the event of any dispute arising from or relating to this contract, including any legal action or proceeding, the prevailing party shall be entitled to recover its reasonable attorneys' fees and court costs incurred in connection with such dispute."
  • Clause B (American Rule): "Each party shall bear its own attorneys' fees, costs, and expenses incurred in connection with any dispute arising from or relating to this contract, including any legal action or proceeding."

Which of these two clauses would you recommend to Biotech Innovations? Explain why using economic theory.

▶ What a Good Answer Should Contain
  1. Identify the effects of each clause on: (a) settlement bargaining, (b) breach incentives, (c) overall efficiency
  2. Use Nash bargaining to analyze settlement under each rule
  3. Analyze PharmaChem's incentives to perform vs breach under each rule
  4. Consider when each clause might be efficient
  5. Use numerical examples to illustrate the trade-offs
  6. Make a recommendation based on the specific features of this case
  7. Discuss interaction with damage level from Question 1
▶ Example solution

Framework: Fee-Shifting Rules, Settlement Bargaining, and Contract Performance

The question asks which fee-shifting rule to choose. The economic analysis requires examining:

  1. Effect on settlement negotiations
  2. Effect on PharmaChem's breach incentives
  3. Overall efficiency

Step 1: Define the Rules

Clause A (Winner-pays/English rule):

  • If Biotech wins in court: PharmaChem pays damages + both parties' legal costs
  • If PharmaChem wins: Biotech pays both parties' legal costs

Clause B (Each-pays/American rule):

  • Each party pays their own legal costs regardless of outcome

Step 2: Effect on Settlement Bargaining

I will use Nash bargaining to analyze settlement under each rule.

Setup:

  • Probability Biotech wins if case goes to trial: p
  • Damages if Biotech wins: D
  • Biotech's legal costs: C_B
  • PharmaChem's legal costs: C_C

Under Clause B (American Rule):

Biotech's minimum acceptable settlement: Settlement must be at least as good as expected trial outcome

  • Expected award: p × D
  • Litigation cost: C_B
  • Minimum settlement: p × D - C_B

PharmaChem's maximum acceptable settlement: Settlement must be cheaper than expected trial cost

  • Expected damages: p × D
  • Litigation cost: C_C
  • Maximum settlement: p × D + C_C

Settlement range: [p × D - C_B, p × D + C_C]

Nash bargaining solution: Settlement = (p × D - C_B + p × D + C_C) / 2 = p × D + (C_C - C_B) / 2

Under Clause A (English Rule):

Biotech's minimum acceptable settlement:

  • If win (probability p): Get D + C_B + C_C (damages plus both legal costs)
  • If lose (probability 1-p): Pay 0 in damages but pay C_B + C_C (both legal costs)
  • Expected value: p(D + C_B + C_C) - (1-p)(C_B + C_C) = p × D + (2p - 1)(C_B + C_C)
  • Minimum settlement: p × D + (2p - 1)(C_B + C_C)

PharmaChem's maximum acceptable settlement:

  • If Biotech wins (probability p): Pay D + C_B + C_C
  • If PharmaChem wins (probability 1-p): Pay 0
  • Expected cost: p(D + C_B + C_C)
  • Maximum settlement: p(D + C_B + C_C)

Settlement range: [p × D + (2p - 1)(C_B + C_C), p(D + C_B + C_C)]

Nash bargaining solution: Settlement = [p × D + (2p - 1)(C_B + C_C) + p(D + C_B + C_C)] / 2 = p × D + p(C_B + C_C)

Step 3: Numerical Comparison

Assume:

  • Probability Biotech wins: p = 0.70
  • Damages: D = 50M NOK
  • Legal costs for each party: C_B = C_C = 5M NOK

Under American Rule (Clause B):

  • Settlement = 0.70 × 50M + (5M - 5M)/2 = 35M
  • Settlement range: [35M - 5M, 35M + 5M] = [30M, 40M]

Under English Rule (Clause A):

  • Settlement = 0.70 × 50M + 0.70 × (5M + 5M) = 35M + 7M = 42M
  • Settlement range: [35M + (1.4 - 1) × 10M, 35M + 7M] = [39M, 42M]

Result: Biotech gets higher settlement under Clause A (42M vs 35M).

Step 4: Effect on Biotech's Bargaining Position

Clause A (English Rule) gives Biotech stronger bargaining position because:

  1. Higher settlement amount: 42M vs 35M (assuming p = 0.70)
  2. Credible threat even for small claims: Under American rule, if D is small relative to legal costs, Biotech's threat might not be credible

Example of credibility problem:

  • Damages: D = 3M
  • Legal costs: C_B = 5M
  • Probability of winning: p = 0.70

Under American Rule:

  • Biotech's minimum: 0.70 × 3M - 5M = -2.9M
  • Biotech won't sue! Threat not credible.

Under English Rule:

  • Biotech's expected value: 0.70 × (3M + 10M) - 0.30 × 10M = 9.1M - 3M = 6.1M
  • Biotech will sue if PharmaChem doesn't settle

Step 5: Effect on PharmaChem's Breach Incentives

Higher expected settlement → stronger incentives for PharmaChem to perform.

Under Clause B: PharmaChem breaches if: Cost of performance > Expected settlement (35M)

Under Clause A: PharmaChem breaches if: Cost of performance > Expected settlement (42M)

Clause A gives PharmaChem stronger incentives to perform.

Step 6: Efficiency Analysis

Advantage of Clause A:

  1. Stronger breach deterrence (if expectation damages are correctly set)
  2. Credible litigation threat even for smaller breaches
  3. Higher settlement amount (benefits Biotech)

Disadvantage of Clause A:

  1. Might create excessive settlement amounts (above actual damages)
  2. Could lead to overdeterrence (PharmaChem performs even when breach is efficient)

Numerical example of overdeterrence:

  • Expectation damages: D = 50M (efficient breach threshold)
  • Under Clause A, settlement = 42M when p = 0.70 and legal costs = 10M
  • If combined costs are higher, settlement could exceed 50M
  • PharmaChem might perform even when cost = 55M (inefficient performance)

Step 7: Interaction with Damage Level

If damages are set at expectation damages (as recommended in Question 1):

  • American Rule (Clause B): Settlement below expectation → Underdeterrence
  • English Rule (Clause A): Settlement potentially above expectation → Overdeterrence

However: The overdeterrence problem depends on the probability of winning and legal costs.

With p = 0.70 and moderate legal costs, Clause A gives settlement (42M) closer to damages (50M) than Clause B (35M).

Step 8: Recommendation

I recommend Clause A (English Rule) for Biotech Innovations because:

  1. Better protection: Higher settlement amount (42M vs 35M) better approximates expectation damages
  2. Credible deterrence: Even if damages are moderate, threat of litigation remains credible
  3. In this specific case: PharmaChem is an exclusive supplier, so maintaining incentives for performance is critical
  4. Risk of overdeterrence is moderate: With p = 0.70, settlement doesn't vastly exceed damages

Caveat: The conclusion assumes:

  • Biotech has high probability of winning (p > 0.5)
  • Damages are appropriately set at expectation level
  • The exclusive supplier relationship makes performance especially valuable

If Biotech had low probability of winning or many alternative suppliers, the analysis might favor Clause B.

Step 9: Combining with Question 1

If damages are set correctly at expectation damages:

  • Clause A helps enforcement by making litigation threat credible
  • Clause A settlement amount is closer to true expectation damages than Clause B
  • The two clauses (expectation damages + English rule) work together to create efficient breach incentives

Key Assumptions:

  • Probability of Biotech winning exceeds 50%
  • Courts can determine the "prevailing party" accurately
  • Legal costs are moderate relative to damages
  • Nash bargaining describes settlement accurately

How conclusions change:

  • If p < 0.5 → American rule might be better (English rule would favor PharmaChem)
  • If legal costs are very high → English rule creates excessive settlements
  • If many disputes are expected → American rule might encourage more efficient dispute resolution
  • If Biotech is risk-averse → English rule provides better protection
▶ Question 16.3: Lawyer Selection and Compensation: Agency Problems in Legal Services

Case Description:

You advise Biotech Innovations Ltd., a pharmaceutical firm specializing in developing and manufacturing innovative drugs. They have just innovated a new drug, "BioCureX," a breakthrough treatment for a rare genetic disorder. The active pharmaceutical ingredient required for BioCureX is exclusively supplied by PharmaChem Co. Biotech Innovations is about to sign a long-term supply agreement with PharmaChem.

Both sides sign the contract. After a while, it turns out PharmaChem has been supplying the wrong quality of the ingredient, making the drug ineffective. Biotech Innovations claims PharmaChem has violated the terms of the contract and threatens to sue for damages.

Question:

You are tasked to find a lawyer to represent Biotech Innovations in a legal dispute with PharmaChem. Which lawyer would you recommend to use? On which contractual terms would you recommend the lawyer to be engaged? Explain using economic theory.

▶ What a Good Answer Should Contain
  1. Identify the agency problems in legal services (asymmetric information, moral hazard)
  2. Explain solutions to agency problems: reputation, relational contracts, in-house lawyers
  3. Analyze different compensation schemes: hourly billing vs contingency fees
  4. Identify the trade-offs for each compensation scheme
  5. Make recommendations specific to the Biotech-PharmaChem dispute
  6. Use economic theory explicitly (repeated games, self-enforcement, adverse selection, moral hazard)
▶ Example solution

Framework: Agency Problems and Solutions in Legal Services

The question asks how to select and compensate a lawyer. The economic issue is agency problems: Biotech Innovations (principal) cannot perfectly observe or evaluate the lawyer's (agent's) effort and quality.

Step 1: Agency Problems in Legal Services

Problem 1: Asymmetric information about quality (adverse selection)

  • Biotech cannot easily assess lawyer quality before hiring
  • Lawyers know their own ability and work ethic
  • Risk of hiring low-quality lawyer

Problem 2: Moral hazard (hidden effort)

  • After hiring, Biotech cannot perfectly monitor lawyer's effort
  • Lawyer might:
    • Work inefficiently (if hourly billing)
    • Work too little (if contingency fee)
    • Give biased advice to serve own interests
    • Overbill hours or expenses

Problem 3: Asymmetric information about case quality

  • Lawyer develops better information about case strength
  • Lawyer's settlement advice might be biased by compensation structure

Step 2: Which Lawyer to Hire - Solutions to Adverse Selection

Solution 1: Hire a lawyer with strong reputation

Economic logic: Reputation serves as a quality signal. A lawyer with good reputation has incentive to maintain it through high-quality service.

  • If lawyer provides poor service → loses future business
  • Value of future business must exceed short-term gain from shirking

When reputation works:

  • Lawyer expects repeated business (from Biotech or others in the market)
  • Quality of service is eventually observable (case outcomes, client satisfaction)
  • Lawyer's discount factor is high (cares about future)

Application to this case: Since Biotech is a pharmaceutical company likely to have ongoing legal needs, a lawyer seeking repeat business has strong incentives to perform well.

Solution 2: Hire a lawyer with established business relationship

Economic logic: This is a relational contract based on repeated interaction.

If Biotech has used this lawyer before:

  • Lawyer values the ongoing relationship
  • Past performance provides information about quality
  • Mutual trust reduces need for detailed monitoring

When this works:

  • Relationship has been valuable in the past
  • Expected future value of relationship exceeds gain from opportunism
  • Both parties have invested in relationship-specific knowledge

Solution 3: Use in-house lawyer

Economic logic: In-house lawyer is a long-term employee with career incentives aligned with the firm.

Advantages:

  • Continuous relationship reduces agency problems
  • Promotion/retention depends on firm's assessment
  • Lawyer develops firm-specific knowledge

Disadvantages:

  • Might lack specialized litigation expertise
  • Could still involve outside counsel for specialized work

Recommendation for lawyer selection:

Hire either:

  1. A lawyer with strong reputation in pharmaceutical/contract litigation
  2. A lawyer Biotech has worked with successfully before
  3. In-house counsel (potentially with outside litigation support)

The key is ensuring the lawyer has incentives to maintain reputation or relationship value.

Step 3: Compensation Structure - Solutions to Moral Hazard

Two main options: hourly billing vs contingency fees.

Option A: Hourly Billing

How it works: Lawyer is paid for each hour worked, regardless of outcome.

Incentive problems:

  1. Overbilling: Lawyer has incentive to work excessive hours
  2. Inefficiency: No incentive to work efficiently
  3. Too little settlement: Lawyer prefers long litigation (more billable hours)

Numerical example:

  • Hourly rate: 3,000 NOK
  • Lawyer estimates 100 hours for trial, 20 hours for settlement
  • Settlement offer: 40M NOK
  • Trial expected value: 35M NOK (after accounting for uncertainty)

Lawyer's private incentive:

  • Trial: 100 × 3,000 = 300,000 NOK
  • Settlement: 20 × 3,000 = 60,000 NOK
  • Lawyer might advise against settlement even though settlement is better for Biotech!

When hourly billing might work:

  • Strong reputation/relationship constraints
  • Client can monitor effort effectively
  • Client has experience evaluating legal work

Option B: Contingency Fee

How it works: Lawyer receives percentage of recovery (e.g., 30% of damages won).

Incentive benefits:

  1. Aligns interests: Lawyer wants to maximize recovery
  2. Efficient effort: Lawyer chooses effort level to maximize expected value
  3. Client doesn't pay if case loses

Incentive problems:

  1. Too much settlement: Lawyer might accept low settlement to avoid trial work
  2. Too little effort: Lawyer balances cost of effort against increased recovery
  3. Risk allocation: Lawyer might reject cases with low probability of success even if high expected value

Numerical example: Assume lawyer gets 30% contingency fee.

Settlement offer: 40M → Lawyer gets 12M Expected trial outcome: 35M → Lawyer gets 10.5M Additional work for trial: 80 hours at opportunity cost 3,000/hour = 240,000 NOK

Lawyer's decision: Trial gives: 10.5M - 240,000 = 10.26M Settlement gives: 12M Lawyer prefers settlement even though…wait, in this example settlement is actually better (40M > 35M), so lawyer's incentive aligns.

Let me redo with opposite case: Settlement offer: 30M → Lawyer gets 9M Expected trial outcome: 40M → Lawyer gets 12M Additional work for trial: 80 hours × 3,000 = 240,000 cost

Lawyer's decision: Trial gives: 12M - 0.24M = 11.76M Settlement gives: 9M Lawyer prefers trial (correct! Trial is better for Biotech too)

But now consider if expected trial is 35M: Settlement: 30M → Lawyer gets 9M Trial: Expected 35M → Lawyer gets 10.5M, minus 0.24M cost = 10.26M

Lawyer prefers trial (10.26M > 9M). But for Biotech: Settlement gives 30M, Trial gives 35M expected minus legal costs If legal costs are high, Biotech might prefer settlement even though lawyer prefers trial.

Step 4: Comparison for This Case

Biotech-PharmaChem dispute characteristics:

  • Clear contract breach (quality defect)
  • Likely high damages (drug effectiveness compromised)
  • Probably strong case (quality is verifiable)

Recommendation for compensation:

Primary recommendation: Contingency fee with cap

Structure: 25-30% of recovery, with adjustments:

  • Lower percentage if settlement occurs early
  • Cap on maximum fee to prevent excessive settlement incentives
  • Minimum fee for lawyer's time if case is strong

Rationale:

  1. Aligns lawyer's incentives with maximizing recovery
  2. Reduces risk for Biotech (no payment if they lose)
  3. Given strong case, contingency fee attracts good lawyers
  4. Adjustments (lower percentage for early settlement) encourage efficient resolution

Alternative: Modified hourly billing with bonus

Structure: Hourly rate + performance bonus based on outcome

Rationale:

  1. Lawyer compensated for time
  2. Bonus aligns incentives with result
  3. Might work better if case requires extensive work regardless of settlement timing

For this specific case: Given that the case appears strong (clear quality defect, verifiable breach), contingency fee is attractive:

  • Good lawyers will take the case
  • Incentives reasonably well aligned
  • Biotech doesn't bear risk of losing case

Step 5: Combining Lawyer Selection and Compensation

Best approach:

  1. Hire: Lawyer with reputation in contract/pharmaceutical litigation OR lawyer Biotech has worked with before
  2. Compensate: Contingency fee (25-30%) with adjustments for settlement timing

This combination addresses both adverse selection (reputation) and moral hazard (aligned incentives).

Step 6: Application to Biotech-PharmaChem

Specific recommendations:

  1. Identify candidates:
    • Lawyers who have represented pharmaceutical firms in supply disputes
    • Lawyers Biotech has used for other matters
    • Large firms with pharmaceutical practice groups (reputation constraint)
  2. Interview:
    • Assess experience with similar cases
    • Check references and outcomes
    • Evaluate communication style and responsiveness
  3. Contract terms:
    • 30% contingency if settled before filing
    • 35% contingency if case filed but settles before trial
    • 40% contingency if case goes to trial
    • This structure encourages efficient settlement while ensuring effort if trial is necessary
  4. Monitoring:
    • Regular updates on case progress
    • Client retains decision rights on settlement
    • Lawyer provides candid assessment of case strength

Key Assumptions:

  • Case outcomes provide information about lawyer quality (reputation works)
  • Lawyer values future business
  • Courts enforce reasonable fee arrangements
  • Case strength is as it appears (quality defect is clear)

How conclusions change:

  • If case is weak or uncertain → hourly billing might be better (hard to attract good lawyer on contingency)
  • If Biotech has limited resources → contingency fee more attractive (shares risk)
  • If relationship with lawyer is very strong → can use simpler arrangements with less detailed monitoring
  • If many similar cases expected → in-house counsel becomes more attractive
▶ Question 16.4: American Rule Impact on Contract Performance Incentives

Case Description:

A start-up tech company develops AI-powered apps for its customers. Since the company is low on cash, it prefers to receive half the payment before it starts developing the app. The remaining half of the payment is paid when the company delivers the app to the customer.

Question:

Assume the company wants to expand to the United States. There, each party to a lawsuit pays its own legal fees irrespective of the outcome of the case. How does this change in the legal environment affect the company's incentives to keep its promises? Which factors does your answer depend on? Explain using economic theory.

▶ What a Good Answer Should Contain
  1. Compare English rule (loser pays) vs American rule (each pays own)
  2. Use Nash bargaining to determine settlement under each rule
  3. Show how settlement amount affects company's performance incentives
  4. Identify conditions under which American rule reduces incentives to perform
  5. Use numerical examples to illustrate the comparison
  6. Explain when customer's threat to sue becomes non-credible
▶ Example solution

Framework: Fee-Shifting Rules and Contract Performance Incentives

The question asks how moving to the American rule (each party pays own legal fees) affects the company's incentives to keep promises, compared to a system where the loser pays all legal fees (common in Norway and other European countries).

Step 1: Define the Two Rules

English Rule (typical in Norway):

  • Loser pays all legal costs
  • If customer wins: Company pays damages + both parties' legal costs
  • If company wins: Customer pays both parties' legal costs

American Rule (U.S. federal courts):

  • Each party pays own legal costs regardless of outcome
  • If customer wins: Company pays damages only
  • If company wins: Each pays own costs

Step 2: Impact on Settlement Bargaining

Setup:

  • Customer's value of app: V
  • Probability customer wins if case goes to court: p
  • Expectation damages if customer wins: D = V
  • Total legal costs: C (assume equal for both parties for simplicity)
  • Under English rule: Loser pays C
  • Under American rule: Each pays C/2

Under English Rule:

Customer's expected value from lawsuit:

  • Win (prob p): Get damages D + recover legal costs → Net: D + C - C = D
  • Lose (prob 1-p): Pay both parties' costs → Net: -C
  • Expected value: p × D - (1-p) × C

Company's expected cost from lawsuit:

  • Lose (prob p): Pay damages D + both legal costs → Cost: D + C
  • Win (prob 1-p): Receive legal cost compensation → Cost: 0
  • Expected cost: p(D + C)

Settlement range: [p × D - (1-p) × C, p(D + C)]

Nash bargaining solution: Settlement = [p × D - (1-p) × C + p(D + C)] / 2 = p × D + [p × C - (1-p) × C] / 2 = p × D + (2p - 1) × C / 2

Under American Rule:

Customer's expected value from lawsuit:

  • Win (prob p): Get damages D, pay own costs C/2 → Net: D - C/2
  • Lose (prob 1-p): Pay own costs C/2 → Net: -C/2
  • Expected value: p × D - C/2

Company's expected cost from lawsuit:

  • Lose (prob p): Pay damages D + own costs C/2 → Cost: D + C/2
  • Win (prob 1-p): Pay own costs C/2 → Cost: C/2
  • Expected cost: p × D + C/2

Settlement range: [p × D - C/2, p × D + C/2]

Nash bargaining solution: Settlement = [p × D - C/2 + p × D + C/2] / 2 = p × D

Step 3: Numerical Comparison

Assume:

  • Customer's value (and expectation damages): D = 120
  • Probability customer wins: p = 0.9 (90%)
  • Total legal costs: C = 20

Under English Rule: Settlement = 0.9 × 120 + (2 × 0.9 - 1) × 20 / 2 = 108 + 0.8 × 10 = 108 + 8 = 116

Under American Rule: Settlement = 0.9 × 120 = 108

Company's breach decision:

Development cost: 80 Revenue from performance: 100 (50 upfront + 50 on delivery)

Under English Rule:

  • Perform: 100 - 80 = 20
  • Breach and settle: 50 (upfront) - 116 (settlement) = -66
  • Company performs ✓

Under American Rule:

  • Perform: 100 - 80 = 20
  • Breach and settle: 50 - 108 = -58
  • Company performs ✓

Both systems deter breach in this example, but American rule provides weaker deterrence (settlement is lower).

Step 4: When Does American Rule Cause Breach?

Consider a different scenario:

  • Customer's value: D = 105
  • Development cost: C_dev = 95
  • Legal costs: C = 20
  • Probability of winning: p = 0.9

Under English Rule: Settlement = 0.9 × 105 + (0.8 × 20)/2 = 94.5 + 8 = 102.5

Company's decision:

  • Perform: 100 - 95 = 5
  • Breach: 50 - 102.5 = -52.5
  • Company performs (5 > -52.5) ✓

Under American Rule: Settlement = 0.9 × 105 = 94.5

Company's decision:

  • Perform: 100 - 95 = 5
  • Breach: 50 - 94.5 = -44.5
  • Company performs (5 > -44.5) ✓

Still performs, but with narrower margin.

Now consider even tighter margin:

  • Customer's value: D = 100
  • Development cost: C_dev = 95

Under English Rule: Settlement = 0.9 × 100 + 8 = 98

Company's decision:

  • Perform: 100 - 95 = 5
  • Breach: 50 - 98 = -48
  • Performs ✓

Under American Rule: Settlement = 0.9 × 100 = 90

Company's decision:

  • Perform: 100 - 95 = 5
  • Breach: 50 - 90 = -40
  • Performs ✓

The margin is smaller under American rule (5 vs -40 = 45) compared to English rule (5 vs -48 = 53).

Step 5: When Lawsuit Threat Becomes Non-Credible

The American rule can make lawsuits non-credible when legal costs are high relative to damages.

Example:

  • Customer's value: D = 10
  • Legal costs: C = 20 (C/2 = 10 per party)
  • Probability of winning: p = 0.9

Customer's expected value from lawsuit: 0.9 × 10 - 10 = 9 - 10 = -1 (negative!)

Customer won't sue! The threat is not credible.

Under English rule: Customer's expected value: 0.9 × 10 - 0.1 × 20 = 9 - 2 = 7 (positive)

Customer would sue.

Implication: Under American rule, small-value contracts are harder to enforce because litigation costs make lawsuits non-credible.

Step 6: Summary of Factors

The American rule reduces the company's incentives to perform, especially when:

  1. Legal costs are high:
    • Higher costs reduce settlement amount under American rule
    • Can make lawsuits non-credible (negative expected value)
  2. App value is small:
    • Low damages mean legal costs are large relative to claim
    • Settlement may not deter breach
    • Lawsuit might not be credible
  3. Customer's valuation is close to development cost:
    • Small profit margin from performance
    • Lower settlement under American rule might make breach profitable
  4. Probability of winning is not very high:
    • Lower p reduces settlement amount
    • Reduces deterrence

Numerical Summary:

Scenario English Rule Settlement American Rule Settlement Difference
D=120, C=20, p=0.9 116 108 8
D=105, C=20, p=0.9 102.5 94.5 8
D=10, C=20, p=0.9 7 (credible) -1 (not credible) Threat fails

Step 7: When American Rule Might Not Matter

The difference is small or negligible when:

  1. Reputational mechanisms are strong
    • Company cares about repeat business
    • Legal enforcement is backup, not primary deterrent
  2. Development costs are much lower than value
    • Large profit margin from performance
    • Company performs even with weak legal deterrence
  3. Parties have ongoing relationship
    • Relational contract provides primary incentives
    • Legal system rarely invoked
  4. Alternative mechanisms exist
    • Escrow
    • Milestone payments
    • Performance bonds

Conclusion

Moving to the U.S. (American rule) reduces the company's incentives to keep promises because:

  1. Settlement amounts are lower (by approximately (2p-1) × C/2)
  2. Lawsuits may become non-credible for small claims
  3. Weaker deterrence especially when legal costs are high

The company becomes less trustworthy, particularly for:

  • Small-value contracts
  • Contracts where profit margin is small
  • Cases where customer has low probability of winning

The company should compensate by relying more on:

  • Reputation mechanisms
  • Relational contracts
  • Payment structures (escrow, milestones)
  • Non-legal enforcement

Key Assumptions:

  • Nash bargaining describes settlement accurately
  • Parties are risk-neutral
  • Legal costs are symmetric
  • Probability of winning is common knowledge

How conclusions change:

  • If legal costs are very low → difference between rules is small
  • If company has strong reputation → legal rules matter less
  • If customer is risk-averse → lawsuits even less attractive under American rule
  • If information is asymmetric about case quality → settlement may fail more often
▶ Question 16.5: Insurance Fraud Dispute: Settlement vs. Litigation Strategy

Case Background:

The company TryggBolig AS offers home contents insurance to private individuals. The industry reports increasing insurance fraud, particularly in claimed thefts and fire damage. Some customers have been denied coverage because the company believes the damages are insufficiently documented.

Question:

After several cases of suspected insurance fraud, TryggBolig AS is considering how to handle disputes with customers who are denied compensation. In one case, a customer claims that a fire destroyed their household contents, but the company believes the fire was deliberately set and has rejected payout. The customer threatens to sue. The company can either settle to avoid trial, or refuse payment and risk litigation. Discuss which strategy TryggBolig AS should choose using economic theory.

▶ What a Good Answer Should Contain

▶ Question 16.6: Consumer Class Actions vs. Individual Litigation

Case Background:

NordEnergi AS is a medium-sized electricity supplier selling power to both households and businesses. In 2024, the company experienced large fluctuations in electricity prices due to increased exports, varying reservoir inflows, and unpredictable weather conditions. Several customers and industrial companies believe prices have been unreasonably high, and there has been debate about whether today's market model for electricity functions as intended.

Question:

The Consumer Council (Forbrukerrådet) is considering filing a lawsuit against NordEnergi for misleading marketing of electricity contracts. Discuss using economic theory why the Consumer Council should be able to sue on behalf of consumers, instead of each individual customer having to file suit separately.

▶ What a Good Answer Should Contain

[TO BE COMPLETED]

▶ Example solution

[SOLUTION TO BE COMPLETED]

The solution should discuss:

  • Transaction costs of individual litigation
  • Collective action problem: Individual harm may be small, litigation costs high
  • Free-rider problem: Each consumer hopes others will sue
  • Economies of scale in litigation: Fixed costs can be spread across many plaintiffs
  • Deterrence effects: Without class actions, companies face insufficient deterrence for small harms to many consumers
  • Numerical example: 10,000 consumers each harmed 1,000 kr, litigation costs 50,000 kr per case
    • Individual litigation: Not rational (1,000 < 50,000)
    • Class action: Rational (10,000,000 > 50,000)
  • Agency problems: Does Consumer Council represent consumers' interests?
  • Alternative mechanisms: Regulatory enforcement vs. private litigation
  • Comparison to other legal systems (U.S. class actions)

Key framework: Transaction costs and collective action problems prevent socially beneficial litigation

▶ Question 16.7: Patent Troll Litigation: Credible Threats, Settlement, and Comparative Institutions

Case Background:

A Norwegian technology company, TechNor AS, has been very successful in its home market and has recently decided to expand its operations to the United States. The company has launched a new product line in the U.S. market, but shortly after the launch, it receives a notice of a lawsuit from an American company, IP Holdings, which is known for being a patent troll. IP Holdings claims that TechNor has infringed on its patent for a technology that is only marginally related to TechNor's products. The notice includes a demand that TechNor must pay a large licensing fee to continue selling its products in the U.S., or else IP Holdings will proceed with a lawsuit. TechNor considers it likely that they would win such a lawsuit but is concerned about the legal costs it would incur (in the U.S., each party pays its own legal costs regardless of who wins the case).

Questions:

  1. TechNor believes that the threat of a lawsuit is not credible and is considering ignoring the notice. Under what conditions would they be right? Discuss using economic theory.
  2. Alternatively, TechNor is considering settling the case, with IP Holdings agreeing to drop the lawsuit in exchange for a one-time payment. What factors determine the size of the payment TechNor is likely to negotiate? Discuss using economic theory.
  3. Patent trolls are less common in Norway than in the U.S. What might explain this? Explain using economic theory. Hint: Two factors you can include in the discussion are that (i) in the U.S., each party pays its own legal costs, while in Norway the general rule is that the losing party pays, and (ii) patent requirements are stricter in Norway.
▶ What a Good Answer Should Contain

A good answer should use game theory for credible threats, Nash bargaining theory for settlement analysis, and comparative institutional analysis for the US-Norway comparison. Your analysis should explain when litigation threats are credible (expected value positive for plaintiff), apply the Nash bargaining solution to find the settlement range and midpoint, incorporate reputation effects showing how patent trolls maintain credibility through repeated interactions, use numerical examples comparing American rule versus English rule fee-shifting, and explain how stricter patent standards and loser-pays rules reduce patent troll profitability. The key economic insight is that seemingly small institutional differences (fee allocation rules, patent standards) can dramatically affect litigation incentives and market outcomes.

▶ Example solution

Baseline and Change:

Baseline: TechNor receives patent infringement lawsuit threat from IP Holdings in US market.

Key institutional feature: American rule - each party pays own litigation costs regardless of outcome.

Strategic situation:

  1. IP Holdings threatens lawsuit
  2. TechNor must decide: Ignore (call bluff) or settle
  3. If TechNor ignores: IP Holdings decides whether to actually sue

Three questions:

  • Q1: When is threat credible? (When will IP Holdings actually sue if ignored?)
  • Q2: If settling, what amount? (Nash bargaining)
  • Q3: Why more common in US than Norway? (Comparative institutions)

Actors and Incentives:

Key actors:

  1. IP Holdings (patent troll): Owns vague patents, threatens litigation for licensing fees
  2. TechNor: Legitimate technology company, likely has valid defense
  3. Future potential victims: Other companies IP Holdings might target

Relevant margins:

  • IP Holdings: Sue (costly) vs. don't sue (lose reputation for credibility)
  • TechNor: Settle (certain cost) vs. litigate (uncertain outcome, litigation costs)
  • Future victims: Whether to take threats seriously based on IP Holdings' reputation

Economic Mechanism:

Question 1: When is Threat Credible?

Framework: Backward induction - threat is credible only if profitable to execute.

IP Holdings' litigation decision (if TechNor ignores):

Expected value of suing: = (Probability of winning) × (Value if win) - (Litigation costs) = P × V - C_IP

Where:

  • P = Probability IP Holdings wins in court
  • V = Licensing fee/damages if win
  • C_IP = IP Holdings' litigation costs

Threat is credible if: P × V - C_IP > 0

Equivalently: P × V > C_IP

Numerical example:

Assume:

  • Licensing fee if IP Holdings wins: V = $500,000
  • IP Holdings' litigation costs: C_IP = $100,000
  • Probability IP Holdings wins: P = 30% (weak case)

Expected value: = 0.30 × $500,000 - $100,000 = $150,000 - $100,000 = $50,000 > 0

Threat is credible - IP Holdings would actually sue if ignored.

When threat is NOT credible:

If P = 15%: = 0.15 × $500,000 - $100,000 = $75,000 - $100,000 = -$25,000 < 0

Threat not credible - IP Holdings loses money by suing.

Key determinants of credibility:

  1. Higher win probability (P) → More credible
    • Stronger patent
    • More clearly infringing product
    • Better legal arguments
  2. Higher damages if win (V) → More credible
    • Larger licensing fee demanded
    • More product sales affected
    • Statutory damages provisions
  3. Lower litigation costs (C_IP) → More credible
    • Experienced litigators (economies of scale)
    • Standard legal templates
    • Pre-existing evidence

Reputation Effects (Repeated Game)

The above analysis assumes one-shot game. But patent trolls operate repeatedly.

With reputation concerns:

If TechNor ignores threat and IP Holdings doesn't sue:

  • Future victims learn IP Holdings makes empty threats
  • Future victims ignore threats
  • IP Holdings' business model collapses

Modified decision:

IP Holdings sues if: P × V - C_IP + Reputation benefit from suing > 0

Or equivalently: P × V - C_IP + K > 0

Where K = Present value of maintaining tough reputation

Numerical example with reputation:

Same parameters: P = 15%, V = $500,000, C_IP = $100,000 Without reputation: -$25,000 (don't sue)

With reputation: Assume K = $50,000 (PV of credibility for future targets) = -$25,000 + $50,000 = $25,000 > 0

Now credible - IP Holdings sues to maintain reputation, even at short-term loss.

Critical factor: Publicity

Reputation effects only matter if threat and response are observable.

  • Public threat: Media coverage, industry knowledge → High reputation stakes
  • Private threat: Secret settlement → Low reputation stakes

TechNor is right to ignore if:

  1. P × V < C_IP (negative expected value for IP Holdings)
  2. AND threat is private (no reputation cost to backing down)
  3. AND IP Holdings has reputation for empty threats

Question 2: Settlement Amount Determination

Framework: Nash bargaining theory

Settlement range:

IP Holdings' minimum acceptable settlement: = Expected value of litigation = P × V - C_IP

If settlement < this amount, better to litigate.

TechNor's maximum willingness to pay: = Expected cost of litigation = P × V + C_TN

Where C_TN = TechNor's litigation costs

If settlement > this amount, better to litigate.

Nash bargaining solution:

Settlement = Midpoint between minimum and maximum = [IP Holdings minimum + TechNor maximum] / 2 = [(P × V - C_IP) + (P × V + C_TN)] / 2 = P × V + (C_TN - C_IP) / 2

Numerical example:

Assume:

  • P = 30% (probability IP Holdings wins)
  • V = $500,000 (damages if lose)
  • C_IP = $100,000 (IP Holdings' litigation costs)
  • C_TN = $200,000 (TechNor's litigation costs)

IP Holdings' minimum: = 0.30 × $500,000 - $100,000 = $150,000 - $100,000 = $50,000

TechNor's maximum: = 0.30 × $500,000 + $200,000 = $150,000 + $200,000 = $350,000

Settlement range: [$50,000, $350,000]

Nash solution: = $150,000 + ($200,000 - $100,000)/2 = $150,000 + $50,000 = $200,000

Interpretation: TechNor pays $200,000 to settle.

Key factors affecting settlement amount:

  1. Higher win probability (P) → Higher settlement
    • Both parties' expected litigation values increase
    • More risk for TechNor
  2. Higher damages (V) → Higher settlement
    • Linear relationship: settlement increases with stakes
  3. Higher TechNor litigation costs (C_TN) → Higher settlement
    • TechNor more willing to pay to avoid costly trial
    • Each additional dollar of TechNor costs adds $0.50 to settlement
  4. Higher IP Holdings litigation costs (C_IP) → Lower settlement
    • IP Holdings more desperate to settle and avoid costs
    • Each additional dollar of IP Holdings costs subtracts $0.50 from settlement

Reputation effects on settlement:

If IP Holdings values reputation for toughness:

  • Won't accept very low settlements (damages reputation)
  • Minimum acceptable settlement increases by reputation cost
  • New minimum: P × V - C_IP + K

Modified Nash solution: = P × V + (C_TN - C_IP + K)/2

Higher settlement amount.

If TechNor values reputation for not being "easy target":

  • Won't pay very high settlements (attracts more trolls)
  • Maximum acceptable settlement decreases
  • Strengthens bargaining position

Asymmetric information:

TechNor doesn't know:

  • IP Holdings' true litigation costs
  • How much IP Holdings values reputation
  • IP Holdings' true assessment of win probability

This creates bargaining friction - negotiations may fail even when settlement range exists.

Question 3: Why Patent Trolls Less Common in Norway

Comparative institutional analysis: US vs. Norway

Key differences:

Feature United States Norway
Fee allocation American rule (each pays own) English rule (loser pays)
Patent standards Broader patents granted Stricter requirements

Mechanism 1: Fee-shifting rules

Under American rule (US):

IP Holdings' expected value: = P × V - C_IP

TechNor's expected cost: = P × V + C_TN

Under English rule (Norway):

IP Holdings' expected value: = P × (V + C_TN) - (1-P) × (C_IP + C_TN) = P × V + P × C_TN - C_IP - C_TN + P × C_IP + P × C_TN = P × V - C_IP(1-P) - C_TN(1-P)

TechNor's expected cost: = P × V + P × C_IP

Numerical comparison:

Same assumptions:

  • P = 20% (weak patent case)
  • V = $500,000
  • C_IP = $100,000 (each side's litigation costs)
  • C_TN = $100,000

United States (American rule):

IP Holdings' value: = 0.20 × $500,000 - $100,000 = $100,000 - $100,000 = $0

TechNor's cost: = 0.20 × $500,000 + $100,000 = $100,000 + $100,000 = $200,000

Nash settlement = ($0 + $200,000)/2 = $100,000

Norway (English rule):

IP Holdings' value: = 0.20 × $500,000 - 0.80 × ($100,000 + $100,000) = $100,000 - $160,000 = -$60,000

TechNor's cost: = 0.20 × ($500,000 + $100,000) = 0.20 × $600,000 = $120,000

Threat not credible in Norway! (-$60,000 < 0)

But if somehow still settling: Nash = (-$60,000 + $120,000)/2 = $30,000

Comparison:

  • US settlement: $100,000
  • Norway settlement: $30,000 (if credible at all)

English rule dramatically reduces patent troll profitability.

Mechanism 2: Stricter patent standards

If Norway grants fewer vague/weak patents:

  • P (probability troll wins) decreases
  • Further reduces expected value for patent troll

Example: If P drops from 20% to 10% in Norway:

Norwegian IP Holdings value: = 0.10 × $500,000 - 0.90 × $200,000 = $50,000 - $180,000 = -$130,000

Even more unprofitable!

Combined effect:

English rule + Stricter patents = Very low patent troll profitability

  • Most threats not credible
  • Settlement amounts tiny when credible
  • Business model doesn't work

Why American rule facilitates patent trolls:

  1. Asymmetric litigation costs:
    • TechNor must pay own costs even if wins
    • Creates positive settlement value even for frivolous claims
    • "Nuisance value" settlements
  2. No downside risk for weak claims:
    • Patent troll risks only own litigation costs
    • Under English rule, risks paying defendant's costs too
    • Much more deterrent
  3. Scales with number of targets:
    • Patent troll can threaten 100 companies
    • Each pays $100,000 nuisance settlement
    • Total: $10 million
    • Under English rule: Loses money on each case

Illustration:

Comprehensive Numerical Example:

Setup:

  • Patent troll threatens 50 companies
  • Each has sales of $1 million from allegedly infringing product
  • Damages if lose: 10% of sales = $100,000
  • Win probability for troll: 20% (very weak case)
  • Litigation costs: $50,000 per side

United States (American rule):

Per-company analysis:

  • Troll's value: 0.20 × $100,000 - $50,000 = -$30,000
  • Company's cost: 0.20 × $100,000 + $50,000 = $70,000
  • Nash settlement: (-$30,000 + $70,000)/2 = $20,000

Troll's total revenue: = 50 companies × $20,000 = $1,000,000

Profitable business model!

Even though troll would lose each individual case if litigated (-$30,000), companies pay to avoid litigation costs.

Norway (English rule):

Per-company analysis:

  • Troll's value: 0.20 × $100,000 - 0.80 × $100,000 = -$60,000
  • Company's cost: 0.20 × $150,000 = $30,000
  • Nash settlement: (-$60,000 + $30,000)/2 = -$15,000

Negative settlement! Troll should pay company to drop case.

Threats not credible. No settlements. No patent troll business.

Assumptions and Robustness:

Key Assumption 1: Parties are risk-neutral

If companies are risk-averse:

  • Willing to pay more than expected value to avoid uncertainty
  • Higher settlement amounts
  • Helps patent trolls even more

If patent trolls are risk-averse:

  • Accept lower settlements to avoid litigation risk
  • Helps defendants

Key Assumption 2: No asymmetric information in settlement

With asymmetric information:

  • Parties have different beliefs about P
  • Settlement bargaining may fail
  • More actual litigation
  • Reputation effects stronger (more observable outcomes)

Key Assumption 3: Reputation effects work as described

If reputation doesn't matter:

  • Patent trolls with negative expected value never sue
  • More threats are non-credible
  • Lower settlement amounts

If reputation very important:

  • Patent trolls sue even at large losses
  • Threats always credible
  • Higher settlement amounts

Key Assumption 4: Courts accurately assess patent validity

If courts make errors:

  • P may be higher than "true" probability
  • Benefits weak patent trolls
  • Even stricter standards needed

Key Assumption 5: No collateral consequences

If litigation has other effects:

  • Publicity damages company reputation
  • Customers worry about legal uncertainty
  • Stock price impacts
  • Companies more willing to settle

Key Assumption 6: Transaction costs of settlement are low

If settlement negotiations are costly:

  • Bargaining may fail more often
  • More actual litigation
  • Fee-shifting rules matter even more

Conditional Conclusion:

Question 1: Threat is credible when:

  1. Expected litigation value positive for patent troll
    • P × V > C_IP
    • Strong enough patent, high enough damages, low enough costs
  2. Reputation effects favor suing
    • Threat is public
    • Patent troll operates repeatedly
    • PV of reputation > Short-term loss from weak case
  3. Institutional environment favorable
    • American rule (no downside risk)
    • Weak patent standards (higher P)

TechNor should ignore threat when:

  • Weak case (low P) + High troll costs (high C_IP) → Negative expected value
  • Private threat → No reputation stakes
  • Troll known for empty threats

Question 2: Settlement amount determined by:

Nash bargaining midpoint: = P × V + (C_TN - C_IP)/2

Increases with:

  • Higher win probability for troll (P)
  • Higher damages at stake (V)
  • Higher defendant litigation costs (C_TN)
  • Troll's reputation concerns

Decreases with:

  • Higher troll litigation costs (C_IP)
  • Defendant's reputation for toughness

Typical range: $20,000 - $200,000 per target in US under American rule with weak cases.

Question 3: Patent trolls less common in Norway because:

English rule (loser pays):

  • Patent troll risks paying defendant's costs if loses
  • With P = 20%, expected cost = 0.80 × 2 × C = 1.6C
  • Makes most weak cases unprofitable
  • Eliminates nuisance value settlements

Stricter patent standards:

  • Lower P (probability of winning)
  • Further reduces expected value
  • Fewer vague patents granted in first place

Combined effect:

  • US with American rule: Positive settlement value even for P = 10% cases
  • Norway with English rule: Negative expected value unless P > 70%+
  • Business model doesn't work

Policy implications:

  1. American rule facilitates patent trolls:
    • Defendants pay costs even when winning
    • Creates "nuisance value" for frivolous claims
    • Enables business model of threatening many companies
  2. English rule deters patent trolls:
    • Loser pays creates downside risk
    • Only strong cases are profitable
    • Natural filter for weak claims
  3. Patent standards matter:
    • Strict examination → Fewer trolls
    • Weak examination → More trolls
    • Quality vs. quantity trade-off

The economic logic: Patent trolls exploit asymmetric litigation costs under American rule. Even when trolls expect to lose, defendants face positive litigation costs, creating settlement value. English rule eliminates this asymmetry by placing litigation costs on losing party, making weak cases unprofitable and deterring patent trolling. The institutional choice between fee-shifting rules has dramatic consequences for litigation incentives and the viability of patent troll business models.

▶ Question 16.8: BankID Fraud Disputes: Simplified Resolution System vs. Courts

Case Background:

The number of cases involving BankID fraud has increased sharply in recent years. Scammers often pose as representatives of a bank or the police and manage to persuade victims to disclose passwords or approve logins to their bank accounts. The money is transferred from the victims' accounts and then moved onward in ways that make it difficult to trace or recover.

The bank may refuse to cover the loss if it believes that a victim of BankID fraud has acted negligently.

Question:

The customer may bring cases before the Financial Complaints Board (Finansklagenemnda). This scheme is free of charge, written, and far simpler and faster than court proceedings, and therefore involves lower costs for the parties. Explain, using economic theory, how such a simplified system may affect the outcome of disputes between banks and customers, compared to a situation in which the customer would have to sue the bank in court.

▶ What a Good Answer Should Contain

A good answer should use the economic theory of settlement bargaining and credible threats. Your analysis should explain when litigation threats are credible (expected value positive for plaintiff), apply Nash bargaining theory to determine settlement amounts under different cost structures, use numerical examples comparing court costs versus complaints board costs to show how lower costs make threats credible for smaller disputes, discuss how bargaining positions change when the higher-probability winner faces different cost structures, incorporate risk aversion showing how customers prefer lower-cost procedures even beyond expected value calculations, and address reputation effects where banks may strategically litigate to deter future complaints. The key insight is that lowering litigation costs dramatically expands access to dispute resolution for small claims while the effect on settlement amounts is theoretically ambiguous depending on win probabilities and party differences in patience and risk preferences.

▶ Example solution

Baseline and Change:

Baseline: Customer must sue bank in court to dispute fraud liability denial.

Alternative: Customer can bring case to Financial Complaints Board (simplified, low-cost alternative dispute resolution).

Key differences:

Feature Court Complaints Board
Cost per party 20,000 NOK 5,000 NOK
Speed Slow (months-years) Fast (weeks)
Formality High Low
Procedure Oral, adversarial Written, simplified

Questions:

  1. How does lower cost affect whether disputes are brought?
  2. How does lower cost affect settlement amounts?

Actors and Incentives:

Key actors:

  1. Customer (fraud victim): Deciding whether to file complaint and settlement demands
  2. Bank: Deciding whether to settle or litigate

Relevant margins:

  • Customer: File complaint vs. accept loss
  • Bank: Settle vs. defend
  • Both: Settlement amount if negotiating

Strategic interaction: Sequential game with settlement bargaining

Economic Mechanism:

Framework 1: Credible Threats

Litigation threat is credible only if expected value is positive for plaintiff.

Court system:

Customer's expected value of litigation: = (Probability win) × (Amount recovered) - (Customer litigation costs) = P × A - C_customer

Where:

  • P = Probability customer wins
  • A = Amount customer lost to fraud
  • C_customer = Customer's court costs

Threat credible if: P × A - C_customer > 0

Or: P × A > C_customer

Numerical example - Court:

  • Amount lost: A = 25,000 NOK
  • Probability customer wins: P = 60%
  • Court costs (customer): C_customer = 20,000 NOK

Expected value: = 0.60 × 25,000 - 20,000 = 15,000 - 20,000 = -5,000 NOK

Threat NOT credible - customer loses money by suing.

Bank can safely refuse payment. Customer has 60% chance of winning but still unprofitable to sue.

Complaints Board:

Same case with lower costs:

  • Board costs (customer): C_customer = 5,000 NOK

Expected value: = 0.60 × 25,000 - 5,000 = 15,000 - 5,000 = 10,000 NOK

Threat NOW credible - customer expects to gain 10,000.

Bank must settle or defend, cannot simply ignore.

Critical insight: Lower litigation costs make threats credible for smaller disputes and lower win probabilities.

Threshold analysis:

Court system: Threat credible when: P × A > 20,000 P > 20,000/A

For A = 25,000: Need P > 80% for credible threat

Complaints Board: Threat credible when: P × A > 5,000 P > 5,000/A

For A = 25,000: Need P > 20% for credible threat

Effect: Complaints board makes threats credible for much wider range of cases.

Framework 2: Nash Bargaining and Settlement Amounts

When litigation threat is credible, parties settle within a range.

Settlement range:

Customer's minimum acceptable settlement: = Expected value of litigation = P × A - C_customer

If settlement < this, better to litigate.

Bank's maximum willingness to pay: = Expected cost of litigation = P × A + C_bank

If settlement > this, better to litigate.

Nash bargaining solution: Settlement = Midpoint of range = [Customer minimum + Bank maximum] / 2 = [(P × A - C_customer) + (P × A + C_bank)] / 2 = P × A + (C_bank - C_customer) / 2

Numerical Example 1: High win probability (P = 80%)

Amount: A = 25,000 Win probability: P = 80% Court costs: C_customer = C_bank = 20,000 Board costs: C_customer = C_bank = 5,000

Court proceedings:

Customer minimum: = 0.80 × 25,000 - 20,000 = 20,000 - 20,000 = 0 NOK

Bank maximum: = 0.80 × 25,000 + 20,000 = 20,000 + 20,000 = 40,000 NOK

Nash settlement = (0 + 40,000)/2 = 20,000 NOK

Complaints Board:

Customer minimum: = 0.80 × 25,000 - 5,000 = 20,000 - 5,000 = 15,000 NOK

Bank maximum: = 0.80 × 25,000 + 5,000 = 20,000 + 5,000 = 25,000 NOK

Nash settlement = (15,000 + 25,000)/2 = 20,000 NOK

Same settlement amount! (When costs equal for both sides)

Actually, this is exactly P × A = 20,000 in both cases.

Numerical Example 2: Moderate win probability (P = 60%)

Court proceedings:

Customer minimum: = 0.60 × 25,000 - 20,000 = 15,000 - 20,000 = -5,000 NOK

Threat not credible! Customer would lose money. No settlement.

Complaints Board:

Customer minimum: = 0.60 × 25,000 - 5,000 = 15,000 - 5,000 = 10,000 NOK

Bank maximum: = 0.60 × 25,000 + 5,000 = 15,000 + 5,000 = 20,000 NOK

Nash settlement = (10,000 + 20,000)/2 = 15,000 NOK

Result: Complaints board enables settlement where court would not (threat credible).

Effect on settlement amounts - Ambiguous:

When P > 50% and equal costs for both parties:

  • Settlement = P × A (same regardless of cost level)
  • Lower costs don't change settlement amount
  • But they do make settlement possible for more cases

When costs differ between parties or other factors matter (see below), lower costs can increase or decrease settlements.

Asymmetric costs example:

If bank has lower litigation costs than customer (economies of scale):

Court: C_customer = 20,000, C_bank = 10,000 Settlement = P × A + (10,000 - 20,000)/2 = P × A - 5,000

Board: C_customer = 5,000, C_bank = 2,500 Settlement = P × A + (2,500 - 5,000)/2 = P × A - 1,250

Lower costs benefit customer (higher settlement) when customer's cost advantage diminishes.

Illustration:

Case Study: Three Dispute Scenarios

Scenario A: Small claim, moderate probability

  • Loss: 10,000 NOK
  • Win probability: 50%
  • Court costs: 20,000 each
  • Board costs: 5,000 each

Court:

  • Customer EV: 0.50 × 10,000 - 20,000 = -15,000
  • No filing (not credible)
  • Bank pays: 0

Board:

  • Customer EV: 0.50 × 10,000 - 5,000 = 0
  • Marginally credible (indifferent)
  • Settlement: 5,000

Effect: Board enables resolution of small claims court excludes.

Scenario B: Medium claim, high probability

  • Loss: 25,000 NOK
  • Win probability: 80%
  • Court costs: 20,000 each
  • Board costs: 5,000 each

Court:

  • Customer minimum: 0
  • Bank maximum: 40,000
  • Settlement: 20,000

Board:

  • Customer minimum: 15,000
  • Bank maximum: 25,000
  • Settlement: 20,000

Effect: Same settlement, but board is faster and cheaper to reach.

Scenario C: Large claim, low probability

  • Loss: 100,000 NOK
  • Win probability: 30%
  • Court costs: 20,000 each
  • Board costs: 5,000 each

Court:

  • Customer EV: 0.30 × 100,000 - 20,000 = 10,000
  • Credible
  • Settlement: 30,000

Board:

  • Customer EV: 0.30 × 100,000 - 5,000 = 25,000
  • Credible
  • Settlement: 30,000

Effect: Both credible, same settlement. Board saves time and money.

Summary pattern:

  • Small claims: Board enables filing, court doesn't
  • Medium/large claims: Both enable filing, similar settlements, board cheaper
  • Access to justice significantly expanded

Assumptions and Robustness:

Key Assumption 1: Parties are risk-neutral

If customer is risk-averse (realistic):

  • Dislikes uncertainty of litigation
  • Values certainty of settlement more than expected value
  • Willing to accept lower settlement to avoid risk

Effect of risk aversion:

Risk-neutral customer (EV = 10,000): Accepts settlement ≥ 10,000 Risk-averse customer: Accepts settlement < 10,000 (e.g., certain 8,000 > risky EV of 10,000)

Court with high stakes amplifies risk:

  • 60% chance of winning 25,000, 40% chance of losing 20,000
  • High variance outcome
  • Risk-averse customer much prefers settlement

Board with lower costs reduces risk exposure:

  • Same win probability but lower downside (lose only 5,000 not 20,000)
  • Less risk aversion effect
  • Customer's bargaining position stronger

Implication: Lower costs help risk-averse customers by reducing downside risk, strengthening their position.

Key Assumption 2: Equal costs for both parties

If bank has lower costs (economies of scale, in-house counsel):

Court: C_customer = 20,000, C_bank = 10,000 Board: C_customer = 5,000, C_bank = 2,500

Nash settlements: Court: P × A + (10,000 - 20,000)/2 = P × A - 5,000 Board: P × A + (2,500 - 5,000)/2 = P × A - 1,250

Lower costs benefit customer: Settlement increases by 3,750.

Key Assumption 3: No reputation effects

If bank values tough reputation to deter future claims:

Bank's calculus includes reputation value:

  • Settling too low signals weakness
  • Encourages more claims
  • Bank willing to litigate even at loss to maintain deterrent

Effect on settlements:

Bank minimum acceptable settlement increases: Settlement_bank = P × A - C_bank + K

Where K = reputational value of fighting (present value of deterring future claims)

Court proceedings: K might be small (few can afford to sue) Board proceedings: K might be large (many can afford to complain)

Paradox: Board's lower costs might increase settlement amounts if bank values reputation for toughness.

Numerical example:

Without reputation: Nash settlement = 15,000 With reputation (K = 10,000): Bank minimum: 0.60 × 25,000 + 5,000 + 10,000 = 30,000 But customer maximum: 20,000 No settlement zone! Bank prefers to litigate to deter future claims.

Alternative: Bank settles at customer's maximum (20,000) to avoid even higher litigation costs, but this is higher than without reputation (15,000).

Key Assumption 4: Patience (time value of money)

If customer needs money quickly (liquidity constrained):

  • Discount factor for delayed payment is high
  • Slow court proceedings very costly
  • Willing to accept much lower settlement for quick resolution

Court: 1-2 year delay for 20,000 settlement Board: 2-month delay for same amount

Customer with 20% annual discount rate: Court: 20,000 / (1.20)^1.5 ≈ 15,800 present value Board: 20,000 / (1.20)^(2/12) ≈ 19,400 present value

Effect: Faster board resolution worth 3,600 more to impatient customer.

Strengthens customer's bargaining position in board proceedings.

Key Assumption 5: Board decisions are binding

If banks can ignore board decisions:

  • Customer must sue in court anyway
  • Board adds delay without replacing court
  • May be worse than direct court filing

In practice: Some boards are advisory only. If so, analysis must account for two-stage process (board then court).

Conditional Conclusion:

Simplified dispute resolution (Complaints Board) affects outcomes through:

1. Expanded access to justice

  • Makes litigation credible for smaller disputes (A = 10,000-25,000)
  • Lowers probability threshold for credibility (P > 20% vs. P > 80%)
